Abstract I English
The aim of this thesis was to explore whether high-tech solutions can conôÙ
tribute to ensuring adequate sanitation in planned refugee camps. In refuôÙ
gee camps, where people live close together, often under poor conditions, 
inadequate sanitation poses a threat to the lives and health of the populaôÙ
tion. As humanitarian organisations are pressured to operate more effecôÙ
tively and efficiently, they are on the outlook for innovative solutions. This 
thesis gives suggestions for a solution using new technology, namely Big 
Data, blockchain and the Internet of Things. The project has been conductôÙ
ed in collaboration with several humanitarian organisations, and has had the 
parallel objective of bringing these stakeholders and technical companies 
closer. Inadequate sanitation, and specifically latrines, was chosen as the 
point of departure for this exploration, as we believe it is a rather overôÙ
looked problem compared to the rest of the refugee crisis. 
The study started with an in-depth analysis regarding the problems related 
to latrine use. The empirical data was collected through reviewing availôÙ
able material on camp management practices and conducting interviews 
with experts from the field. This was transformed into models using ProdôÙ
uct/Service-system theory and System Dynamics modelling. The analysis 
showed that the challenges with inadequate sanitation could be viewed at 
three levels; the camp management level, the water, sanitation and hygiene 
(WASH) management level, and the refugee level. If the central problem was 
to be improved, a solution would have to address the challenges on all three 
levels at the same time. A subsequent analysis was conducted with regards 
to the possibilities for addressing said challenges. This was done through 
case studies relevant to the subject, and through exploring new technoloôÙ
gies with potential to enable new solutions.
This lead to the conclusion that the solution should consist of three differôÙ
ent systems; a motivational system, a data collection and analysis system, 
and a collaboration system. The consolidation of these systems makes the 
refugees a source of data in a seamless way by registering every time they 
use a latrine, and motivates them to participate by giving a benefit. The data 
generated from this system, enables humanitarian organisations to perform 
useful analysis and perform consequent interventions based on the results. 
It both ensures improvements in the short term, in the respective refugee 
camp, and strengthens humanitarian operations in the long run. 
The thesis concludes with a final concept and recommendations for how 
humanitarian organisations can benefit from new technology. The concept 
is kept at a generic level, to ensure flexibility. It is designed so it can be used 
for further development in real-world refugee camps. Our recommendation 
is that humanitarian organisations take advantage of the new technological 
possibilities and that they ease this process by creating partnerships with 
academia, private companies and each other.

Preface
This master thesis is the result of the combined effort of Charlotte Amalie 
Lindegaard Petersen and Caroline Maria Tromer Dragsdahl and constitutes 
the final delivery for the Master of Science in Design and Innovation at The 
Technical University of Denmark. The project was carried out from January 
2017 to July 2017 and is rated at 35 ECTS credits.

Word Definitions
Cash-based aid Distributing cash as aid, instead of in-kind aid. The cash-based aid 
can be both actual cash, vouchers, debitcards, simcards or a type of closed loop 
money (see In-kind aid and Closed loop money)
Closed loop money A made up type of money, like monopoly money. Often usable 
within limited stores or areas (see Cash-based aid)
Cluster A group of humanitarian organisations in each of the main sectors of humanôÙ
itarian action, e.g. health, nutrition and shelter. 
Displaced person A person who has been forced away from their home due to e.g. 
conflict or natural disaster. If the person is displaced within their own home country, 
they are internally displaced. 
Development Humanitarian response that developes the community, and lasts more 
than a year, often several years (see Relief)
In-kind aid Distributing goods such as food, clothes and blankets as aid (see Cash-
based aid)
Refugee A displaced person who has crossed national borders and who can not reôÙ
turn home safely (see Displaced person)
Relief Humanitarian response to immediate humanitarian crisis, such as earthquakes 
or tsunamis. Most often lasts less than a year (see Development)
Sanitation The safe disposal of human waste

This report is structured into four sections, each describing a distinguished phase in the deôÙ
sign process. Each section has its own introduction and all chapters start with a short sumôÙ
mary page. These are incorporated to ease the reading process and give the reader overview 
along the way.

how these technologies can give value to your organisation. It will take you through the workôÙ
shops conducted with the Danish Red Cross, Danish Church Aid and Danish Refugee Council 
and give insight into their thoughts. Particularly the four-pager in Chapter 14 can be of interôÙ
est, as it is designed to be used seperately as an inspirational leaflet for NGOs.

The past decade has brought increasing 
pressure on humanitarian organisations to 
professionalise their operations. The many 
stakeholders involved in refugee relief are 
trying out new ways of organisation and new 
ways of giving aid to make sure resources 
are spent efficiently and sustainably. One 
new trend is cash-based aid, which is disôÙ
tribution of money instead of e.g. items, so 
refugees can buy what they decide they need 
the most. High-tech solutions are used to an 
unexpected degree in this context, which 
gives new possibilities for using technology 
and data to create better solutions for both 
refugees and organisations. 
This thesis takes advantage of the new trends 
and explores whether sanitation issues, can 
be relieved using a new approach, which is 
enabled by the new technology. In the end 
the conclusions go beyond sanitation, into 
recommendations for humanitarian organiôÙ
sations on how they can benefit from techôÙ
nology in their future operation. 
The aim of this master thesis is to explore 
whether new technology can improve the 
quality of life for people living in refugee 
camps. Humanitarian crises are accelerating; 
Worldwide more than 60 million people have 
fled their home and are right now displaced. 
The need for accommodation and organisaôÙ
tion of displaced people is also accelerating 
the urgent need for well-functioning refugee 
camps. 
When looking at a refugee camp or humanôÙ
itarian crises in general, one can point out 
many aspects that are not functioning in 
an optimal way; inadequate access to food, 
drinking water, shelter, clothes, and healthôÙ
care is threatening peoples lives. In this theôÙ
sis, we address a rather overlooked challenge; 
sanitation. Hygiene issues, and specifically 
issues connected to excretion, are among 
the greatest health threats when many peoôÙ
ple are gathered. When trying to ensure adôÙ
equate sanitation, poor overview of the situôÙ
ation, time, and economic constraints make 
hygiene management a difficult task.

cally related to the artefact in mind, being a 
latrine or another object of interest. 
These three layers of the theoretical focus 
are expected to give different viewpoints of 
the same problem. By combining the theoôÙ
ries, we hope to find new relevant insights 
related to the problem statement. We also 
hope to discover new connections between 
the theories, which to our knowledge have 
never been used in this kind of combination

1.2.	Scope of Problem: Setting 
system boundaries
When saying ãrefugee campã, one might 
think of a large variety of different types 
of camps in different settings around the 
world, from the tent camps near the urban 
settings of Greece to the large shelter settleôÙ
ments in the rural areas of Kenya. It would be 
impossible to address the issue of sanitation 
in all these different types of camps during 
the course of this thesis. In order to narrow 
down the scope, an ãarchetypeã of refugee 
camp will be defined as the unit with which 
we are operating. 
Humanitarian organisations typically opôÙ
erate using official guidelines and toolkits, 
such as The Camp Management Toolkit 
(IOM et al., 2015) and The Sphere Handbook 
(The Sphere Project, 2011). These toolkits set 
standards for all types of functions in a camp 
from amount of soap and water per resident, 
to how infrastructure and shelter is organôÙ
ised. 
The archetype is likewise defined from an 
understanding of and choice between the 
standards found in the Camp Management 
Toolkit and The Sphere Handbook, as well as 
discussion with actors in the humanitarian 
sector. 
The archetype camp of this thesis has the 
following characteristics:
1.1.	Theoretical Motivation
The theoretical foundation for this thesis is 
built on three areas; Product/Service-SysôÙ
tems, System Dynamics and Theory of TechôÙ
nical Systems (Figure 1). The theories and 
the motivation for using them is briefly inôÙ
troduced here. 
In order to address the sanitation challenge 
with a solution that is durable and socially 
responsible, we apply Product/Service-SysôÙ
tem (PSS) principles throughout the whole 
thesis and use them as the fundamental layer 
of the theoretical focus. The motivation for 
using this approach is the broad applicabilôÙ
ity of PSS principles to all kinds of different 
contexts. The use of PSS for humanitarian 
innovation is new, but has strong potential to 
provide economically sustainable solutions 
for both organisations and businesses.
When designing a new product or system 
it is not enough that it performs correctly, 
it must also function well in relation to the 
socio-technical system it is part of. To supôÙ
port the ãsystemã part of PSS, and analyse 
the problem as part of a whole, the theory of 
System Dynamics (SD) is included as a secôÙ
ond layer of the theoretical focus. The inôÙ
sights obtainable through SD adds a dimenôÙ
sion of ãhow things affect each otherã inside 
a refugee camp, and cast light upon different 
ãwhat-ifã scenarios. 
Since the main problem addressed in this 
thesis revolves around the artefact ãlatrineã, 
the Theory of Technical Systems (TTS) is inôÙ
cluded as the third and final layer of the theôÙ
oretical focus. TTS will give insights specifiôÙ
Figure 1.	 Combination of the three theories.
==PAGE:17==
introduction I chapter 1 I section 0
3
A planned camp
A ãplanned campã means that it has been decided to build a camp 
at a certain time, with specifications for location and size. This exôÙ
cludes self-settled camps, collective centers, transit centers and 
emergency evacuation centers. This means that the infrastructure 
of the camp is also thought of when planning the camp structure.
Approximately 20.000 residents
This is the recommended number of residents provided by the 
official guidelines. This excludes camps that are too big for some 
kind of central system to reach out to all the sectors of the camp, 
and those that are too small for implementation of a large solution 
to be financially feasible.
Lifetime of minimum one year
In development situations the economic incentive for making lonôÙ
ger-lasting solutions is higher. This excludes temporary camps 
where a sudden emergency calls for a short-term provision of aid. 
There are also different social structures in play, when people have 
to build a kind of life and a community, rather than just survive on 
full support for a few months. 
Located in a rural area
ãRural areaã means areas like a field, plain, desert or bush, often an 
area with plenty of space. This excludes camps located in urban 
areas, which are often placed in existing buildings. However it does 
not imply the host community isnãt present nearby. There may be 
villages or even small towns in very close proximity to the camp.
Distanced from extreme weather conditions
Camps which are exposed to extreme drought, flood, earthquakes, 
hurricanes and alike, will not be included. It is very unlikely for a 
planned camp to be situated in areas like this in the first place. 
This characteristic is merely to say that it is assumed that the basic 
necessities such as solid ground and an acceptable rate of water 
supply is possible. 
Suitable for cash-based aid
The cash-based aid can be at various levels of implementation, it 
might be fully implemented or just in the pipeline, but the camp is 
deemed suitable for such systems by the camp management.  
==PAGE:18==
introduction I chapter 1 I section 0
4
There are two other characteristics which 
are important, but have been deliberately 
excluded from the archetype. These characôÙ
teristics will be considered to a lesser degree 
in this thesis. Firstly, no difference is made 
between different types of refugees, e.g. 
whether they come from Africa or the MidôÙ
dle East. There will of course be cultural difôÙ
ferences between people with origin in difôÙ
ferent parts of the world. Specifically latrine 
usage and norms are very different across 
the world. However, for the sake of simpliciôÙ
ty in this thesis, the inter-cultural differencôÙ
es between people inside the camp are not 
being considered. This means that culture 
is important when discussing e.g. behaviour 
coaching and participation, but it is assumed 
that all new initiatives will need a degree of 
instruction which will be equal to all in the 
same camp. 
Secondly, the geographical position of the 
camp is not being considered. As long as the 
above listed characteristics are fulfilled, all 
locations can apply. However, a quick exôÙ
clusion based on the list above will disclose 
that this type of camps will probably never 
be found in e.g. Denmark or other WestôÙ
ern-European countries. The reason for this, 
is a subject for a whole other discussion. The 
type of camp in mind will typically be presôÙ
ent in Africa and the Middle East, and possiôÙ
bly Asia. There are hundreds of camps which 
fit this archetype (DRC, 2017). A complete list 
of camps is unobtainable, but fact sheets for 
some individual camps, and for many counôÙ
tries can be found online. An example of a 
factsheet can be seen to the right in Figure 2.

The problem statement is approached using 
the following research questions (RQ):
Main RQ: Can high-tech solutions conôÙ
tribute to ensuring adequate sanitation in 
planned refugee camps?
Secondary RQs:
RQ1: How can PSS, TTS and SD be comôÙ
bined to explore and resolve humaniôÙ
tarian issues?
RQ2: What are todays sanitation chalôÙ
lenges, and how can we understand 
them in depth? Which possibilities are 
there for solving the  challenges? 
RQ3: How can we design a concept 
which applies the affordance of new 
technology to address several actorsã 
needs at the same time?
RQ4: How can we inspire humanitarian 
organisations to use new technology? 
These secondary research questions are 
used as leading questions for each of the 
four sections of the thesis, and as support to 
answer the main research question.
1.3.	The Core of the Problem 
The aim of our project is to ensure adequate 
sanitation in planned refugee camps, in orôÙ
der to improve well-being and health of resôÙ
idents by using data-driven solutions. The 
core objective of this thesis is thus to: 
 Understand whether high-tech solutions
 can contribute to ensuring adequate 
sanitation in planned refugee camps
Since the whole refugee crisis is one large 
problem with endless subproblems, it is imôÙ
portant to keep in mind what fraction of the 
larger whole this thesis is concerned with. 
That is the intended function of the objecôÙ
tive. 
If the end result of this thesis produces apôÙ
plicable knowledge about how new technoôÙ
logical solutions can ensure adequate sanitaôÙ
tion and thus create better living conditions 
for refugee camp residents, then the result 
can be considered successful. Disease outôÙ
breaks and undignifying sanitation facilities 
are among the largest problems that refuôÙ
gees face (Byars, 2017; Araya, 2017). EnsurôÙ
ing decent living conditions is important for 
NGOs who are measured and must report to 
donors how well they are using their fundôÙ
ing (DRC, 2017; DRK, 2017). The thesis aims to 
enable NGOs to improve these living condiôÙ
tions. Therefore, NGOs are the main recipiôÙ
ents of this project.

2.	 Combine and alter design theories and methods to form relevant models 
and tools for analysis of complex problems

1.7.	What Does ãSolutionã Mean?
When using the word ãsolutionã, one might 
tend to think of a kind of physical product 
that solves the needs stated in the problem 
definition. When ãsolutionã is used in this 
thesis, this is however not the case. Rather 
than developing and introducing a physical 
product to solve the problem, this thesis 
seeks to change the way the system around 
the problem works - to develop and introôÙ
duce a new way of using the products alôÙ
ready existing. More specifically, we work 
with how the whole system surrounding laôÙ
trines works. Of course, this might include 
certain changes to the products already in 
place, or adding new elements. However, the 
main point of this thesis is not to develop a 
product, but develop the operation of a sysôÙ
tem.  In other words, the solution space for 
our masters project covers systems of prodôÙ
ucts, services, and stakeholders and not sinôÙ
gle products in isolation. 
The system-changing nature of the solution 
is also the main argument for why all three 
theories are interesting to work with. Each of 
the theories has itsã strength, which together 
can form a whole solution. PSS ensures that 
the desired solution is service-oriented; SD 
gives the foundation in a system change; and 
TTS is relevant since the change is connectôÙ
ed to physical artefact(s). 
1.8.	Disclaimer: A refugee camp is 
the last resort
Before moving on, an important point has to 
be made; the whole refugee camp situation 
in itself is not an optimal solution. A refugee 
camp is unwanted by the countries and orôÙ
ganisations creating it, and by the people livôÙ
ing in it; nobody really wants a refugee camp. 
Multiple organisations agree that setting up 
a refugee camp must be the very last option, 
when no other possibilities for hosting the 
refugees exist (UNHCR, 2014a; IOM et al., 
2015).
Working within this setting can seem futile. 
However, the root problems involving wars 
and inhumane living conditions in certain 
parts of the world are unfortunately not exôÙ
pected to be solved any time soon. Refugee 
camps are still widely used solutions, with 
more than four million people living in these 
conditions (UNHCR, 2017a). Even though this 
thesis concerns a concept that is highly unôÙ
desirable, it is still highly relevant, since refôÙ
ugee camps most likely will be an inevitable 
part of the world for many years to come, 
and affect millions of people. The aim for this 
thesis is to bring relief to those affected, not 
to solve their unwanted situation entirely.

Introduction to Section 1
Research Question
Section 1 covers the research clarification part of this thesis. It will give an introducôÙ
tion to the context related to the problem statement, and perform an analysis of the 
theory which will be used to address this problem. The following research question 
will be answered:
RQ1: How can PSS, TTS and SD be combined to explore 
and resolve humanitarian issues?
Outline
First, the refugee crisis will be introduced, followed by a description of how humaniôÙ
tarian NGOs work. A short introduction will also be made to the trending technologies 
in the humanitarian context, and to the issues related to sanitation in refugee camps. 
Then the three theories PSS, TTS and SD will be described and analysed. This includes 
an introduction to the basics of the theory, an analysis of their applicability in the huôÙ
manitarian context, and an elaboration of how they are used in this thesis and what 
their analytical contribution will be. Lastly, it is analysed how the three theories can 
be linked, and a new method of analysis is explored using TTS and SD in combination. 
Outcome
When having finished reading this section, you will have a fundamental understanding 
of the refugee crisis and the current operational work of NGOs in the humanitarian 
context. You will furthermore know the fundamentals of the three theories used in 
this thesis, and understand why we have found it beneficial to use those three in comôÙ
bination.

Figure 5 on the next page shows the annual 
number of displaced people during the last 
ten years. In  2011 the displacement figures 
increase dramatically. The Arab Spring, and 
the prolonged tense situation in the Middle 
East, has caused millions to flee and Syria to 
become the largest source of refugees, overôÙ
taking Afghanistan. Turkey has moreover 
become the worldãs largest refugee hosting 
country, but despite this developing regions 
still host more than 86 percent of all refuôÙ
gees. The situation in Africa has had some 
breakthroughs, but durable solutions are 
still out of reach. Just in sub-Saharan AfriôÙ
ca, more than 15 million people are displaced 
(UNHCR, 2017a). 
In addition to the high numbers of newly 
displaced people, returns have become very 
low, and more refugees are living in proôÙ
tracted refugee situations. Protracted refuôÙ
gee situations are when 25.000 or more refôÙ
ugees from the same nationality have been in 
exile for 5 years or longer in a given asylum 
country. The situation in Syria is for examôÙ
ple now becoming such a protracted refugee 
situation (UNHCR, 2016a, 2014b). This means 
that durable long term solutions are even 
more necessary. 
This chapter gives background knowledge 
on the refugee crisis, the work of humanitarôÙ
ian non-governmental organisations (NGOs) 
and the challenges and possibilities in this 
context. It is not a comprehensive  overview, 
but includes the essentials for understandôÙ
ing the context this thesis operates within.
 
2.1.	The Refugee Crisis 
The past decade has changed the scale and 
character of the humanitarian crisis sigôÙ
nificantly. The humanitarian crisis of global 
forced displacement has reached unpreceôÙ
dented heights. According to the most reôÙ
cent Global Appeal Report (UNHCR, 2017c), 
the number of displaced persons in 2017 is 
projected to reach 68.8 million, of which 17.2 
million have refugee status. 
It is not only the sheer number of people who 
have been forced to flee their home, but the 
acceleration which is worrying. Since 2010 
the number of displaced persons has doubled 
(UNHCR, 2017a). The reasons people leave 
their homes are multiple, and include cliôÙ
mate change, natural disasters, persecution, 
poverty and war. Figure 4 below illustrates 
where the many refugees are in the world. 
Chapter 2	 I Introducing the Context

==PAGE:32==
introducing the context I chapter 2 I section 1
18
development and (re)building of a communiôÙ
ty. They last for more than a year, and often 
several years. The latter is the focus for this 
thesis. Even though the official guidelines 
recommend a maximum number of 20.000 
refugees in a camp (IOM et al., 2015),  camps 
with several hundred thousands refugees 
exist. 8 out of 10 of the largest refugee camps 
are located in Africa. Here people arrive by 
foot, often walking for days. The Middle 
Eastern refugees in contrast are often subôÙ
ject to human trafficking, arriving by truck 
or the dangerous route over the MediterraôÙ
nean ocean. The planned or managed refuôÙ
gee camps all have some level of involvement 
from humanitarian NGOs (UNHCR, 2014a). 
The next paragraph will explore their work.
The demographics of refugees prove anothôÙ
er challenge. More than half of all refugees 
are children below 18 years of age (Figure 
6). Many of these are unaccompanied. In 
2015 almost 75.000 unaccompanied children 
lodged asylum applications (UNHCR, 2017a).
All of these people need a place to stay while 
the situation in their home country stabilisôÙ
es. By far most refugees are accommodated 
privately, especially in the urban areas (FigôÙ
ure 6). Nevertheless more than four million 
people live in planned camps, accounting 
for almost 25% of all refugees. The camps 
are almost exclusively placed in rural areas. 
Camps are often, and should be, used as a 
last resort for accommodation, but the ãpriôÙ
vate accommodationã is not problem-free 
either. Living in the street is e.g. also categoôÙ
rised as private accommodation. In Turkey 
only a few hundred thousand of the more 
than 2.5 million refugees live in camps [1]. 
Due to lack of accommodation for refugees, 
many are living on the streets in dangerous 
and unsanitary conditions. Not all host comôÙ
munities have the possibility or willingness 
to provide refugees with better options. This 
confirms that there is no indication of the 
time for refugee camps being over, however 
criticised it is (UNHCR, 2016a, 2014a).
Response to humanitarian crises can be split 
into two types of situations; relief and deôÙ
velopment. Relief situations are in response 
to an immediate humanitarian crisis such as 
earthquakes or tsunamis, and they typically 
last up to a year. Development situations are, 
as the name indicates, responses involving a

humanitarian  work, it sets out standards for 
all the practical measures in a camp. ExamôÙ
ples are: How many latrines should there be 
in a camp? How to manage disease outbreaks? 
How many calories should a resident eat per 
day?
It is an important part of the standards not 
only to provide a specific amount of aid and 
facilities, but to ensure people-centered huôÙ
manitarian response (The Sphere Project, 
2011). This means that humanitarian NGOs 
must strive to ensure that refugees can live 
in a dignified manner, being an active part of 
the camp and not just beneficiaries.
2.2.2.	 Role Distribution
Before 2005, there was a tendency to perform 
humanitarian response in an ad hoc and unôÙ
structured manner, with many humanitarian 
NGOs overlapping each other. In response 
to this, the Inter-Agency Standing CommitôÙ
tee (IASC) launched a process to improve the 
effectiveness and efficiency of humanitarian 
response through greater predictability, acôÙ
countability, responsibility, and partnership 
in situations of internal displacement [2]. A 
key initiative of this reform was the creation 
of the Cluster Approach. An overview of the 
clusters can be seen in Figure 7.
2.2.	The Work of Humanitarian 
NGOs
This section gives a brief introduction to huôÙ
manitarian NGOsã work, the challenges they 
currently face, and the trends which provide 
new possibilities
2.2.1.	 Principles and Standards
When a humanitarian NGO works in a refôÙ
ugee camp, they have to meet certain stanôÙ
dards. First of all, they have to comply with 
the humanitarian principles of humanity, 
neutrality, impartiality and operational inôÙ
dependence, regardless of whether it is a 
national or international NGO, or nationôÙ
al authority (OCHA, 2012). These principles 
represent the ethical foundation for stakeôÙ
holders carrying out humanitarian work in 
emergencies.
When managing a refugee camp, the manôÙ
agement unit strives to apply minimum 
standards from The Sphere Handbook (The 
Sphere Project, 2011). These standards are 
based on the belief that persons affected by 
a crisis have the right to a life with dignity 
and assistance, and that all possible steps 
must be taken to alleviate human suffering. 
Besides stating general core standards for 
Figure 7.	 Overview of clusters and their leads. Source: IOM et al., 2015
==PAGE:34==
introducing the context I chapter 2 I section 1
20
paigns (DRC, 2017; DRK, 2017). 
These limited resources combined with 
very high demands calls for solutions within 
humanitarian work that are low-cost, effiôÙ
cient, reusable and scalable. The new trends 
emerging within technology in this field is 
therefore a much needed input.
2.3.	New Trends: High-tech 
low-cost initiatives
The nature of humanitarian work can seem 
low-tech, since the primary goal is to alleviôÙ
ate chaotic situations and save lives. HowevôÙ
er, high-tech technologies at relatively low 
prices are actually a large part of the humanôÙ
itarian work done today, and the popularity 
is rising (OCHA, 2014).  This section will give 
a brief overview of what technological initiaôÙ
tives are upcoming in humanitarian work. All 
the technologies are elaborated in Chapter 7.
2.3.1.	 What ãNewã Technologies are 
Emerging?
The following technologies (Figure 8) are not 
per se new, but their application within huôÙ
manitarian work is still novel. They all have 
in common that they are relatively high-tech 
low-cost (HTLC) technologies, meaning that 
they are suitable for humanitarian work. 
The first technology mentioned here is the 
Internet of Things (IoT). It is described by 
the IT University of Copenhagen as ãa globôÙ
al infrastructure for the information society, 
enabling advanced services by interconnectôÙ
ing (physical and virtual) things based on exôÙ
isting and evolving interoperable information 
and communication technologiesã [3]. The 
IoT is gaining popularity in the industry of 
today due to the reduced costs in computing 
in general, and the increasing connectivity 
around the world. It is estimated that around 
95% of the world population live within the 
coverage of the 2G network, meaning that 
more people have access to telecommunicaôÙ
tion than to running water (Biggs et al., 2016). 
IoT is rising from being a technology that is 
improving existing business, to being a busiôÙ
ness on its own. 
Clusters are groups of humanitarian NGOs 
in each main sector of humanitarian action. 
They are designated by the IASC and have 
clear responsibilities for coordination. 
The advantages of the cluster approach, 
compared to the working methods used beôÙ
fore, is that multiple humanitarian NGOs can 
be present in the same camp without getting 
in each othersã way. There can, and will still 
be overlaps between the different clusters, 
and in some camps one organisation may be 
responsible for multiple areas, or multiple 
organisations may co-operate in one cluster. 
The idea is to give a better role distribution 
and smoother coordination.
2.2.3.	 High Demands
Even though the cluster approach has enôÙ
abled the NGOs to cooperate better, there 
is still a large and growing number of actors 
involved in humanitarian operations, with a 
high level of diversity. This adds to the comôÙ
plexity of carrying out humanitarian work. 
(Lindenberg, 2001; Besiou et al., 2011) 
Since most of the work done by humanitarôÙ
ian NGOs is funded by donations,  the many 
actors involved also increase the competiôÙ
tion for resources. This means that an NGO 
also have to put a lot of work into documentôÙ
ing how they do their work, and not just in 
carrying it out. The donors are increasingôÙ
ly demanding that the funding is used effiôÙ
ciently, and that most (if not all) of the funds 
go straight to humanitarian aid, and not to 
costs like staffing, administration and camôÙ
 Over the past decade, faced with 
growing resource constraints, 
humanitarian agencies have held high 
hopes for contributions from the 
private sector, particularly the 
business community.
- Office for the Coordination of 
Humanitarian Affairs (OCHA, 2014)
ã
==PAGE:35==
introducing the context I chapter 2 I section 1
21
Another widely used term in todaysã indusôÙ
try is Big Data. The term is used to describe 
a data set that is large and complex (Chen 
et al., 2014). The data that comes out of an 
IoT installation will often be characterised 
as Big Data, and these two terms are hence 
closely related. They can however still exist 
individually. Big Data analytics has previôÙ
ously been used for analysis and long-term 
decision-making. However, when connectôÙ
ed to IoT it can enable real-time monitoring, 
which enables immediate response. Big Data 
is thus shifting towards also being a proacôÙ
tive tool. 
The newest trend mentioned here is blockôÙ
chain, which barely has any use cases yet. 
Bitcoin is the most known application of the 
technology. It is most easily described as 
a very secure type of code, which enables 
sharing and transfer of value and information 
(Gupta, 2017). In the past half year blockchain 
has gone from rather unknown, to becomôÙ
ing recognised by humanitarian NGOs as a 
technology with big potential that should be 
investigated further (UN, 2017).  
Beside these globally emerging technologies, 
it is also worth mentioning that technologies 
which are domesticated in developed counôÙ
tries, are also being tested and used in the 
humanitarian context. Examples of these are 
smartphones, apps, and credit cards. These 
are not per se new initiatives in the humanôÙ
itarian context, but they are also relevant 
since these technologies are closely related. 
2.3.2.	 Challenges with Technologies
There are several challenges with using 
these high-tech low-cost technologies in 
developing countries and in refugees camps 
in particular (Biggs et al., 2016). 
Policy-wise, the big challenge is the security 
of the data collected, and the privacy of the 
people affected by the data. Other challengôÙ
es are the lack of people with relevant techôÙ
nical skills in the local areas, and the regulaôÙ
tions and limitations on cross border traffic 
of the data, which would often be needed to 
get it analysed properly. 
Technically, a large challenge is using power 
consuming technologies in locations that are 
usually of the electrical grid. Furthermore, 
even though these solutions are classified as 
high-tech low-cost, the cost is still a chalôÙ
lenge in contexts with very limited funds, 
since initial investments are often high.
These challenges will always be factors in 
a series of trade-offs. Examples of these 
are complexity versus robustness of physôÙ
ical technical equipment, range and data 
throughput versus cost, and battery life verôÙ
sus data throughput.

2.4.	 Sanitation in Refugee Camps
The building and maintenance of latrines are 
part of the work done by the Water, SanitaôÙ
tion and Hygiene (WASH) cluster. This group 
takes care of not only latrines but also clean 
water, sewage and showers. There will ofôÙ
ten be overlaps with other clusters, such as 
the Health cluster. There may also be other 
NGOs  responsible for e.g. cleaning the laôÙ
trines. All this makes cooperation important 
(IOM et al., 2015). 
When managing the construction and mainôÙ
tenance of latrines, the overall responsibility 
lies with the WASH cluster employees.  The 
usual procedure in an chaotic emergency 
situation is that other needs, such as nutriôÙ
tion and medical help, are met first. Later the 
need for adequate sanitation facilities is met, 
often after problems related to their absence 
occur, such as disease outbreaks (Byars, 
2017). Even though the absence of adequate 
sanitation facilities is not directly deadly, the 
resulting health issues are. The consequent 
costly medical treatments erode the already 
scarce resources.
When establishing latrine facilities, the first 
solutions are often improvised and made 
from few materials. An example of such first 
facilities could be pitholes (Picture 2). Later 
the latrines will be more well-constructed, 
or separate toilet buildings might be erected 
(Picture 3). Several templates, standards and 
checklists exist on how to build latrines (e.g. 
UNHCR, 2015b, 2015d, 2017b). 
Decisions regarding the construction and 
placement of latrines are aspired to be 
made with the participation from refugees. 
This will often be through a refugee WASH 
committee, where it is desired to have repôÙ
resentatives from both men, women, youth, 
and people with disabilities. It is however 
not always possible to implement participaôÙ
tion  due to time constraints, or the refugees 
might not prioritise or wish to participate 
(IOM et al., 2015). 
Picture 2.	Poor quality plastic shielding 
of pithole. Source: TEAR Australia
Picture 3.	Isobox, a common latrine 
solution. Source: Araya, Nea Kavala,2017

2.5.	Humanitarian Innovation
The subject of humanitarian innovation is 
not only on the agenda of humanitarian 
NGOs, but has also made its way to the agenôÙ
da of  corporate, political and public organs. 
An important driver for this interest is the 17 
Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) forôÙ
mulated by the UN in 2015 (Figure 9) [4].
Partnerships between NGOs, governments 
and private companies are essential to reach 
the goals, and thus for humanitarian innovaôÙ
tion. This thesis strives to bring the technical 
private sector closer to that of the humaniôÙ
tarian organisations. It therefore closely reôÙ
lates to goal number seventeen; Partnership 
for the goals, but does so through focusing 
on goal six; Clean water and sanitation. 
The interest in using technology for humanôÙ
itarian aid is rising (Betts & Bloom, 2014). 
During the time it has taken to conduct this 
thesis, talks on the use of blockchain in the 
humanitarian context have begun emerging, 
and recently even the UN opened a seminar 
saying it was one of the three most interestôÙ
ing areas to watch (UN, 2017). The interest 
from universities is also growing, and several 
bodies at the Technical University of DenôÙ
Figure 9.	 The Sustainable Development Goals. Source: UN.
 ã The SDGs are good, 
because companies can see 
how they can contribute
- Thomas Bustrup,
Dansk Industri (UN, 2017)
mark are looking at how the university can 
contribute, and how partnerships with e.g. 
humanitarian NGOs can be formed.  
Humanitarian actors may be drawn by the 
funding and know-how that the private secôÙ
tor offers. However, they are still hesitant 
due to the fear disregarding the humanitarôÙ
ian principles, in favor of commercial goals. 
The organisations are therefore struggling 
with how to tackle these potential partnerôÙ
ships (OCHA, 2014).
To conclude, this thesis comes at a highôÙ
ly relevant time, where innovation and new 
initiatives constantly emerge.

To fulfill these three objectives, we apôÙ
proached the task with scientific methodoloôÙ
gy, inspired by systematic literature reviews. 
The literature search was conducted by usôÙ
ing a search protocol (e.g. Biolchini et al., 
2005; Kirk, 2013). This systematic approach 
was chosen in order to make the literature 
review complete and replicable. The search 
protocols reflect the objectives above, and 
can be seen in Appendix page 6.
Keywords for the search were chosen 
through a number of iterations, based on the 
literature found and feedback from other reôÙ
searchers. These keywords were combined 
in numerous ways to achieve results. An exôÙ
ample could be:
ãSystem Dynamicsã AND (Humanitar* OR 
Refugee OR Crisis OR Camp OR cluster)
Articles were sorted to only include English 
results, but format was not restricted. The 
databases used were mainly Web of Science, 
Mendeley, Scopus and FindIt. 
Research in humanitarian work has an applied 
nature, and many of the subjects researched 
moreover are within very new fields. ThereôÙ
fore, for the review of the research in the 
humanitarian context, some unconventional 
sources were also used. Youtube, social meôÙ
dia and online news media all contributed to 
giving a basic understanding of and insight 
into the situation, which scientific articles 
could not.
This thesis works simultaneously with three 
different areas of theory and methods within 
the domain of engineering design and prodôÙ
uct development; Product/Service-Systems 
(PSS), The Theory of Technical Systems 
(TTS), and System Dynamics (SD).
The research question for this literature 
study (RQ1) is: 
How can PSS, TTS and SD be 
combined to explore and resolve 
humanitarian issues?
The analysis will explore how each individual 
area can contribute to resolving humanitarôÙ
ian issues, and how a linkage between them 
can generate new ways of approaching them.
This chapter describes the basics of the theôÙ
ories and methods, in order to make future 
use of these theories readily comprehensiôÙ
ble. The description is brief, followed by a 
more thorough discussion of their applicaôÙ
bility in the humanitarian context, in order 
to answer the research question. Finally the 
three theories are linked together, and their 
interplay discussed.

researchersã systematic reviews whenever

The literature search gave very few results

3.2.	Product/Service-Systems
This section covers Product/Service-SysôÙ
tems theory (PSS). The basics of the theory 
are introduced, followed by an analysis on 
how the theory can contribute to the huôÙ
manitarian context and how it will be used 
in the thesis.
3.2.1.	 The Basics
PSS is an innovation strategy, whose core 
lies in creating the right combination of 
products and services needed to aid a user 
to fulfill their needs. The theory dates back 
to the late 1990s (Goedkoop et al., 1999) and 
many similar concepts have emerged and 
are used in the industry (Sakao & Lindahl, 
2009). PSS solutions distinguish themselves 
by taking a broad holistic view on technical 
systems and their life cycle. The usersã needs 
and activities are considered from the very 
beginning of the development process. PSS 
thinking has furthermore been linked closeôÙ
ly to sustainability and ecodesign. (Sakao & 
Lindahl, 2009; McAloone & Andreasen 2004). 
The ãPSS way of thinkingã changes the focus 
from the value of the product itself, to the 
value of the utility that lies within the prodôÙ
uct and the related services (Tan et al., 2007). 
In that way, the concept of e.g. a car will 
change from the product ãcarã to the utility 
ãsafe and comfortable transportation from A 
to Bã. 
Specific tools have been developed to supôÙ
port the PSS methodology. In this thesis 
we take our starting point in the PROTEUS 
methods (Finken et al., 2013), which support 
the PSS strategy development. These tools 
support the four dimensions, called the PSS 
framework, which is believed to be importôÙ
ant for a PSS concept to be successful (FinkôÙ
en et al., 2013). The dimensions can be seen 
in Figure 10, and are Ecosystem, Value propôÙ
osition, User activity cycle and Offering life 
cycle. The ecosystem is about understanding 
the whole network of stakeholders and their 
needs, influences and motivation. The value 
proposition captures the customers actual

3.2.4.	 Theory Models: The Analytical
Contribution
This section presents the PSS tools which

Ecosystem map
There are many ways to map stakeholders 
and the interactions between them (Jû¡rôÙ
gensen, 2009). In PSS it is called ecosystem 
mapping (Finken et al., 2013). The stakeholdôÙ
er map gives an overview of the stakeholders 
which are important for a company, a PSS or 
the specific value offering. The interconnecôÙ
tions between stakeholders are especially 
important and can provide valuable insights. 
Stakeholders can e.g. be connected by monôÙ
ey, information, service or products. Often 
different colours, icons and figures are used 
to make the ecosystem a suitable graphical 
representation, which can be easily commuôÙ
nicated. One example is shown in Figure 11.
User Activity Cycle
The UAC tool (Finken et al., 2013) is a cycliôÙ
cal representation of a potential userãs acôÙ
tivities and needs. It is used to identify and 
gain insight into these needs and activities, 
which can then be used to explore new busiôÙ
ness opportunities. A UAC is, as many of the 
PSS tools, a suitable communication tool 
both internally in an organisation, and to 
In spite of this business oriented focus, we 
argue that PSS can have great benefits for the 
humanitarian context. Firstly, humanitarian 
NGOs in some ways have similar needs to 
the business. And secondly, a PSS is a holisôÙ
tic solution, which can encompass the high 
complexity of the humanitarian context.
The strong focus on the life cycle of the PSS 
means that the solutions will be designed for 
durability and sustainability from the very 
beginning (Baines et al., 2007). The economôÙ
ic focus mentioned by Haase, and the foôÙ
cus on durability, efficiency and effectivity 
matches well with business needs. However, 
the limited funds and resources humanitarôÙ
ian NGOs have at their disposal give them a 
similar need and desire to reduce costs and 
boost efficiency. Moreover, the focus on the 
utility breaks with the idea that each indiôÙ
vidual must have ãtheir ownã of something, 
being it e.g. a car or a product covering baôÙ
sic human needs (Tan et al., 2007). The idea 
of selling services, rather than products, can 
enable selling to the bottom of the pyramid 
[5], and matches well the limited resources 
obtainable by the individual refugee (Nielsen 
& Santos, 2013). 
PSS is often highlighted as the way to obtain 
a resource-efficient, circular economy (TukôÙ
ker, 2015). The PSS framework takes a holisôÙ
tic, systemic approach to design. SystemôÙ
ic Design has been recognised as likely to 
achieve better and more sustainable results 
than traditional product design approaches. 
Thoroughly understanding the ecosystem is 
essential for designing good solutions in the 
humanitarian context. PSS provides tools to 
map and get an overview of the complex sysôÙ
tems in the humanitarian world. Moreover, 
the methods are easy to use in non-academôÙ
ic settings and are created specifically to enôÙ
gage stakeholders and let them understand 
each othersã needs. 
We therefore argue that PSS can support 
this activity and that the PSS framework can 
provide valuable insights.

3.3.	The Theory of Technical 
Systems
This section covers the Theory of Technical 
Systems (TTS). The basics of the theory are 
introduced, followed by an analysis on how 
the theory can contribute to the humanitarôÙ
ian context and how it will be applied.
3.3.1.	 The Basics
TTS describes how a transformation process 
is influenced by related technical systems, 
human systems, information systems and 
management systems. It originates from a 
publication in 1988 by Vladimir Hubka, and 
revolves around the nature of technical sysôÙ
tems (Hubka & Eder, 1988). A technical sysôÙ
tem is by Hubka defined as ãall types of man-
made artefactsã. The theory aims to bring 
together various viewpoints of many types 
of professionals, and show where and how 
they influence engineering products. It also 
explains the influences that a product exerts 
on its environment. The theory is intended 
to be general and abstract, and applicable to 
many different disciplines within engineerôÙ
ing. 
TTS has since been used as a foundation 
for other design engineering theories (AnôÙ
dreasen & McAloone, 2008), and also modôÙ
ified in different ways by other researchers, 
e.g. (Andreasen et al., 2015), (Matzen, 2009) 
and (Tan et al., 2005). However, for the purôÙ
pose of the analysis in this thesis, it is deôÙ
sired to use Hubkaãs original version of the 
theory and its core. 
3.3.2.	 Applicability in Humanitarian 	 	
Context
The concepts of TTS is relevant anywhere 
a technical system plays a central role in a 
transformation process, which is almost in 
any real life situation. Since the theory is 
made exactly with the purpose of being geôÙ
neric and widely applicable, it is hard to arôÙ
gue that it should not have applicability in 
any particular field. According to the literôÙ
ature search carried out, it seems that there 
is no direct coupling between TTS and the 
humanitarian context so far. However, this 
does not necessarily mean that the princiôÙ
ples behind TTS are not applicable when opôÙ
erating in the humanitarian context. 
Using TTS, Hubka intends to carefully disôÙ
assemble the elements related to a certain 
change, and categorise the many different 
influences, in order to create an overview. 
The fundamental idea behind this has formed 
the basis of later design engineering theories 
and methods, such as Systematic FormgivôÙ
ing Theory (Tjalve, 1976) and Domain TheôÙ
ory (Andreasen, 1980). These theories have 
again developed into further design engiôÙ
neering methods, which finally end up in the 
current approaches for product design and 
development, referred to as ãthe CopenhaôÙ
gen Schoolã (Andreasen & McAloone, 2008). 
These basic product design and developôÙ
ment methods most certainly have validity 
in the humanitarian context, since they form 
the entire basis for carrying out this thesis 
in the first place. They make up the foundaôÙ
tion of how we are viewing and working with 
the problem. It is hereby not said that TTS is 
the origin of all engineering design methods. 
However, the argument stands that since 
this thesis is an application of engineering 
design in a humanitarian context, the use of 
TTS will automatically be valid. 
3.3.3.	 This Theoryãs Place in the Thesis
Seen in relation to the two other theories 
used, TTS will be the most artefact-orientôÙ
ed engineering theory used. It contains eleôÙ
ments of both analysis and synthesis.
The desired output from using TTS as an anôÙ
alytical tool is the mapping of processes and 
their related stakeholders/artefacts, in orôÙ
der to reveal new insights. What might seem 
obvious at a glance will perhaps reveal areas 
for improvement or problematic procedures, 
when looked at the level of detail that TTS 
enables. It will enable the approach of sciôÙ
entific methodology, where all variables are 
listed and varied one at a time, instead of 
trying to deal with everything at once, and 
==PAGE:46==
literature study I chapter 3 I section 1
32
not knowing which change caused which 
effect. In short: TTS can help explore where 
change can happen in the given transformaôÙ
tion process.
3.3.4.	 Theory Models: The Analytical
Contribution
The core of TTS is the transformation modôÙ
el (Figure 13). This model seeks to map out 
what happens when, in very broad terms, 
something undergoes some kind of change, 
and more technically speaking, when an inôÙ
put is transformed to another state and beôÙ
comes an output. The theory says that when 
any type of transformation process happens, 
the change is induced by:
Ts: Technical system
The products used to create the transformaôÙ
tion 
Hs: Human System
The humans/stakeholders involved in the 
transformation
Is: Information System
The information needed to make the transôÙ
formation
Ms: Management and Goal System
The objectives and rules to follow during the 
transformation

3.4.2.	 Applicability in Humanitarian 
Context
SD is a theory which has won wide applicaôÙ
tion on social issues, compared to PSS and 
TTS. Recently courses and books on how to 
use SD for social change have emerged (e.g. 
Stroh, 2015). In the following we will explain 
and argue how SD is a useful tool in the huôÙ
manitarian context. Four arguments form 
the basis of this discussion:
1. SD can help humanitarian NGOs profesôÙ
sionalise their operations. 
2. The humanitarian context is complex and 
SD is good for complex problems. 
3. SD is a strong change management tool 
with potential to bridge the gap between 
NGOs and corporate organisations. 
4. SD can capture the worth of soft values, 
which is important for NGOs.
As mentioned in ã2.2. The Work of HumaniôÙ
tarian NGOsã, the demands from benefactors 
are becoming stricter. As high efficiency and 
effectiveness is demanded by donors, huôÙ
manitarian NGOs are forced to professionôÙ
alise (Thomas and Kopczak, 2005), but the 
NGOs donãt necessarily have the means and 
methods to do so. Several factors differenôÙ
tiate NGOs from corporate organisations, 
including extreme time pressure, very poor 
operating conditions, and lots of decisions 
having to be made on-site (GonûÏalves, 2008). 
The methods developed by and for corpoôÙ
rate organisations can often be challenging, 
or even dangerous to apply in this context 
(Mannell, 2010). SD suffers from the same 
potential flaw as many other methods, as it 
is also developed for an industrial context, 
not a humanitarian. However SD is naturalôÙ
ly designed to capture complexities, and is 
therefore potentially more agile than other 
methods (Besiou et al., 2011).
The humanitarian context is complex due 
to both the number of actors involved, time 
delays, uncertain conditions, infrastructure 
and a number of other challenges. In other 
words the humanitarian context has both 
dynamical and detail complexity. 
3.4.	System Dynamics
This section covers System Dynamics (SD). 
The basics of the theory are introduced, folôÙ
lowed by an analysis on how the theory can 
contribute to the humanitarian context and 
how it will be used in the thesis.
3.4.1.	 The Basics
SD is a modelling and simulation methodolôÙ
ogy, which can be used to study how actors 
in a system interact and influence each other 
over time. It was originally developed by Jay 
W. Forrester in the 1950s to study inventory 
management, but has since expanded beôÙ
yond industrial complexities to include enôÙ
vironmental and social issues (Berariu et al., 
2016; Besiou et al., 2011). 
SD explains systems using variables, flows, 
and dynamics, which are communicated usôÙ
ing a graphical language. The variables are 
elements, which can be seen, felt, counted or 
measured. The dynamics are their behaviour 
over time. The theory is behaviour-oriented, 
in the way that it is not as important what 
the specific problem is, it is the nature of 
the problem which is studied. It is analysed 
using behaviour archetypes, which produce 
characteristic behaviour that is common for 
many systems. By understanding and changôÙ
ing these archetypes, all kinds of problems 
can be addressed (Meadows, 2009).
SD is a form of system thinking, and can be 
used both qualitatively and quantitatively, 
often using computational aids. The idea is 
that in order to ãfix something you are first 
obliged to understand the whole systemã 
(Lewis Thomas, according to Stroh, 2015). 
What the ãwhole systemã is, can be hard to 
define, since a contradicting mantra of sysôÙ
tem thinking is that there are no separate 
systems; everything is connected. It is hence 
important to specify the boundaries of the 
system. 
==PAGE:48==
literature study I chapter 3 I section 1
34
same model. The argument is that by not 
giving soft values a value, they are by default 
assigned the only value which is known not 
to be true - zero (Forrester, 1961). SD takes 
these values into account, as stocks can be 
non-measurable values e.g. a feeling or mood 
(Meadows, 2009). It can do so, because the 
important aspect is the behaviour of the sysôÙ
tem, not necessarily the actual values. These 
soft values are important for humanitarian 
NGOs, as e.g. the level of panic or feeling of 
responsibility can have crucial effect on the 
behaviour of people and systems. 
The four arguments form our confidence 
that SD can be highly relevant for the huôÙ
manitarian context and specifically for this 
thesis. 
3.4.3.	 This Theoryãs Place in the Thesis
System Dynamics is a completely new theory 
to both authors of this thesis, and is recently 
winning recognition in the humanitarian and 
social context. We therefore consider the 
theory a fresh take on the issues humanitarôÙ
ian NGOs face, but also a great opportunity 
to broaden the theoretical toolbox in engiôÙ
neering design. 
SD brings a way to model and work with 
different scenarios, which the rather static 
models in TTS and PSS do not provide. The 
opportunity to show the true influence and 
importance of softer values is also welcomed. 
These values are often expressed as stateôÙ
ments next to other models, and are diffiôÙ
cult to communicate and agree upon with 
stakeholders. Therefore it is interesting that 
others have obtained good results using SD 
as a change management tool for communiôÙ
cation across multiple organisations. It is our 
ambition that SD in this thesis can support 
bridging the gap between corporate and 
humanitarian NGOs, and give all stakeholdôÙ
ers valuable insight into complex problems. 
Detail complexity arises when there is a 
large number of components and/or posôÙ
sible combinations in a system (GonûÏalves, 
2008). Detail complexity is to a high degree 
present in the humanitarian context, which 
is apparent by e.g. the high number of acôÙ
tors. SD  demands clear system boundaries 
and definition of which level of detail we are 
working on. This type of complexity must 
also be managed in PSS, which might prove 
useful when combining the theories.
Dynamic complexity is on the other hand 
one of SDãs great strengths. GonûÏalves, 2011 
and Besiou et al., 2011 both discuss this subôÙ
ject. They argue humanitarian NGOs work 
in a context with high dynamic complexity, 
and that SD methodology can capture these 
dynamics. Dynamic complexity arises from 
cause-and-effect interactions of agents, 
but especially when the dimension of time 
delays is taken into account. According to 
GonûÏalves, dynamic complexity is similarly 
described in SD literature. Dynamic comôÙ
plexity is important to understand, as people 
ãtend to use simple mental models to make 
decisionsã (Forrester, 1971). But the world is 
not correctly captured using a simple mental 
model - therefore humansã decisions are ofôÙ
ten contra-productive to the goals we want 
to reach (Meadows, 2009; Stroh, 2015). SD is 
therefore important in order to understand 
the complexity and make it comprehensible. 
This act of making the complex compreôÙ
hensible is a strong advantage of SD. SD can 
be a great visualisation tool, and has gained 
popularity as a boundary object between 
modellers and actors (Stroh, 2015). Its apôÙ
plicability as a change management tool is 
even highlighted, and a change management 
model has been built upon the SD methodolôÙ
ogy (Stroh, 2015). This suggests that SD can 
be used to bridge the gap between the corôÙ
porate and humanitarian world, which is a 
learning objective of this thesis. 
Last, but not least SD is one of the few tools 
which can encompass both ãsoftã non-meaôÙ
surable values and economic values in the 
==PAGE:49==
literature study I chapter 3 I section 1
35
rises, the number of free spaces in the kinôÙ
dergartens will fall.   
Time delay creates the dynamics of the sysôÙ
tem. Time is a critical factor in SD because 
long and short-term impacts of a change are 
often very different, and even reverse. Time 
delays can be minutes, months or even years. 
Time delay is depicted with two parallel lines 
transverse to the flow arrows. 
With these three elements combined we now 
have a causal loop diagram, and can analyse 
the loops present in the model.
A central concept in SD is feedback loops. A 
feedback loop is a circular structure, where 
a succession of causes and effects travel 
around a loop and come back to affect the 
initial variable. There are two types of feedôÙ
back loops, balancing (B) and reinforcing (R). 
If a change in a variable travels around the 
loop and results in a effect in the same direcôÙ
tion on that variable, the loop is reinforcing 
and we mark the loop ãRã. If a change in a 
variable results in an effect in the opposite 
direction, the loop is balancing, and we mark 
it ãBã. The causes and effects are marked with 
a rounded square and named variables, but 
could in principle be taken out of the modôÙ
el and become a variable of their own. The 
difference simply lies in the boundaries, and 
what we wish to focus on. 
3.4.4.	 Theory Models: The Analytical 	 	
Contribution
SD relies heavily on graphical representaôÙ
tions of systems. The visual mapping of the 
problem gives the opportunity to ãsay evôÙ
erything at once, which is great since sysôÙ
tems happen all at onceã (Meadows, 2009). In 
this thesis we are working with unquantified 
SD models, also called causal loop diagrams 
(Figure 14), and consists of three elements; 
variables, flows and time delay (Zsifkovits et 
al., 2016).
Variables are the foundation of the system. 
They store a certain quantity of material 
or information. Variables do not have to be 
physical, but can also be ãgoodwillã or ãhopeã. 
We can think of the variable as a bathtub, 
which can be more or less full depending on 
the in- and out-flow. Variables are repreôÙ
sented with a box.
Flows are the elements which modify the 
quantity of the variable e.g. how much is in 
the bathtub. Flows are represented with an 
arrow. When the arrow points to another 
variable, we give it a plus or minus symbol. 
The plus indicates that a change in the origôÙ
inal variable, will result in a similar change 
in the variable it points to. E.g. if there are 
more children born, the population number 
will rise. The minus indicates that a change 
in the original variable will have an opposite 
effect. E.g. if the number of children born

TTS and SD
In his descriptions of transformation sysôÙ
tems, Hubka draws the transformation modôÙ
el as shown in Figure 15. A feedback-arrow is 
added at the end of the transformation proôÙ
cess, as a ãside effectã of changing the operôÙ
and from one state to another. 
Hubka writes: 
ãImplied in this model are superimposed proôÙ
cesses of feedback which involve measuring 
the output of a (total or partial) system or 
process, comparing this to a desired standard 
or goal, and dynamically altering the input to 
attempt to correct any detected error or defiôÙ
ciencyã (Hubka & Eder, 1988). 
This idea of feedback in the transformation 
system (note: not in the technical system 
itself, which has so far been the subject of 
definition) correlates quite closely with the 
ideas of SD. 
Hubka continues to write: 
ãThe dynamic behavior of the processes may 
be stable, or problems of instability may occur. 
Such feedback takes place for the operand, for 
the process, and for each of the operators - it 
is thus the duty of internal and external feedôÙ
back loops for each system. Feedback is one of 
the relationships between elements of a transôÙ
formation systemã (Hubka & Eder, 1988). 
3.5.	Linkage Between the Three
Theories
As it has just been described, the three theôÙ
ories are believed to be capable of having 
a useful interplay if linked together. It will 
now be explored how the theories have been 
combined previously and what the potential 
for linking them is. 
3.5.1.	 Previous Combination of 
Theories
To our knowledge, there are to date no preôÙ
vious works combining all three theories. 
It will now be examined what relations has 
been made in the literature so far.
PSS and TTS
As mentioned earlier, many engineering deôÙ
sign methods have roots in TTS, and it can 
thus be said that PSS and TTS are somewhat 
automatically connected due to both being 
engineering design methods. 
If looking at more specific couplings beôÙ
tween PSS and TTS, a good case is the linkage 
made between the UAC and the transformaôÙ
tion model made by (Matzen, 2009). Matzen 
makes a long series of transformation modôÙ
els, which are linked together to perform a 
UAC. In other words, each step in the UAC 
is expanded and analysed further using the 
transformation model. 
PSS and SD
During the literature search there have not 
been found any examples of connecting PSS 
and SD. There have been examples of PSS 
innovation methods analysed using SD, but 
none which truly use the two methods toôÙ
gether. However, the way PSS uses system 
boundaries to limit the ecosystem of interôÙ
est is similar to the way SD uses boundarôÙ
ies to limit the scope of the problem adôÙ
dressed. The theories both struggle with 
setting boundaries when scoping problems, 
especially with regards to sustainability isôÙ
sues (Nabavi, 2017). There can also be drawn 
connections from the way the life cycle of a 
Figure 15.	 Transformation model. Source: Hubka 
& Eder, 1988
==PAGE:51==
literature study I chapter 3 I section 1
37
System Dynamics
The ãsystemã in SD is characterised by being 
a living and changing organism. According to 
SD, everything is a system, and all systems 
are connected to other systems. 
Meadows defines: 
ãA system is an interconnected set of elements 
that is coherently organized in a way that 
achieves something. If you look at that defiôÙ
nition closely for a minute, you can see that a 
system must consist of three kinds of things: 
elements, interconnections, and a function or 
purposeã (Meadows, 2009).
She continues to describe how the most imôÙ
portant part of a system is its function or 
purpose. SD focuses in general more on what 
the outcome and behaviour of the system is, 
rather than the parts of the system. This is 
in contrast to both other theoriesã definiôÙ
tions, where PSS is partly focused on what 
elements are present in the system, and TTS 
is almost entirely focused on the elements 
themselves. 
How Do The Definitions Work Together?
To summarise the above, each theoryãs defiôÙ
nition of ãsystemã is:
It is clear that the three theories do not have 
the same definition of what a system is. They 
are different because their original purposôÙ
es are different. However, since ãsystemã is 
such a broad term, this does not necessarôÙ
ily mean that the theories are then incomôÙ
patible. According to the Merriam-Webster 
dictionary, a system is defined as ãA reguôÙ
larly interacting or interdependent group 
This again implies that Hubka is aware of 
the fact that the mechanisms of feedback 
are essential in the operation of a system. 
He continues to explain that feedback is imôÙ
portant in e.g. the development, testing and 
modification of the design of a prototype, 
meaning feedback from the users to the deôÙ
signer. However, regarding the dynamics of 
the feedback loops within the system, HubôÙ
ka does not go into further details. This is 
where SD takes over in explaining this part 
of the field, for the sake of this thesis. 
3.5.2.	 Common Denominator
In all three theories, one word recurs; sysôÙ
tems. Based on the previous definitions and 
discussion of the three theories, it is clear 
that the word ãsystemã plays an important 
role in this thesis. However, it is not a very 
specific word. How does each theoryãs defiôÙ
nition of ãsystemã correlate with the others, 
and does this have an impact on how the 
theories work together?
Product/Service Systems
The ãsystemã in PSS is somewhat vaguely 
defined in the literature. However, from sysôÙ
tematic literature reviews (e.g. Haase et al., 
2017; Tukker, 2015) it seems the meaning beôÙ
hind ãsystemã is the fact that products and 
services are working together. Since PSS 
deals with the concept of mixing products 
and services, which were originally offered 
individually, this new combination has been 
summarised as a ãsystemã.
Theory of Technical Systems
The ãsystemã in TTS has a more techniôÙ
cal definition than in PSS; it is an element 
with multiple parts. It is by Hubka defined 
that these elements can be called ãtechnical 
meansã, which ãare designated as systems 
with particular attributesã (Hubka & Eder, 
1988). So here the system is in fact only the 
product itself, compared to PSS where the 
system was the combination of product and 
service.

The point of this discussion is to justify the 
compatibility of the three theories, despite 
them having different definitions of the 
same word. Each theoryãs definition actually 
reflects quite well what the analytical conôÙ

section 2

Introduction to Section 2
Research Question
Section 1 gave a brief introduction to the context related to the problem statement, 
along with an analysis of the theory which will be used to address this problem. SecôÙ
tion 2 will cover the first descriptive study of this thesis, and will dive deeper into an 
analysis of where the real challenge lies within ensuring adequate access to latrines 
using high-tech solutions. The following research question will be answered:
RQ2: What are todays sanitation challenges, and how can we understand 
them in depth? Which possibilities are there for solving the challenges? 
Outline
First, we will describe the network surrounding a refugee camp and analyse the presôÙ
ent challenges related to sanitation, seen from three different angles; the NGOs, 
the WASH-management and the refugee. For this purpose we use System Dynamics 
modelling and User Activity Cycles. Then we analyse how the humanitarian sector 
has adapted solutions related to the problem statement, in order to understand the 
maturity of the humanitarian sector and therefore the solution space. This will be 
done through three case studies, featuring multiple short cases, with an analysis of 
their successes and failures. Third, we will analyse how mature the technology related 
to high-tech solutions is to operate in a humanitarian context. Fourth and lastly, we 
will conclude on all three analyses together in order to set the scene for the concept 
development. 
Outcome
When having finished reading this section, you will know the challenges related to 
sanitation in a refugee camp, and know examples of how humanitarian NGOs have 
worked with some of these challenges so far. You will furthermore get a fundamenôÙ
tal understanding of the technologies trending in the humanitarian context. You will 
lastly know the most important aspects to consider when starting the concept develôÙ
opment phase.

4.1.1.	 In Field Actors
In the field, a multitude of clusters fill out the

WASH service provider, meaning an NGO or 
a company who is responsible for delivering 
the physical equipment for the latrines and 
other sanitation facilities. 
The WASH management also collaborates 
with a selection of refugees in the camp, 
who are collected in a WASH committee. 
The committee represents the populationãs 
point of view when matters of e.g. placement 
of latrines are discussed. In addition to the 
refugees in the WASH committee, the rest 
of the camp population is naturally also relôÙ
evant when discussing adequate sanitation. 
All people in the camp are connected to the 
sanitation facilities, due to their basic human 
needs. The arrows connecting the actors, tell 
us, that it is especially information and prodôÙ

The ecosystem map is much larger than deôÙ

along with all the different service providers 
and committees. It is worth noting that since 
both the clusters and service provider roles 
are filled by NGOs, and one NGO can take on 
several different roles in the same camp, the 
roles and tasks might overlap.

5.1.	Rapid Modelling Techniques
Three UACs and three causal loop diagrams 
were modelled related to the sanitation chalôÙ
lenges. The tactic for building these models 
was to make a first rapid iteration, based on 
the written material and interviews. Later 
the models were expanded through severôÙ
al iterations as more and more knowledge 
was gathered. Especially the UACs have gone 
through several iterations, and were shown 
to some of the interviewees for feedback. 
The causal loop diagrams were built with 
help from a ãGuidelines for system dynamôÙ
ics modelling of sustainability initiatives imôÙ
plementation v.3ã, and the creator of these 
guidelines as facilitator (Andersen, 2017). 
Even though the method itself seems simple, 
it was beneficial to use a systematic proceôÙ
dure to carry out the modelling work. The 
diagrams are all connected to each other 
through core variables, which are the most 
interesting to investigate deeper. 
 
The UAC models have both the function of 
summarising the acquired knowledge in an 
easy-to-understand-format for ourselves, 
and to function as a boundary object when 
discussing with interviewees. The causal 
loop diagrams have the function of showing 
how more aspects of the sanitation chalôÙ
lenge are interconnected, and of giving an 
overview of a complex problem.
This chapter is an analysis and overview of 
the sanitation and hygiene challenges in refôÙ
ugee camps. Our research has focused on 
unsheathing challenges in the archetypal 
camp described in chapter 1. An archetype 
is however an artificial construct. Therefore 
sources have been used, whose focus isnãt 
constrained to the archetype. The material 
has been selected and refined to create the 
best possible overview.
The research phase made it clear that not all 
challenges related to sanitation in a refugee 
camp can be understood from one perspecôÙ
tive. Depending on the source, there are ofôÙ
ten different views on eg. what is the most 
critical regarding sanitation. 
 
To ensure all angles are covered, and thereôÙ
by gain a more complete overview, we dividôÙ
ed the challenges into sub-perspectives. The 
three levels shown in Figure 21 are the result 
of this exercise.   
 
At the top of the figure we have the NGO 
level, which we define as the level associatôÙ
ed with setting up the entire refugee camp, 
running it and taking it down again. The NGO 
level consists of several clusters, which have 
responsibility of specific areas of the camp.
 
One of these areas is represented by the 
WASH management level, meaning the 
employees who, among other things, are 
responsible for the set-up, operation and 
maintenance of the sanitation facilities. 
 
The most narrow level is the personal level. 
It is concerned with the individual refugees 
living in the camp; their views on everyday 
life and their access to the sanitation facilôÙ
ities. 
 
These three levels make up the framework 
from which the challenges related to sanitaôÙ
tion will be explored. The levels will hence be 
analysed separately, with independent modôÙ
els.

The main point of concern at this level is 
to ensure that as many refugees as possible 
have their basic needs covered, as set out by 
the standards. The causal loop diagram at 
this level (Figure 24) (Appendix page 31) is 
a representation of NGOsã response mechaôÙ
nisms and capability to handle crises, which 
address the point of concern. The most imôÙ
portant loops illustrated are:
R1: Crises reinforce themselves. When there 
are many, they become more, as the time 
pressure rises and capability to handle them 
falls. 
R2: Short-term investments lead to quick reôÙ
lief, but reinforce problems in the long run. 
R3: Long-term investments slowly give reôÙ
lief, but reinforce the positive effect in the 
long run.
R4: Durability reinforces itself in the long 
run. When it is high, the amount of available 
facilities rises, leading to fewer people per 
facility, leading to higher durability (lower 
wear).
The main challenges at this level are: 
Challenge 1: Needs assessment
Challenge 2: Decision making
Challenge 3: Pressure from donors 
These challenges have been derived from 
the points of impact and loops, and will be 
elaborated in the following sections.
5.2.	Challenge Level 1: NGO level
The main sources for empirical knowledge 
supporting this level are the interviews with 
Lisbeth ElvekjûÎr (DRK), Kasper Bro Larsen 
(DRK), Christer LûÎnkholm (DCA), Paul Byars 
(IRC) and Stephanie Araya (volunteer), along 
with official toolbooks and standards on 
how to manage a camp (OIM et al., 2015; The 
Sphere Project, 2011). 
 
The main activity of the actors on this level is 
constructing, operating and deconstruction 
of the camp. This central activity is modelled 
in an UAC (Figure 23) (Appendix page 30). 
Breakdowns are indicated by lightning bolts. 
These negatively influence the cycle at three 
points of impact (POI), marked with numbers 
in the UAC:
POI 1: During the needs assessment, knowlôÙ
edge about the situation and statistics is ofôÙ
ten missing.
POI 2: When planning, knowledge about 
most effective layout of camp  is needed, but 
not present. Planning for both construction 
and deconstruction of camp is also difficult, 
due to missing knowledge.

POI 3: During operation, knowledge about 
essential activities in camp is missing.
