5.2.1.	 Challenge 1: Needs assessment
The pre-phase of the camp lifecycle has three 
breakdowns, at POI 1 and 2, which weaken 
the assessment of needs and the planning of 
the refugee camp. The breakdowns are all reôÙ
lated to needs for knowledge. Important deôÙ
cisions are made at this point, e.g. how large 
the camp needs to be, what type of shelters, 
which registration system, which participaôÙ
tion initiatives and which food distribution 
system will be used (IOM et al., 2015). RelatôÙ
ed to sanitation, the unknown factors are the 
needed number, type and location of latrines. 
The consequence of this lack of knowledge is 
often not immediately apparent, but appears 
once the refugee camp is up and running. 
ãThere is no end to the challenges. It is 
very difficult to assess the
 needs beforehand.
- Byars, 2017
One of the variables that is most at risk of 
incorrect assessment is the number of refuôÙ
gees arriving to the camp. This is influenced 
by large exogenous factors such as how many 
people might be fleeing from a drought, or 
a raging war (Byars, 2017; Araya, 2017). This 
means that determining the number of arôÙ
rivals to the camp is almost impossible to 
get correct without an extensive amount 
of research and data available. And even so, 
there is no guarantee that the estimate will 
be correct since the exogenous factors are 
extremely uncontrollable.
 
Similarly, there is a risk of inaccurately asôÙ
sessing how long the refugees will stay in the 
camp before moving on. The consequencôÙ
es of not assessing these needs accurately 
is both that the task of managing becomes 
harder for the camp management, and that 
the living conditions become worse for the 
refugees. If there is a great discrepancy beôÙ
tween what the camp was built for, and what 
it is actually being used for, it makes the opôÙ
eration and management of the camp much 
harder to execute (Byars, 2017; Araya, 2017).
5.2.2.	 Challenge 2: Decision making
The second highlighted challenge is in the 
operational phase, and is essentially the difôÙ
ficulty of making decisions when the camp is 
up and running. The decisions are essentialôÙ
ly centered around the dilemma of spending 
the right amount of money at the right activôÙ
ities at the right time. 
ãIt is about doing things quickly to 
save peopleãs lives and dignity. 
Speed is the ultimate goal.
- Byars, 2017
Picture 6.	 The extent of a refugee camp in Iraq. Source: IHH
==PAGE:71==
sanitation challenges I chapter 5 I section 2
57
5.2.3.	 Challenge 3: Pressure from 
donors
The lack of knowledge about essential activôÙ
ities in the camp in POI 3, includes lack of 
knowledge about exactly how money is beôÙ
ing spend. This means less to show donors in 
yearly reports and newsletters. HumanitariôÙ
an organisations feel an increasing pressure 
from donors to provide information on how 
efficiently they spend the money (LindenôÙ
berg, 2001; Besiou et al., 2011).
ãIt is a demand from the donors that 
their money is safe
- LûÎnkholm, 2017
 
The NGOs struggle with very difficult workôÙ
ing conditions under which they must make 
crucial decisions, as described in the chalôÙ
lenges above. It leaves a high risk of not 
spending the money most efficiently. The 
donors are aware of this risk, and battle it by 
setting up conditions. The distribution methôÙ
od is shifting, so that funds are now given for 
specific projects, and not for an organisation 
to distribute themselves. This puts a strain 
on the administrative costs and activities, 
which can easier be overlooked (LûÎnkholm, 
2017; Larsen, 2017). As an example the EuroôÙ
pean Commisionãs European Civil Protection 
and Humanitarian Aid Operations (ECHO) 
are demanding that a greater proportion of 
the funds is given directly to the beneficiaôÙ
ries (DRK, 2017; DRC, 2017).
Additionally, NGOs face high levels of corôÙ
ruption, which eat up the scarce funds. It is 
estimated by the Secretary-General of the 
UN that 30% of all funds for humanitariôÙ
an aid is lost to corruption [7]. The donorsã 
fear of inefficiency and corruption affect the 
NGOs, as they donãt get as many donations 
as they potentially could. As illustrated with 
R1,  a lower amount of donated funds leads 
directly to lower amount of available funds, 
and therefore to an increasingly worse capaôÙ
bility to handle crises. 
The causal loop diagram illustrates this diôÙ
lemma with the reinforcing loops R2, R3 
and R4 (see Figure 24). When the amount 
and severity of crises rise, more focus is put 
on short term investments (R2) which can 
quickly save peopleãs lives. This often means 
investing in less durable solutions. R4 tells 
us that less durable solutions in the long 
run exacerbate the problem. Therefore long 
term investments are also needed (R3). These 
however often only slowly cover the short 
term needs. The difficulty is in which ratio 
of long term versus short term investments 
to make, and when. Currently, decisions deôÙ
pend greatly on the employeesã experience, 
and subsequently knowledge sharing in each 
organisation. The employeesã knowledge is 
supported by standards, toolkits and codes 
of conduct. The knowledge sharing about 
experiences in the field, is however rarely 
optimal. It is not even certain that projects 
are evaluated in a way that is suitable for own 
or other employeesã learning (DRK, 2017). 
The problem is however not only the straôÙ
tegic decisions, it is also difficult to make 
everyday decisions, when knowledge about 
the essential activities in the camp is missing 
(POI 3). It can e.g. be difficult to know exactly 
what the camp population and demographôÙ
ics are, since people are free to come and 
go whenever they like. If more knowledge 
was available  at POI 3, it could affect R1, and 
heighten the capability of handling the crisis. 
The consequence of not making the optimal 
decisions (low knowledge) and getting the 
most out of the limited funds available (low 
available funds) is that it becomes harder for 
the camp management to take care of the 
many situations that constantly arise and 
need to be taken care of. Thereby the reinôÙ
forcing loop R1 is strengthened in a negative 
direction. This ultimately leads to the living 
conditions being worse for the refugees, and 
therefore even more crises to solve.

The main point of concern seen from the 
WASH Managementãs point of view is to miôÙ
nimise the number of health issues in the 
camp. This is illustrated by the causal loop 
diagram (Figure 27) (Appendix page 33). 
The loops are:
B1: The managementãs focus on WASH is balôÙ
anced. A reaction to the issues leads to a reôÙ
laxation period.
B2:  The camp population is ultimately balôÙ
anced, as a higher camp population leads to 
less motivation, meaning more ill people and 
subsequent deaths.
B3: The camp population is balanced, as 
a higher camp population leads to more 
ill people, worse access to healthcare and 
therefore more deaths.
B4: The quantity of latrines is balanced, due 
to the same effect as B1.
B5: The quality of latrines is balanced, due to 
the same effect as B1.
The main challenges at this level are: 
Challenge 1: Prioritisation
Challenge 2: Instability
Challenge 3: Motivation
Challenge 4: Collaboration (POI 1, 3, 4, 5)
These challenges have been derived from 
the points of impact and loops, and will be 
elaborated in the following sections.
5.3.	Challenge Level 2:
WASH management in camp
The main sources for empirical knowledge 
supporting this level are the interviews with 
Paul Byars (IRC), Stephanie Saavedra Araya 
(volunteer) and Katerina Polychroni (UNôÙ
HCR), along with official toolbooks and stanôÙ
dards on how to manage WASH in a camp 
(OIM et al., 2015; The Sphere Project, 2011).
 
The main activity of the actors is planning, 
building, maintaining and disposing of the 
WASH facilities in the camp. This central 
activity is modelled in an UAC (Figure 26)
(Appendix page 32). Breakdowns are inôÙ
dicated by lightning bolts. These negatively 
influence the cycle at five points of impact 
(POI), marked with numbers in the UAC:
POI 1: The needs assessment is hindered due 
to budget constraints and lack of knowledge 
and overview.
POI 2: When choosing a solution, budget 
constraints often dictate what is chosen.
POI 3: Installation is hindered by lacking 
overview and contradicting decisions.
POI 4: The daily operation is hindered by the 
refugeesã lack of motivation to use the faôÙ
cilities. The facilities often do not meet the 
required standards, and coordination on e.g. 
cleaning arrangements is difficult. 
POI 5: Participation from refugees is difficult 
when the camp population varies greatly, 
and the interest from refugees is low.

B4 and B5). The exact time for this point to 
occur varies from camp to camp, but often 
serious illness or death is needed before an 
intervention happens (Byars, 2017). At this 
point the WASH Management will intervene 
and thus decrease health issues and deaths. 
However, if the quality or quantity is not good 
enough, it will drop until the point where a 
new intervention is needed. The loops B2 
and B3, however unpleasant, tell us that the 
camp population is part of stabilising the sitôÙ
uation and that if the situation if bad enough 
for people to die, it will be part of relieving 
the pressure on the facilities.
5.3.2.	 Challenge 2: Instability
Many refugee camps struggle with an unstaôÙ
ble or unknown camp population, as seen in 
POI 5 (Byars, 2017; LûÎnkholm, 2017; Larsen, 
2017; Araya, 2017; IOM et al., 2015). Crises 
worsen the fluctuations in camp population. 
The Arabic Spring e.g. contributed to very 
unstable arrival rates (UNHRC, 2016). The 
problem with camp populations is however 
widely known, and a worldwide issue (Byars, 
2017; Polychroni, 2017). 
ãOne day there might be 150 people arôÙ
riving, another day 0. Last summer there 
were 3500 refugees, now there are 400.
- Araya, 2017
5.3.1.	 Challenge 1: Prioritisation
The UACs for WASH management level and 
NGO level overlap; when assessing the needs 
for the whole camp, it makes sense to also 
assess the needs related to sanitation. HowôÙ
ever, often the planning and construction 
of proper and hygienic sanitation facilities 
is down prioritised and postponed to a latôÙ
er stage in the life cycle of the camp (POI 
1). When handling an emergency situation, 
the most basic needs such as food, shelter 
and security are prioritised higher by both 
refugees and the camp management (Byars, 
2017). Temporary sanitation solutions such 
as pit holes are accepted, since they covôÙ
er the most immediate needs, and are very 
budget friendly solutions (POI 2).
 
ãWASH is usually ignored in the first
phases of an emergency situation,
people care more about danger and
violence
- Byars, 2017
However, bad sanitation facilities lead to 
bad hygiene and appertaining health issues, 
which ultimately results in deaths among the 
refugees. When the health issues have come 
to a point where they cannot be ignored, the 
management turns their focus to sanitation 
and an intervention will take place (loops B1, 
Picture 7.	 Refugees arriving to a camp. Source: IHH
==PAGE:75==
sanitation challenges I chapter 5 I section 2
61
The WASH management has a responsibility 
to keep the hygiene in the camp good and 
the number of ill people down, and thereôÙ
fore use interventions (B1, B4 and B5) to try 
to avoid the problems. They might also try 
participation or campaigns to educate the 
camp population about the consequences of 
bad hygiene. 
5.3.4.	 Challenge 4: Collaboration
As many NGOs can be involved in WASH 
Management at the same time, they very ofôÙ
ten have to collaborate. This is a challenge 
since the employees from each NGO have 
different backgrounds and ways of workôÙ
ing, but may also have organisations behind 
them with very different goals (Byars, 2017). 
Knowledge sharing between the NGOs can 
be the key to solving some of the issues in 
POI 1-4. 
Even though the average time a refugee is upôÙ
rooted is 17 years [8], the refugees often exôÙ
pect the camp to be a very temporary home. 
Some refugees also view not only their own 
affiliation with the camp as temporary, but 
also the entire camp. They believe the camp 
will only exist for 2-3 weeks, when in reality 
it is 6-12 months or even more (Araya, 2017; 
Byars, 2017). This creates a transition menôÙ
tality which entails a behavior where a perôÙ
son is not interested in taking care of their 
surroundings, since they feel no ownership 
or responsibility for them. This is worsened 
when the conditions upon arrival are already 
in a bad state. It can be related to how trash 
in the streets often seems to reproduce itôÙ
self, as more people are keen to throw their 
waste where there already is some, or the 
mentality when people attend a festival and 
donãt care about their surroundings because 
they are only staying for a week [54]. 
The mentality is common in many camps, 
and follows the unstable camp population, 
making it hard to run e.g. campaigns encourôÙ
aging certain behavior (POI 4), or to create 
well functioning structures with committees 
and participation (POI 5) (Byars, 2017). The 
instability affects the possibilities for making 
a good needs assessment (POI 1) and thereôÙ
fore living up to the standards in the operaôÙ
tional phase (POI 4).
5.3.3.	 Challenge 3: Motivation
One of the main reasons that refugees are 
not motivated to use the sanitation facilities 
is the transition mentality (POI 4), but there 
are several other reasons, which will be exôÙ
plored in the next challenge level. The causôÙ
al loop diagram shows that the direct conôÙ
sequence of a lack of motivation is more ill 
people. 
ãOften there is human waste lying 
around near the homes, or even where 
the kids are playing
- Araya, 2017
Picture 8.	 There are many stakeholders 
in a refugee camp. Source: IHH
But it is a challenge to get the infrastructure 
for knowledge sharing going while managôÙ
ing all the other tasks. The organisations do 
often not even have well functioning knowlôÙ
edge sharing procedures internally (DRK, 
2017). Evaluation reports are often not made 
due to scarce resources, and lack of data, so 
the knowledge is lost. The difficulty of workôÙ
ing together is increased due to the presôÙ
sures with regards to time and budget. It is 
a contributing factor to making the wrong 
decisions during operation of the camp, and 
thus also to the increased pressure from doôÙ
nors since money is seemingly wasted. 
==PAGE:76==
sanitation challenges I chapter 5 I section 2
62
5.4.	Challenge Level 3: 
Personal behaviour and needs
The main sources for empirical knowledge 
supporting this level are interviews with Paul 
Byars (IRC), Katerina Polychroni (UNHCR) 
and Stephanie Saavedra Araya (volunteer), 
along with official toolbooks and standards 
on how to manage WASH in a camp (OIM et 
al., 2015; The Sphere Project, 2011).
 
The main activity for the actors on this level 
is to keep themselves and their families alive. 
The refugeesã main concern is obtaining a 
reasonably good life standard and secure 
their future. In this analysis the central acôÙ
tivity is the usage of a latrine, which is a part 
of covering their basic needs. The activity is 
modelled in an UAC (Figure 29) (Appendix 
page 34). Breakdowns are indicated by 
lightning bolts. These negatively influence 
the cycle at eight points of impact (POI), 
marked with numbers in the UAC:
POI 1: It is often decided not to use the laôÙ
trines due to bad experiences and low motiôÙ
vation. This is where the cycle often breaks.
POI 2, 3 and 8 : The walk to and from the laôÙ
trine is hindered due to safety concerns and 
distance.
POI 4 and 6: Entering and exiting may be unôÙ
safe and not private enough.
POI 5: The latrines themselves might be unôÙ
clean, smelly and not safe enough.
POI 7: Sinks may be altered, making it imposôÙ
sible to wash hands.
The main point of concern from the refuôÙ
geesã point of view is deciding whether to 
use the latrine or not. The factors influencôÙ
ing this choice are illustrated by the causôÙ
al loop diagram (Figure 30) (Appendix page 
35). The loops are:
R1: The lack of motivation for using the 
shared facilities is reinforced by a desire for 
access to own facilities.
R2: The perceived ease of open defecation is 
reinforced by a lack of motivation.
B1: The lack of motivation is balanced when 
the consequences get bad enough to be reôÙ
alised.
The main challenges at this level are: 
Challenge 1: Cleanness 
Challenge 2: Safety

Challenge 5: Ownership

5.4.1.	 Challenge 1: Cleanness
The cleanliness of the facilities are an issue 
and discussion point in refugee camps. The 
issue arises from the structural design of the 
latrines, but also the cleaning. Even though 
many camps have dedicated cleaning comôÙ
panies, sometimes cleaning the toilets up to 
three times a day, many refugees still report 
the toilets as unclean and smelling badly 
(Araya, 2017) (POI 5). The level of cleanliness 
can however be debated, as it is not an absoôÙ
lute measure. Different people and cultures 
have different standards (Polychroni, 2017). 
One factor contributing to the issue is that 
the WASH-management is often not the one 
responsible for the cleaning. This means that 
even though the WASH management reôÙ
ceives the complaints and bears the responôÙ
sibility of ensuring adequate facilities, they 
do not have the power to adjust the cleaning 
procedures. 
ãThe toilets are not always clean, but 
people also have different perceptions 
about what is clean, so 
itãs not so easy to tell
- Polychroni, 2017
Which cleaning remedies must be available 
is also a debating point, including both hand 
washing and cleaning of oneself (POI 7). One 
interviewee points out that even though this 
issue might seem luxurious, people are very 
concerned with it. They might have comôÙ
pletely different toilet habits and regard 
other ways as unhygienic. Many refugees 
in Greece are for example used to ãTurkishã 
toilets, which are used while squatting, and 
usually have water to clean oneself with afôÙ
terwards. The practice of using toilet paper 
can seem unnatural or unhygienic compared 
to this.
5.4.2.	 Challenge 2: Safety
Women and children in particular do not feel 
safe when going to use or using the sanitaôÙ
tion facilities (Polychroni, 2017). This is an isôÙ
sue that is present at many points in the UAC 
(POI 2-6 and POI 8). It is especially a problem 
during night-time, when some camps have 
scarce or no lighting. Instead some women 
might wake up their partner in order to esôÙ
cort them to the facilities, or use makeshift 
solutions such as buckets, a hole in the house 
or simply squat behind a bush, avoiding the 
often long walk to the latrines. It is with good 
Picture 9.	 Women are often victims of assault. Source: IHH

65
reason that children and women fear these 
trips, as gender-based violence and abuse 
towards children are widespread problems. 
Save the Children estimate that the scale of 
abuse towards children is significant, unôÙ
der-reported and that it is a global problem 
in any emergency (CsûÀky, 2008).
ãWomen are afraid to use the toilets 
during the night because there are 
security incidents
- Polychroni, 2017
The Camp Management Toolkit (IOM et al., 
2015) also addresses the issue and specificalôÙ
ly states that unlit and non-lockable latrines 
should be avoided. Moreover the camp layôÙ
out, which sometimes forces women to walk 
a long distance to latrines, is pointed out as a 
large risk factor. The issue is a question of reôÙ
sources, but can also be a dilemma, as placôÙ
ing guards and protective personnel has the 
risk of worsening the situation, since guards 
are sometimes pointed out as the source of 
assault as well (CsûÀky, 2008). 
5.4.3.	 Challenge 3: Privacy
Beside the fear and risk of being assaulted, 
there is also an issue of privacy and dignity 
while using the facilities (POI 4 and POI 6). 
ãSome of the cultures donãt accept having 
a menãs toilet close to the womenãs toilet. 
You shouldnãt be able to see the women go 
when nature calls
- Polychroni, 2017
The measures to ensure privacy and gender 
division are often not good enough compared 
to common cultural and religious practices. 
Some cultures e.g. donãt accept having the 
menãs latrine very close to the womenãs or 
sometimes require shielding of a part of the 
path to the womenãs latrines. Even the more 
established facilities have their issues, with 
e.g. no private space for changing clothes 
before showering or insufficient shielding 
from the opposite sex, making it possible to 
see or enter the area easily. The pre-fabricatôÙ
ed toilet facilities which are often bought by 
NGOs rarely take these issues into account, 
even though they are part of the checklists 
for good practices (UNHCR, 2015c).
5.4.4.	 Challenge 4: Distance
The distance to the latrines is a great issue, 
both due to comfort and safety (POI 2 and 
POI 8). Camp layout is therefore of great imôÙ
portance. The issues of privacy and safety 
furthermore contribute to this challenge. 
Female-headed households arriving after 
the establishment of the camp might be 
forced to settle at the outskirts, leaving them 
exposed to assault (IOM et al., 2015). 
ãThere are maybe 50-100 meters from 
most of the containers to the toilets
- Araya, 2017
Moreover women with many children have a 
lot of work with bathing and generally taking 
care of their childrenãs hygiene, which makes 
the long distance from the home problematôÙ
ic. As with most other factors at this level it 
can be discussed what ãtoo far awayã means. 
In the camp in Nea Kavala in Greece, the resôÙ
idents furthest from the facilities have 120 
meters walking distance (Araya, 2017). Many 
of the families live at this distance, since the 
largest residential facilities are located there, 
as well as the public playground. The refuôÙ
gees donãt always have a choice as to where 
they will stay, but if they do, they might be 
forced to make these difficult trade-offs beôÙ
tween which facilities it is important to be 
close to or whether they should stay close to 
friends and relatives. 
 
==PAGE:80==
sanitation challenges I chapter 5 I section 2
66
5.4.5.	 Challenge 5: Ownership
NGOs strive to ensure that each household 
has an individual latrine (IOM et al., 2015). 
This is however rarely fulfilled, sometimes 
because the refugees arenãt interested, but 
often due to lack of resources. People who 
are used to having their own private latrine 
at home are often very eager to get one in 
the camp as well (Byars, 2017). This can result 
in a reinforcing effect where the motivation 
to use the common facilities drops, resulting 
in sometimes very creative homemade soluôÙ
tions (POI 7). 
ãIf people are used to having a private 
toilet, and they know they will be in the 
camp for a long period, they are usually 
very eager to get their own
- Byars, 2017
In some camps people report having their 
own latrine is the number one thing they are 
missing (Araya, 2017), and would choose a 
private latrine over most other services. It is 
common that the shared facilities are altered 
in order to provide alternative facilities closôÙ
er to the home. In Nea Kavala refugee camp 
(Araya, 2017) the public water taps were alôÙ
tered,  and had hoses connected to them, 
which were led to a small enclosure next to 
some of the homes. These enclosures funcôÙ
tioned as private shower facilities and someôÙ
times even toilet facilities for the children. 
This homemade hose-system however didnãt 
provide solutions for plumbing, meaning all 
the water ran out into the street and thus 
posed a potential health threat.
The private latrines must be of some stanôÙ
dard before being adopted as a good soluôÙ
tion. In one camp new residential containôÙ
ers had private toilets directly connected to 
the living room. The common toilets were 
locked, as there supposedly was no need for 
those anymore. But unfortunately it turned 
out the toilets in the residential containers 
leaked water into the living room, meaning 
the room would smell foully and water would 
drench the things inside. In this case people 
broke the locks to be able to use the comôÙ
mon latrines. 
5.4.6.	 Challenge 6: Knowledge
Knowledge and cultural habits can also afôÙ
fect the motivation greatly. Refugees might 
come from low density populated areas, 
where they are not used to living many peoôÙ
ple close together. These refugees thus have 
little understanding for the health issues 
that can arise in densely populated areas, 
if sanitation facilities are not used correctôÙ
ly and hygiene is not made a high priority 
(Byars, 2017). The refugees can be surprised 
that they have to change the habits they are 
used to, since they did not have any hygiene 
issues when e.g. defecating out in the open 
at home (Byars, 2017). 
ãThe refugees normally donãt know the 
consequences of being many people in 
one place, since many have never been 
in a camp before, and are used to live in 
smaller villages
- Byars, 2017
The cultural habit heightens the perceived 
ease of open defecation and can make the 
reinforcing loop R2 spin out of control. If it 
does so however, it might become balanced 
by the loop B1, which states that people will 
learn that open defecation is harmful when 
the consequences become bad enough.

how the WASH management experiences 
the refugeesã motivation. And the prioritisaôÙ

on the NGO level. This is particularly conôÙ

Please note that some of the case studies will 
not fit 100 % with the refugee context and the 
archetype camp. Several of the case studies 
are from developing countries instead. We 
believe that many parallels can still be drawn 
to the refugee camp context, even though 
the ãtranslationã has to be done carefully and 
take the differences into account. 
6.1.1.	
The Case Study Method
The essence of a case study is to try to illuôÙ
minate a decision or a set of decisions; why 
they were taken, how they were implementôÙ
ed, and with what result (Schramm, 1971). 
The optimal way to obtain knowledge is to 
analyse a large sample of data points regardôÙ
ing a problem in question. It is however not 
always possible to obtain this large sample 
population, as it is the case in the work of 
this thesis. Therefore it is beneficial to apply 
case studies to the empirical data collected, 
since a case study makes it possible to proôÙ
vide an in-depth explanation of the social 
and behavioural problems in question, withôÙ
out this large sample population (Yin, 2003). 
Application and Usage
The main purpose for using case studies in 
this thesis is to disseminate the collected 
empirical knowledge in an analytically sound 
and structured way, summarising the imôÙ
portant points of multiple cases. Since the 
case studies are conducted based on colôÙ
lected data, and no interventions, they are 
descriptive case studies. It is furthermore 
a qualitative kind of case studies, since few 
cases are analysed in depth instead of comôÙ
paring multiple cases (Zainal, 2007).  
 
One of the primary sources of knowledge in 
these case studies are the earlier mentioned 
expert interviews performed with a broad 
variety of experienced representatives from 
the field of humanitarian work. Expert interôÙ
views is one of the most important sources 
of evidence and of qualitative data collection 
in case studies (Yin, 2003). 
6.1.	Introduction to Case Studies
The challenges described in the previous 
chapter are not novel news within the huôÙ
manitarian sector. Many organisations all 
over the world are working on these chalôÙ
lenges and other related issues. The chalôÙ
lenges are certainly not limited to refugee 
camps either, and the range of organisations 
working on some aspect of the challenges is 
therefore very wide. 
 
In order to learn from previous and existing 
initiatives which address part of our problem 
statement, we perform a series of case studôÙ
ies to explore the solution space and underôÙ
stand what has been done and with which 
results and learnings. We believe the solution 
to the challenges can be found by combining 
three themes; Sanitation and Payment, RefuôÙ
gee Finances and High Tech Low Cost in RefôÙ
ugee Camps. The three themes are illustrated 
in Figure 33.
The hypothesis for these case studies is that 
each theme is quite well-functioning on its 
own. If each theme is working well, we claim 
that there is reason to believe that the comôÙ
bination of all three will also have a good reôÙ
sult, provided that the learnings and issues 
found in the case studies are considered. 
The following case studies and analysis will 
determine these learnings and issues.

Pitfalls
Case studies are criticised for lack of rigor, 
and for being difficult to draw a general conôÙ
clusion from, since only one case is being 
analysed in depth. It is however not the point 
of the case studies conducted in this thesis 
to draw statistical conclusions, but rather 
analytical conclusions (Yin, 2003). It is hence 
believed that case studies will still provide 
great value of knowledge for this thesis. 
 
Another pitfall when conducting case studôÙ
ies is to be biased before performing the anôÙ
alytical work, and thus potentially distort the 
outcome and conclusions. There is no sound 
method to avoiding this other than to have it 
in mind when conducting the case studies, 
and analyse everything with as little prejuôÙ
dice as possible.
6.1.2.	
Search Methods 
The case studies were found from searches 
focusing on each category individually, tryôÙ
ing to get as broad a variety of cases as posôÙ
sible. The starting point for each search was 
typically a case mentioned by an interviewee. 
Each case was researched online, which reôÙ
sulted in more cases emerging. Semi-strucôÙ
tured searches using the keywords for each 
theme were performed, but with less useful

A quick presentation of this case can be seen

Sanitation problems are largely present 
in the slums and rural areas of developing 
countries, such as Kenya and India. In these 
types of informal settlements, the current 
sanitation solutions are both undignifying 
and cause immense environmental damage 
[10]. Sanergy decided to address this probôÙ
lem, and started their work in 2010.
 
It was decided to try to solve the sanitation 
crisis with a system-based approach. The 
solution needed to be durable and sustainôÙ
able, and pays regard to the whole sanitation 
value chain meaning handling the waste all 
the way from excretion to recycling.
 
The motivation for having local Fresh Life 
Toilets (FLTs) was both to improve the genôÙ
eral health and comfort of the population 
living nearby, and to create a motivation for 
certain activities to happen by advertising 
with proper sanitary conditions, e.g. getting 
kids to school. The purpose of making the 
FLTs was not only to ãdonate aidã to the peoôÙ
ple in need, but rather to make people micro 
entrepreneurs by selling the toilets to them, 
and thus making them Fresh Life Operators 
(FLOs). This meant that both the sanitation 
crisis and the local economy was improved 
with the same initiative. 
Sanergy sells a prefabricated, low-cost, and 
high-quality toilet facility to locals who are 
interested in improving the sanitary conôÙ
ditions in their local community, and at the 
same time make a livelihood out of running 
the toilet facility by being a FLO. Sanergy 
builds the toilet, and the FLO then maintains 
the toilet, and charges everyone who wants 
to use it a small fee. The payment can vary 
from an online solution to just a coin in hand. 
The FLO will be able to repay the price for 
the toilet and start to make profit for themôÙ
selves within approximately nine months 
[11]. Sanergy furthermore collects the waste 
deposited in the FLT every day, and makes 
sure the waste is transported and disposed 
of or recycled properly, e.g. turned into ferôÙ
tiliser. The FLO pays Sanergy for this service.
 
Today there are more than 1000 working 
FLTs placed in informal settlements. This 
also means Sanergy has created over 900 
jobs, since each toilet is a small business in 
itself. Paying to use a proper toilet is comôÙ
mon in many parts of the world, especially 
in India [12].

In rural Africa, it is a challenge to ensure acôÙ

Grundfos wanted to create the missing link

believed was related to lack of money and 
capacity for operation and maintenance. 
Grundfos wanted to address the problem in 
a way that broke with conventional thinkôÙ

in the long term. The critical question for 
Grundfos was whether people in developing 
countries would be willing and able to pay 
for water, and if so, whether the payments 
were enough to cover the expenses of having 
a high quality and reliable water system.
 
Grundfos used their expertise in pump techôÙ
nology together with revenue collection and 
water management to create LifeLink. It 
is as mentioned a water ATM, where water 
can be collected through a dispenser unit 
called AQtap, and payed for using a smart 
card called WaterCards. This is supported by 
a water management system which collects 
transactions and data, as well as pumping 
and water treatment stations to ensure waôÙ

To conclude; a sanitation facility which takes 
payment is a reliable business, once it is up 
and running. The challenges are high initial 
costs, as well as the users having limited acôÙ

6.2.4.	 Conclusion on Sanitation and 
Payment
This chapter sought to answer the question:
How does payment for sanitation services 
function now, and why is it successful/unôÙ
successful? 
It was found that sanitation for payôÙ
ment-solutions today function in a way 
where the sanitation unit itself is financialôÙ
ly sustainable. Even though the initial inôÙ
stallation of the sanitation unit might be 
an expense, it is supporting its own daily 
operation and maintenance. This is possiôÙ
ble mainly due to the fact that even though 
people in these low sanitation regions have 
limited funds, they are still willing to pay for 
using high quality sanitation facilities. Since 
access to sanitation is such a basic human 
need, people are inclined to accept a new 
solution, once offered. An important prereqôÙ
uisite for this to succeed is however that the 
sanitation solution is developed in collaboraôÙ
tion with the people who are intended to use 
it, so it fits their needs and customs. 
 
The sanitation for payment-solutions have 
the common characteristic of having one or 
more sanitation facilities collected in one loôÙ
cation. This way of making a sanitation acôÙ
tion something ãyou leave the home to doã 
with the promise of high quality once you 
get there also seems to be a factor of sucôÙ
cess. Even though people might prefer to 
have their own sanitation facilities close by, 
it seems that the high quality is enough comôÙ
pensation. The cases also show that the sanôÙ
itation facilities function with both old-fashôÙ
ioned payment methods (such as the option 
to pay with a coin at the Fresh Life Toilets) 
and technology based payment methods 
(such as the WaterCards at the water ATMs). 
They also function when being managed by 
both local individuals or by larger organisaôÙ
tions. 
 
The barrier for the solutions not being even 
more widespread is most likely that the soluôÙ
tions come with a relatively high initial cost.

million connections [23]. Kenya is even more 
developed, with up to 90% of the population 
owning a mobile-phone [26]. Beside the acôÙ
cess to cellphones, M-Pesaãs was successful 
due to the way it launched the service. The 
marketing was clear, focusing on one mesôÙ

points in Kenya, and entailed massive trainôÙ

able to explain the service to customers [21]. 
This way M-Pesa could quickly build up the 
volume needed to benefit from network efôÙ
fects; the more subscribers the service had, 
the more it made sense for the individual to 
sign up. However, the service is still heavily 
dependent on cash. Money must be cashed-
in and out at agentsã stores, who also take 
a commission. Ensuring the liquidity (e.g. 
enough cash at the cash-out points) requires 
a massive system, in which middlemen ferry 
around the cash between the stores [25].
M-Pesa now has lots of competition, with 
about 260 different Mobile Money services 
around the world. They are most popular in 
Africa, Asia and Latin America and accessible 
in more than 60% of the developing markets 
[18]. Moreover, M-Pesa was quickly after its 
launch used for distributing cash-based aid, 
and mobile money has since been adopted 
by many humanitarian NGOs [22] [19].
A quick presentation of this case can be seen 
in Appendix page 43.
M-Pesa is a mobile money transfer serôÙ
vice developed in 2007. It uses basic mobile 
phones or an app to enable users to secureôÙ
ly send, receive and store money. The serôÙ
vice was formed when it was noticed that 
Kenyans used to transfer mobile airtime as 
a proxy for money [18]. In Kenya, access to 
conventional financial services is very limitôÙ
ed and only a small portion of the population 
have a bank account. Transporting cash is 
also dangerous and slow, with a high risk of 
street robbery and corruption [19] [20].
 
M-Pesa was launched by Vodafone for SafaôÙ
ricom and Vodacom, the largest mobile netôÙ
work operators in Kenya and Tanzania [21]. It 
was quickly adapted by the population, and 
in 2015 more than two-thirds of the adult 
population used the service. Mobile-monôÙ
ey have a significant impact on Kenyaãs GDP 
as up to 51% is estimated to flow through 
the network [22].  M-Pesaãs success can be 
ascribed to several reasons,. The most imôÙ
portant is the explosive growth in access to 
cellphones. According to GSMA, at the end 
of 2015 there were over half a billion mobile 
subscribers in Africa, equivalent to 46% of 
the population. Moreover access to smartôÙ
phones is rapidly growing, with over 226

which is one of UNHCR goals. Such aid is also 
called multi-purpose grants (MPGs) [28]. The 
refugees were provided with a bank account, 
with an ATM card and PIN code, which gave 
them access to a monthly allowance of about 
$210.
 
This kind of aid not only gave refugees a 
more dignified life, but also saved a lot of 
overhead costs and prevented corruption.

6.3.5.	 Conclusion on Refugee 
Finances 
This chapter sought to answer the question:
How do refugees receive, use and exchange 
money and why is it successful/unsuccessful? 
It is common for refugees to receive cash-
based aid from different humanitarian orôÙ
ganisations. This form of aid is preferred by 
both organisations and refugees. The orôÙ
ganisations emphasise the greater dignity 
of choice for refugees and the positive efôÙ
fect the local community often experiencôÙ
es. Moreover cash-based aid can help fight 
corruption, as the possibilities for digitising 
are better. The main challenge is however 
that it can be more vulnerable to exploitaôÙ
tion of the system as cash can be harder to 
control. This challenge is often dismissed by 
organisations, but donors often fear abuse, 
meaning it must be addressed. Cash-based 
aid is therefore often given with some kind 
of conditionality. It is almost always distribôÙ
uted based on vulnerability criteria to secure 
the most vulnerable part of the population, 
but additional criteria such as sending kids 
to school etc. can be made. To ensure the 
money reaches the right person, various disôÙ
tribution channels are used. 
 
Cash-based aid and more private monôÙ
ey transactions between refugees happen 
through a multitude of channels. Mobile 
money, such as the M-Pesa system, has beôÙ
come increasingly popular in the developing 
countries. Digital systems have the advanôÙ
tage of being quick, secure and mobile, all 
factors which are essential for refugees and 
humanitarian organisations.
 
Beside cash-based aid, money can also be 
privately moved around by using e.g. moôÙ
bile-money, Hawala or Western Union. 
Non-digital methods of distributing cash-
based aid include plain cash, which can be 
handed out in envelopes, or different kinds 
of vouchers. Closed-loop money systems 
are also an option, which ensures the money 
stays within the system which could include 
only one specific camp. 
To conclude; refugees increasingly receive 
cash-based aid, in many forms. It can be conôÙ
ditional or unconditional, physical or digital, 
and plain cash or part of a closed loop monôÙ
ey system. All cash-based aid provides more 
dignity to refugees than conventional aid. 
The important factors are security, flexibility 
and quick distribution.

case studies I chapter 6 I section 2
A quick presentation of this case can be seen 
in Appendix page 49.
In 2008 UNHCR started giving cash-based 
aid in Jordan. The refugees were providôÙ
ed with a bank account, with an ATM card 
and a PIN code. With this the refugees could 
withdraw their allowance each month. UNôÙ
HCR however regularly had to perform presôÙ
ence-checks to make sure that only eligible 
refugees received the aid and the card had 
not e.g. been handed on to another famiôÙ
ly. It was therefore desired to find a system 
which performed more securely and guaranôÙ
teed that the limited resources only reached 
those eligible. 
 
With the introduction of the biometric techôÙ
nology in 2012 the presence checks became 
obsolete, as well as the problems with lost 
ATM cards or forgotten PIN codes. In the 
new system the iris is scanned upon regisôÙ
tration, and is afterwards used directly at 
the bank and ATMs to retrieve the aid. UNôÙ
HCR partnered with Cairo Amman Bank and 
IrisGuard to set up the system. The bank inôÙ
stalled the technology in 85 branches and 
200 ATMs. This new system ensured money 
could be distributed faster and with greatôÙ
er security. This way the scarce resources 
could be redirected for other purposes [27].
As far as we are aware, this is the only place 
in the world that uses this system in a refuôÙ
gee camp. Iris scanners are however becomôÙ
ing popular and might soon become a stanôÙ
dard smartphone feature [36].

In December 2015 a tech start-up company 
called AidTech tested the new technology 
blockchain in a humanitarian context. The 
company collaborated with the Irish Red 
Cross and Lebanese humanitarian experts, 
to carry out two tests in Aker Refugee Camp 
and the city of Tripoli. The objective was to 
distribute cash-based aid using the blockôÙ
chain technology, and thereby ensuring a 
secure dignifying way of distributing aid to 
refugees.
First a survey was carried out by six LebaôÙ
nese academics. AidTech then partnered 
with the humanitarian organisations to disôÙ
tribute $10.000 from private sponsors to 100 
Syrian families using 500 intelligent vouchôÙ
ers, which primarily functioned by scanning 
a QR code printed on them. A local superôÙ
market agreed to train their cashiers to use 
the system, and let refugees buy groceries 
using the vouchers. All 500 vouchers were 
redeemed with great satisfaction for the refôÙ
ugees. The transactions could be monitored 
by the Red Cross in real time. 20 fraudulent 
vouchers were created, which all failed at 
the point of sale [37]. 
Blockchain has had very few actual use casôÙ
es, beside Bitcoin, and this case is completely 
unique as it is applied in a humanitarian setôÙ
ting.

Internet of Things (IoT) is the term coverôÙ
ing computing devices embedded in everyôÙ
day objects, that have the ability to send and 
receive data via a connection, and is further 
explained in Chapter 7. This technology is 
often associated with smart cars or intelôÙ
ligent houses which can be controlled via 
your mobile phone. In the HTLC humanitarôÙ
ian context, IoT is mostly the use of sensor 
equipment connected to monitoring devices 
with the purpose of collecting data about an 
issue of concern. 
 
An example of an application of IoT is monôÙ
itoring the functionality of hand pumps in 
Kenya. There was a problem of the pumps 
breaking down and that it took very long 
before it was repaired due to weak commuôÙ
nication channels between the people using 
the pump and the people repairing it. By inôÙ
stalling a simple accelerometer in the pump 
handle to capture the movement when it 
was being used, and transmitting this data 
via SMS, it was possible to see when a pump 
was not functioning anymore, since the hanôÙ
dle had stopped moving. This resulted in a 
ten-fold reduction of the downtime of a 
dysfunctioning pump, and the maintenance 
work could therefore be optimised (Biggs et 
al., 2016). 
 
Another example is monitoring the frequenôÙ
cy of latrine use and handwashing in a hyôÙ
giene promotion project in Indonesia. Water 
flow sensors and motion detectors were inôÙ
stalled in the toilet facilities, to give a meaôÙ
sure for evaluating how effective the hygiene 
promotion programs were. The evaluation 
was based both on the data from the senôÙ
sors, and  on a survey made among the parôÙ
ticipants. The project found that the survey 
indicated a much higher percentage of peoôÙ
ple washing hands after toilet visits, than the 
data showed (Biggs et al., 2016). 
 
These two examples show that IoT has poôÙ
tential to work for everyday use as a moniôÙ
toring system, and for research purposes as 
a supporting or leading source of data. It is 
however also relatively expensive to put up, 
as it requires some sort of investment. FurôÙ
thermore, the durability and reliability of the 
system has to be carefully considered, espeôÙ
cially if the system is to be used for someôÙ
thing as important as making sure villages 
has access to clean water.

6.4.4.	 Conclusion on HTLC in Refugee 
Camps 
This chapter sought to answer the question:
How are high-tech solutions currently used in 
refugee camps and why are they successful/
unsuccessful? 
It was found that the development of techôÙ
nology used in refugee camps and other 
developing regions is not lagging as much 
behind as expected, compared to developed 
countries. Technology is not down-prioriôÙ
tised in favour of more ãclassic development 
activitiesã, such as building accommodations 
and create livelihoods. The reason for this 
seems to be not the excitement and novelty 
of using technology, but simply having more 
safe and durable solutions, which enable a 
better relief assistance and potentially saves 
lives. A technology based solution often has a 
potential for a good economy of scale, meanôÙ
ing that more people can eventually receive 
help with the same resources available. 
 
However, the use of technology comes with 
a set of challenges as well. Even though the 
term is called high-tech low-cost, the investôÙ
ment in technological solutions is often a relôÙ
atively high cost for organisations with limôÙ
ited funds. Although there might be a good 
return in the long run, it is often a challenge 
to find the funds to invest here and now. The 
durability and reliability of these technologiôÙ
cal systems are in need of improvement. The 
connectivity in the relevant areas is not alôÙ
ways present, just like in the sanitation and 
payment cases. 
 
To conclude; the use of HTLC is interesting 
due to the increased efficiency and scalabiliôÙ
ty once implemented. It ensures a better reôÙ
lief assistance. It however faces challenges of 
being costly, as well as unreliable if located in 
areas with bad connectivity.

A much discussed technological subject 
during the last couple of years is blockchain. 
Blockchain is a fundamentally new way of 
handling trust during transaction of anything 
valuable, and it has been foreseen to have as 
large an impact on the financial system, as 
the internet had on media [38]. Since the exôÙ
tent of blockchain is so large, this chapter

7.1.2.	 What is Blockchain?
The example above showed that there is 
definitely room for improvement when hanôÙ
dling exchange of value, transactions and 
long chains of transportation with shifting 
responsibility. This improvement might be 
present in blockchain technology. 
 
The following paragraph is inspired and 
quoted by the TED Talk from 2016 by Bettina 
Warburg: ãHow the blockchain will radically 
transform the economyã [40]. 
 
Bettina Warburg, a blockchain researchôÙ
er, entrepreneur and educator, states that 
blockchain is the next big step in the evoôÙ
lution of how we handle commerce and fiôÙ
full size, with more explanations in Appendix 
page 52. The process proceeds as follows:
 
The insulin is produced in Denmark, then 
loaded on a cooling container that has a 
thermometer on the inside and a display 
showing the temperature on the outside. 
The container is transported by truck to a 
harbour, and changes hands from the driver 
to the captain via a ãletter of responsibilityã. 
Afterwards the container is loaded on a ship, 
which sails all the way to India. On board the 
ship, the captain has to fill out an error reôÙ
port if anything happens to the insulin, e.g. 
regarding the temperature limits. Upon arôÙ
riving in India, the container is loaded on anôÙ
other truck, which transports it to the clinic. 
The clinician has the insulin in stock and disôÙ
tributes it to patients. 
 
Sometimes the insulin does not have the 
desired effect in the end, potentially injurôÙ
ing or killing patients. If this happens, it beôÙ
comes apparent that during this transporôÙ
tation process, there are many possibilities 
for where in the chain the insulin could be 
damaged. 
 
A few possibilities are highlighted with exôÙ
clamation marks in the figure. The first one 
is the insulin being produced incorrectly 
from the factory due to an error in e.g. qualôÙ
ity control or a system failure meaning the 
insulin does not meet the standards for proôÙ
duction. The source of damage could also 
be an accident during the truck driving, e.g. 
due to a faulty container or simply the driver 
not knowing the strict demands for the temôÙ
perature of the insulin. Another potential 
source of damage is if the captain does not 
file an error report correctly, due to a simôÙ
ple human error or deliberate fraud to avoid 
expenses for compensation. Finally, there is 
also the possibility that the clinic does not 
store the insulin as prescribed, or hands it 
out to patients without the required guidôÙ
ance. 
Regardless of what caused the insulin to be 
damaged, there is one general issue: it is very

people. Everyone has a copy of the folder and

Every time anyone in the group uploads, deôÙ

completely visible for anyone in the group. 
And due to the continuous synchronisation 
of the folder, there will always only be one 
true version of it, even though it is changing 
all the time and present on multiple computôÙ

There are of course many technical details 
to blockchain which are not explained here. 
There are also different variations of blockôÙ

pared to each other. This is elaborated in the 
paragraph ã7.1.4. Why it Worked: Technical 
elaborationã. But at its core, the above deôÙ
scription is how blockchain works. It can be 
summarised as a public registry that stores 
transactions in a network and is replicated, 
making it very secure and hard to tamper 
with. It is at the same time a container for 
data which enables very secure data storing 
and handling. 
7.1.3.	 How it Works: Example of insulin 
transportation - improved!
Now that the basics of blockchain have been 
explained, it is time to look at the insulin 
transportation system example again. Which 
improvements can be made to this process, 
if blockchain technology becomes a part of 
it?
 
The new process is showed in Figure 37. A full 
size figure and more thorough description of 
the process with more details can be found 
in Appendix page 53. In this improved 
process, blockchain is illustrated as the cenôÙ
tral shared system with which all parties of 
the transportation chain communicates. The 
process step by step is generally the same 
as described above. However, new elements 
of digitalisation has been added. These are 
e.g. that the thermometer inside the cooling 
container is not just communicating with 
the display on the outside, but also sending 
nance, going from old models with financial 
institutions and banks to something enôÙ
tirely new; a distributed, transparent and 
autonomous system for exchanging value. 
The technology itself is described as a deôÙ
centralised database that stores a registry 
of assets and transactions across a peer-to-
peer network, meaning it is basically a public 
registry of who owns what and who transôÙ
acts what. This description is confirmed by 
several other actors in the blockchain field. 
It is by IBM defined as a shared, immutable 
ledger for recording the history of transacôÙ
tions [41], and by Gupta specified as enabling 
virtually anything of value to be tracked and 
traded, reducing risk and cutting costs for all 
involved (Gupta, 2017). 
 
The way blockchain obtains these abilities 
is through its new and unique code and way 
of verification. The transactions of value and 
information become secured through crypôÙ
tography. Over time the transaction histoôÙ
ry is locked in blocks of data that are crypôÙ
tographically linked together, creating an 
immutable, unforgeable record of all of the 
transactions across the network. The record 
is replicated on every computer that uses 
the network (Figure 36). This metaphor of 
keeping track through a chain of immutable 
blocks of data is the origin of the name 
ãblockchainã.
Blockchain is therefore not one website, 
app, programming language or company. 
The utility of blockchain can be compared to 
a Google Drive folder shared by a group of

creating a blockchain.

The use of blockchain itself is an arrangeôÙ

known. The technical details will therefore 
be elaborated in the following paragraph.

scanning of a QR code on the container. This 
is also sent to the blockchain. The real-time 
communicating thermometer and the relatôÙ
ed data analysis furthermore removes the 
need for the captain to file any error reports.
Looking back at the four issues that were

to solving these:
Solution 1: Real-time monitoring of temperaôÙ

When the temperature of the container is

Solution 2: Immutable shared ledger
The basic structure of how blockchain stores

In a public blockchain, all users in the netôÙ
work are anonymous, and the operation and 
maintenance is done only by the users of the 
network through a race among all users to 
solve an advanced mathematical problem 
first (elaborated in ãConsensus Algorithmsã). 
Bitcoin is the most famous real world appliôÙ
cation of a public blockchain. A public blockôÙ
chain is characterised by anyone being able 
to join the network and everyone performôÙ
ing the same role. There are hence no perôÙ
missions in this type of network. 
 
A private blockchain is more like an excluôÙ
sive club, where the identity of all users are 
known, and the operation and maintenance 
of the blockchain is overlooked by a facilitatôÙ
ing company and not only the users (elaboôÙ
rated in ãConsensus Algorithmsã). A private 
blockchain is characterised by having reôÙ
strictions on who can join the network, and 
users having different roles. There are hence 
permissions present in this type of network. 
 
In between a fully public and private blockôÙ
chain is a third option called a consortium 
blockchain, sometimes referred to as a 
semi-private blockchain. This type of blockôÙ
chain is similar to a private blockchain, only 
it is not a single company that is facilitating 
it. Instead this blockchain is facilitated by a 
group, like the name implies. 
 
Please note that the terms ãpublicã, ãpriôÙ
vateã and ãsemi-privateã are not identical 
to the meanings they normally have for e.g. 
businesses, schools and hospitals. They are 
used here to ease the understanding of the 
different types of blockchain. The terms 
ãun-permissionedã, 
ãpermissionedã 
and 
ãsemi-permissionedã cover the same types 
of blockchain, and are more in tune with 
the vocabulary normally used for discussing 
blockchains. 
 
See further explanation of public and private 
blockchains in Appendix page 56. 
Shared Ledger
Blockchain technology is a method of keepôÙ
ing track of transactions. There are several 
different blockchains present in the world 
at the same time, with different networks of 
people connected to each of them. One perôÙ
son can be part of multiple blockchains, and 
different blockchains can also feed data to 
each other - again, just like Google Drive. 
The metaphor ãshared ledgerã refers back 
to the old system where a business would 
have a big book with all transactions written 
down. Blockchain has the same utility, but 
instead of having only one book, everyone in 
the network has access to the same online 
book at all times. 
See further explanation on shared ledger in 
Appendix page 54. 
Peer-to-peer Network
Even though the utility of blockchain was 
compared to Google Drive in the description 
above, their fundamental structures are very 
different. For Google Drive, all synchronisaôÙ
tion goes through an external server. With 
blockchain, the synchronisation happens 
directly from computer to computer, hence 
the term ãpeer-to-peer networkã. 
 
See further explanation on peer-to-peer 
network in Appendix page 55. 
Public and Private Blockchains
It was mentioned above that different variaôÙ
tions of blockchain exist. By this, it is primarôÙ
ily meant that a blockchain can be public or 
private, operating respectively with or withôÙ
out permissions in the network.

have to create a block which is difficult to 
produce, and which satisfies certain requireôÙ
ments. At the same time it has to be easy for 
others to validate. This is done through solvôÙ
ing a very complex mathematical problem 
and finding a hash, as described above. This 
number can only be found through millions 
and millions of tries on computers. This proôÙ
cess of seeking out hashes to build blocks is 
called ãmining blocksã.
In proof-of-work, all the ãminersã are in a 
race to find the hash and build the block as 
fast as possible. This makes it very compreôÙ
hensive and expensive to operate a public 
blockchain. The alternative to this is the priôÙ
vate blockchains which uses an alternative 
way of obtaining consensus.
If a blockchain is private, it will typically use 
the consensus algorithm ãproof-of-stakeã. 
Here the basic principle behind finding a 
hash and mining blocks in the same. HowôÙ
ever, this task is assigned to the users in the 
network who has a lot on stake, and thus have 
the strongest incentives to be good stewards 
of the system, instead of everyone being in 
a race. This makes it faster and cheaper to 
operate.
A sub-version of proof-of-stake is the PracôÙ
tical Byzantine fault tolerance (PBFT) algoôÙ
rithm. Here the idea is to let multiple users 
ãvoteã on a block they find true, and when 
enough users has done this, the block is valôÙ
idated. 
 
See further explanation on consensus algoôÙ
rithms in Appendix page 61. 
Block Creation and Hashing
Regardless of the type of blockchain, the 
overall structure of one block being created 
at a given time interval by a member of the 
network is the same. The creation of blocks 
almost function like a heartbeat of the sysôÙ
tem, where all the transactions that have 
happened since the last block are collected 
and written into a new immutable block.
Each block contains a hash, which is a digital 
fingerprint that is unique to any kind of data 
written in the block. The hashes are linking 
the blocks together in a chain, since the hash 
of the previous block is always used to create 
the hash of a new block. 
 
See further explanation on block creation 
and hashing in Appendix page 59. 
Consensus Algorithms
Consensus Algorithms describe how the 
network of users reach agreement on what 
is true in the blockchain. Since blockchain 
technology relies heavily on being operated 
by the users, the method for reaching agreeôÙ
ment is very important for the functionality. 
These methods are referred to as consensus 
algorithms, and change depending on the 
type of blockchain. 
 
The need for a consensus algorithm arises 
when a new block is being created. Multiple 
users can create a block at the same time, 
so there needs to be some way of agreeing 
which block is selected to be valid. Obtaining 
consensus can be quickly described as one 
user suggesting a new block, which other 
users can validate if they find it to be true. 
If a blockchain is public, it will typically use 
the consensus algorithm ãproof-of-workã. If 
a user is to perform a proof-of-work, they

7.1.5.	 Key Advantages and Challenges
For a business to have an immutable shared 
ledger by using blockchain technology has 
several advantages. First of all, it will be 
time saving and cost saving, compared to 
approaches used today. It will also offer a 
tighter security with regards to transactions 
and data handling. The permissioned type 
of blockchains will give enhanced privacy to 
the users of the network. It will also enable 
an improved auditability for the transactions. 
All this together will result in an increased 
operational efficiency for the business using 
blockchain (Gupta, 2017). 
 
Blockchain is of course not a perfect techôÙ
nology, and the advantages listed above 
come with a lot of challenges. The biggest 
challenge will probably be the implementaôÙ
tion and adaption of blockchain among the 
general public. Since blockchain is such a 
different way of doing transactions and hanôÙ
dling value, it will be hard for the general 
public to fully understand why this new sysôÙ
tem is a good option, and convince them to 
use it. It will probably happen in a slow and 
incremental way, like the change from paôÙ
per letters to e-mail. And since blockchain 
is decentralised and highly depend on the 
users joining in and operating it, its impleôÙ
mentation is very dependent on the usersã 
adaption. For a business, the decision to 
use blockchain technology will also be inôÙ
fluenced by the large switch in operational 
practises, meaning both a significant strateôÙ
gic step towards this new system, and a relôÙ
atively high initial cost to implement it. The 
advantages and savings with blockchain will 
have to be very clear before a business wants 
to take this step and invest in the technology 
[42]. 
 
Blockchainsã largest advantage is also its main 
challenge; trust. If a blockchain system is imôÙ
Hacking and Forks
Blockchain is said to be unhackable, but this 
is not entirely true. It is technically possible 
to hack it. However, blockchain is constructôÙ
ed in such a way that any hacker attack will 
require so much time and resources, that it 
will be virtually impossible for the attacker 
to generate any profit from it. The reason for 
this lies in how new blocks are created, with 
one user creating it and the other users in 
the network validating it. If more users make 
blocks a the same time, the block with the 
most validation wins. 
 
If a hacker makes an attack, a fork with two 
paths is created in the blockchain. The hackôÙ
er works on the ãfalse pathã, and the rest 
of the network keeps working on the ãtrue 
pathã. 
For the false path to get accepted into the 
blockchain, and thus for the hacker attack 
to be successful, the hacker needs more valôÙ
idation behind the false path than the rest of 
the network can provide for the true path. 
This means that the hacker needs more than 
51% of all the validation power (either comôÙ
puter power or value at stake) in order to 
win. To control or own so much in such large 
networks is what makes a successful hackôÙ
er attack virtually impossible, and definitely 
unprofitable. 
 
See further explanation on hacking and forks 
in Appendix page 64.

One of the most novel advantages of blockôÙ
chain is the possibility for using smart 
contracts. A smart contract written in the 
blockchain is, like contracts in real life, an 
agreement on a set of rules. A smart contract 
however has the ability to be automatically 
executed, e.g. as part of a transaction. It will

7.2.	Internet of Things
The Internet of Things is already briefly deôÙ

for application are given. The examples are

this paragraph will look at some of the other 
aspects of the IoT. 
 
The IoT is the general the term for multiôÙ
ple devices connected to and exchanging 
data with the internet as well as each other. 
These devices can be sensors, as described 
in the case study. It can also be everything 
from mobile phones, computers and cars to 
parking meters, thermostats and supermarôÙ

source of data which can be further analysed 
with human interventions, it can also funcôÙ
tion as multiple machine-to-machine apôÙ
plications, where the devices automatically 
communicate with each other and act acôÙ
cordingly (Biggs et al., 2016). This can e.g. be 
the thermostat which tells the heating sysôÙ
tem to turn up the heat, or the supermarket 
shelves automatically ordering new grocerôÙ
ies when they are empty. 
7.2.1.	 Key Advantages and Challenges
The IoT gives several new possibilities. The 
biggest one is a greater level of monitoring 
and measurements. This can be of both the 
state and actions of humans, machines, enôÙ

tomated machine-to-machine communicaôÙ

in fewer human errors and minimised need

The trust is built into the blockchain system,

transactions, and through the unhackable 
nature of the system. This means there is no 
longer a need to know an individual in adôÙ

fore a blockchain system can get up and runôÙ
ning, the users need to trust that the system 
will be able to carry out all these functionôÙ

to handle value and transactions is basically 
to take banks out of the picture, and put the 
responsibility of handling value back into the 
hands of individuals. And even though the 
blockchain system ensures that you do not 
have to trust these individuals, you still need 
to trust that the blockchain system can make 
up for this lack of trust in individuals [43]. 
 
The many possibilities with blockchain can 
not be obtained if it is used alone. An importôÙ

combination with other fast growing techôÙ
nologies, such as Big Data and the Internet 
of Things (IoT). Both IoT and blockchain can

IoT and Big Data can be stored in blockchain.

mobile phones, computers, cars, parking

7.3.	Big Data
Big Data is an important phenomenon, which 
is quietly changing our everyday lives, comôÙ
panies and organisations. The data generatôÙ
ed every day by both people and machines 
has grown exponentially. Every day, 2.5 quinôÙ
tillion bytes of data are createdãso much 
that 90% of the data in the world today has 
been created in the last two years [45]. The 
size of these data sets is incomprehensible, 
and it can still be difficult to grasp the conôÙ
cept of Big Data and the impact it can have 
on the world. 
 
How to define Big Data is still being disôÙ
cussed, and definitions vary depending on 
what context it is presented in and for which 
use it is applied (Chen et al., 2014) [47]. In 
general, Big Data refers to datasets that canôÙ
not not be perceived, acquired, managed, 
and processed by traditional IT and softôÙ
ware/hardware tools within a tolerable time 
(Chen et al., 2014). Popularly said; if it is too 
big to handle in MS Excel then it is Big Data. 
But Big Data doesnãt only refer to the size of 
the data.
 
Big Data has been around as a term since the 
ã90s, and several have tried to claim the fame 
for coining it [46]. In 2001 however, a definiôÙ
tion emerged, which has had significant imôÙ
pact on how we define Big Data today. Doug 
Laney presented a 3 Vs model (Volume, VaôÙ
riety, Velocity), which was used to describe 
the challenges and opportunities generated 
by the rapidly increasing data (Laney, 2001; 
Chen et al., 2014). Now, the common definiôÙ
tion has been expanded to include a total of 
5 Vs [48]:

Technical instability
The parts of the world where humanitariôÙ

weather conditions can also be extreme and 
further jeopardise technical stability. When

heavy and constant technical support.
 
Late payoff
The initial costs for especially solutions inôÙ

Accurate decisions

from not only own but also othersã successes 
and mistakes.
 
Targeted use
One of the obstacles for using cash-based aid

set up, which can be difficult to administer. 
Blockchain has the potential to become the 
perfect middle-way between the restricted 
vouchers and unrestricted cash, as well as a 
middle-way between open and closed loop 
systems. Some funds can be earmarked, or 
kept in a closed loop, while others can be 
completely unrestricted like cash.

8.1.2.	 Solution Space
The solution space was explored through 
three themes of case studies in Chapter 6. 
It was found that for each theme there were 
examples of successful implementations 
with challenges to consider. What is interestôÙ
ing to explore is the overlap between these 
three themes, where the solution space for 
our thesis lies. 
 
The linkage between sanitation and payment 
was found existing and functioning due to 
the basic human need for good hygiene. 
The refugee finance cases found that cash-
based aid exists in many forms, and it is in 
general improving dignity and well-being of 
refugees. HTLC was found to exist at a surôÙ
prisingly high level, and improving general 
relief assistance. What can be derived from 
these case studies is that the solution space 
is large since each of the themes shows that 
successful implementations are possible. 
Several factors are pointing in the direction 
of a solution linking these three themes. 
 
However, this does not prove that a solution 
coupling sanitation and refugee finances 
with an enabling high-tech low-cost techôÙ
nology will be a good solution no matter 
what. It simply proves that the soil for creôÙ
ating a solution like this is fertile, and that it 
might be feasible with the right external inôÙ
terventions. The case studies also provided a 
range of learnings and issues to consider in 
the future work. The three themes had the 
challenges of high initial costs, dependency 
on access to mobile phones and connectiviôÙ
ty, and risk of exploitation of the system.
 
Another important take-away from the case 
studies is that several of them are operating 
8.1.	Scoping the Problem
This chapter summarises all the previous 
chapters in section 2, and analyses how the 
problem can be scoped so future developôÙ
ment of a solution is feasible.
8.1.1.	 Where to Make Changes
The challenges were analysed in Chapter 5, 
and resulted in an overview of the many sanôÙ
itation and hygiene challenges present in a 
refugee camp. These challenges should be 
addressed in a future concept, but it is imôÙ
portant to bear in mind that not all challengôÙ
es are necessarily present in the same camp 
at the same time. Throughout the analysis 
it has become clear that even though the 
issues here could all fit into the archetype 
camp, the cultural and personal differences 
have a large influence on what type of soluôÙ
tion is possible and desired. Therefore the 
context is extremely important.
 
The UAC modelling showed us that the chalôÙ
lenge lies not within the activities in the cyôÙ
cle, but with the needs for fulfilling these 
activities. It is the breakdowns which interôÙ
rupt the intended cycle that is the problem. 
Therefore the focus must be on removing 
these breakdowns at the three levels. The 
causal loop diagrams tell us how these probôÙ
lems are interrelated. It is important to bear 
the dynamics of these systems in mind when 
designing a future concept. Problems at the 
deepest, most tangible level, can create very 
large negative effects at the top level. Or 
put in another way, if the refugees are not 
motivated to use the toilets, it is extremely 
important to ensure that the WASH interôÙ
ventions are functioning, and therefore balôÙ
ancing the number of ill people. If it fails, we 
can see that a high number of ill people will 
contribute to a negative cycle at the NGO 
level, which will drain resources and capabilôÙ
ity to handle the crisis. We therefore suggest 
that the concept has to be three-fold.
 
At the personal level, the concept must adôÙ
dress the lack of motivation to use the faôÙ

8.2.	Preparing for Concept 
Development
Section 2 has had the function of not only 
clarifying the problem, but also being an exôÙ
ternal search for existing concepts and good 
practices. The coming concept development 
phase in Section 3 is focused on a more inôÙ
ternal search for new concepts. In reality, the 
external and internal search has happened 
somewhat iteratively, but for readability it 
is presented in a more linear fashion in this 
report.
 
To consolidate the research in this secôÙ
tion, we decided to make a specification of 
demands, in order to define what the conôÙ
cept needs to include, and how it should 
be formed. The process was iterative and a 
challenge, as the concept to specify is not a 
physical product, but a system. Moreover, the 
boundaries for developing the concept are 
very wide. The demands are more important 
factors to consider, rather than needs which 
must be solved in order for the solution to 
work. The specification of demands had to 
merge the most important aspects from the 
whole context study.
 
In order to approach this task, all the breakôÙ
downs from the UACs were listed and dividôÙ
ed into a set of factors which define adequate 
sanitation. The factors were an elaboration 
on ãadequate sanitationã from the problem 
statement: Understand whether high-tech 
solutions can contribute to ensuring adequate 
sanitation in planned refugee camps. The PSS 
mindset was used here, as it was the ãaccess 
to sanitation facilitiesã that was the central 
objective for the specification of demands, 
and not the product ãlatrinesã. The factors, 
combined with the learnings from the case 
studies and the affordance of the technolôÙ
ogy, were translated into a specification of 
demands, through several iterations. 
8.1.3.	 Maturity of Technology 
The challenges that the NGOs face correlate 
well with what the new technologies can 
offer. It is however also a demand that the 
technology fits with how the NGOs work, 
and that is it mature enough for the intended 
purpose. In this context, particularly blockôÙ
chain is the newest and most unknown techôÙ
nology. 
 
A measure for how mature the blockchain 
technology is can be found on the Gartnerãs 
Hype Cycle (Figure 38) [51].  This is a graph 
which is released once a year by Gartner, 
a large research and advisory company. It 
shows how hyped upcoming technologies 
are, and estimates how long it will take for 
them to be mainstream. On the newest Hype 
Cycle, we can find blockchain technology 
near the peak of hypeness [51]. Since this 
graph was made in July 2016, it is likely that 
it is even further progressed now, since the 
blockchain field moves very fast. Blockchain 
technology is said to be part of the platform 
revolution, which means a shift from techôÙ
nical infrastructures to ecosystem-enabling 
platforms. 
So blockchain is mature and well-defined 
enough to be out of the ãinnovation triggerã 
phase, but it is still far from being mainstream 
(5-10 years). It is thus still ãshapeableã, which 
is good for the potential involvement of NGOs 
in using the technology. The technology is 
however still not so well-defined and testôÙ
ed so it is risk-free to get involved with. It is 
still in a stage where a company or an organôÙ
isation is taking a chance by using this new 
technology. This can be very hard for NGOs 
to do, since they are mainly working without 
profit, and do not have ãbreaksã in their work 
to make large organisational changes. This 
does however not mean that NGOs should 
wait to use the blockchain technology until 
it has been mainstreamed adapted in 5-10 
years. They should still start getting involved 
and ãjoin the movementã along with other 
companies and organisations, if they want to 
make full use of the potential of blockchain 
technology. In conclusion, it is assessed that

The final specification of demands can be 
seen on Appendix page 66. It divides the 
concept into three systems (Figure 39). The 
three systems are inspired by what we found 
to be the biggest problem at each level of the 
challenge analysis.
 
The first system is a motivational system. 
This system has a twofold purpose; it must 
address the lack of motivation to use the 
facilities, and ensure that the refugees are 
motivated, or at least willing to become data 
sources. 
When the refugees are data sources we can 
create a data collection and analysis system 
for the WASH management. This system has 
the purpose of ensuring analysis to enable 
more rapid and better, data-driven intervenôÙ
tions. 
The top system is the collaboration system, 
which has the purpose of enabling NGOs to 
better collaborate internally and externally 
with other NGOs to enable better knowlôÙ
edge sharing and therefore more capability 
to handle crises.
 
The specification of demands represent the 
optimal solution, meaning that all the chalôÙ
lenges found in the analysis are addressed, 
if all demands and criteria are fulfilled. In 
the next concept development section, the 
specification of demands will therefore be 
used to evaluate different concepts, to see 
which one is the most optimal solution adôÙ
dressing most challenges.

Introduction to Section 3
Research Question
Section 2 gave an overview of the challenges which should be addressed in a concept, 
along with a range of aspects to consider, and a description of the new possibilites 
with the technology. Section 3 will cover the prescriptive study of this thesis, and 
will show how the a concept was delevoped. The following research question will be 
answered:
RQ3: How can we design a concept which applies the affordance of new 
technology to address several actorsã needs at the same time?
Outline
First, we will describe the conceptualisation phase consisting of brainstorming and 
idea generation. This results in six initial concepts, which are evaluated and reduced to 
one concept. This concept is described on a generic level, and is then further detailed. 
Lastly, different parts of the concept are tested through an experiment at Roskilde 
Festival, and workshops with NGOs.
Outcome
When having finished reading this section, you will have thorough insight into the 
concept development part of this thesis. You will know the arguments for why the 
concept turned out as it did, and hence be ready to understand the final concept preôÙ
sentation in the next section, along with reflection of it.
