9.1.1.	 Inspirational Methods
For the motivational system it was chosen 
to try out behavioural design, namely a tool 
called the Behaviour Change Wheel (Nielsen 
& Aakerlund, 2016). The model van be seen in 
Figure 41. The rationale for this was that the 
motivational system in our concept deliberôÙ
ately tries to change a behaviour, and it was 
therefore found that this field of design theôÙ
ory could be useful. The Behaviour Change 
Wheel is a model which classifies inspiraôÙ
tional words into three categories. The words 
can be picked one by one and combined into 
concepts or several words can be chosen at 
once. The tool proved to be an interesting 
source of inspiration, but it was too specific 
This chapter covers the concept developôÙ
ment phase towards a final concept for the 
three systems (Figure 40). The concept deôÙ
velopment goes through three iterations 
before a concept for detailing is chosen; 
an explorative idea generation iteration, a 
co-creating iteration and an evaluation itôÙ
eration. The iterations are not happening in 
a strict chronological order, but have overôÙ
laps and jumps between them towards a fiôÙ
nal concept. After the iterations,  a concept 
is chosen for detailing and further analysis. 
9.1.	Iteration 1: 
Explorative idea generation
Starting an idea generation is an exciting 
but difficult phase for a design engineer. 
There are many methods from the toolbox 
to choose between, and it is important to 
try and decide early on what will be part of 
the project. The explorative idea generation, 
has the ultimate goal to enlarge the solution 
space as far as reasonably possible and is an 
opportunity to try out several quick methôÙ
ods. The process so far has had the form of 
an external search (Ulrich & Eppinger, 2012). 
which aimed at finding existing solutions 
through case studies, and consulting experts 
through interviews. The next step is thus an 
internal search process, using team knowlôÙ
edge and creativity to generate solution 
concepts (Ulrich & Eppinger, 2012).
 
The specification of demands (Cross, 2008; 
Ulrich & Eppinger, 2012) and the three sysôÙ
tems figure were a good point of departure 
for this next step, as the methods and ideas 
could be split depending on the three sysôÙ
tems. For all three systems, several rounds 
of brainstorming with rules (Cross, 2008) to 
obtain best results were performed, each 
with different inspirational sources. It was a 
clear goal from the beginning to have at least 
70 ideas. By setting this goal we tried to push 
the boundaries of our imagination, in order 
to have a large quantity of ideas and thereôÙ
by ensuring a broad idea space (Cross, 2008). 
Brainstorming sessions varied between team

9.1.2.	 Generating Holistic Concepts 
To create wholesome concepts which comôÙ
bined ideas for each of the three systems, 
a morphological chart (Cross, 2008). The 
method is also sometimes known as a conôÙ
cept combination table, and provides a way 
to consider combinations of solution fragôÙ
ments systematically (Ulrich & Eppinger, 
2012). Fragments are usually divided into 
columns which correspond to the subprobôÙ
lems. In this case the ideas were divided into 
the three systems, each representing their 
own subproblem. These were laid out on a 
table so new combinations could be found. 
The combination of fragments rarely sponôÙ
taneously leads to a solution to the overall 
problem (Ulrich & Eppinger, 2012). The fragôÙ
ments must be integrated, which isnãt always 
possible, but the process itself creates addiôÙ
tional creative thought. To inspire creativiôÙ

Differentiated value
Refugees are being motivated for 
better hygiene practices, by giving 
money spent on hygiene articles a 
higher value. 
Reward for latrine use
Refugees are motivated to use the laôÙ
trine facilities, by receiving a reward 
for using them. This is done through 
a digital wallet which is linked to a 
device, that transmits a signal to a 
receiver. When the signal is received 
the reward is transferred to a digital 
ID.
Group contracting
A chosen group of refugees are hired 
for a job together. The NGO can 
choose refugees, who electronically 
verify they are working on the job 
together, and receive payment when 
the job is done.
Leasing latrine
Refugee pays deposit to the NGO, 
who provides a private latrine. A 
cleaning fee is paid by the refugee. 
When the refugee leaves, the deposit 
is returned.
Job push messages
Refugees are enabled to take job 
opportunities, by NGOs and other 
refugees sending work requests. This 
is done through text push messages, 
which are sent to the refugee who 
can then take the task. 
Individual contracting
Refugees can sign contracts through 
their phone through a digital ID and 
signature, and get small quick jobs.

9.2.2.	 Expert Workshop
The second workshop was conducted with 
an expert within the field of humanitarian 
work, namely Lisbet ElvekjûÎr who is a HuôÙ
manitarian Advisor within the Danish Red 
Cross (Appendix page 74). The purpose 
was to get feedback on the six concepts preôÙ
viously described, as well as developing them 
together. The workshop was conducted by 
laying out the concepts for the participant, 
and then ask her to explain the concepts to 
us. By turning the roles around in this way, 
we aimed to get insight into where she put 
value in the concepts, and which parts of the 
concepts seemed most interesting to her. In 
order to get some variation in the workshop, 
we took turns explaining a concept to each 
other, one at a time. For all concepts she was 
asked to mention pros and cons, and also to 
come with suggestions for further developôÙ
9.2.	Iteration 2: 
Co-creating concepts
During the idea generation, it was desired to 
supplement our own knowledge with input 
from external parties, both for development 
of more ideas, and for feedback on existing 
ideas. For this purpose, two workshops were 
conducted. 
 
9.2.1.	 Fresh Eyes Workshop
The first workshop was conducted with 
three fellow students from the Design and

purpose of getting some fresh eyes on the

the refugees and the NGOs. Each group was 
given a set of cards representing the chalôÙ
lenges and reservations that was relevant for 
their respective role. They were furthermore 
given a set of shared cards representing new 
technological possibilities from blockchain, 
IoT and Big Data. The participants were then 
asked to play out a small role-play where the 
ãrefugeesã and ãNGOsã discussed a disagreeôÙ

9.3.	Iteration 3: 
Concept evaluation
Based on the feedback gained from the 
expert workshop, it was decided to narôÙ
row down the six rough concept into three 
more specific concepts; a differentiated valôÙ
ue concept, a reward concept, and a work 
concept (Appendix page 77). The leasing 
concept was dismissed as it was very similar 
to some programmes the NGOs already had 
(ElvekjûÎr, 2017), and the three work concepts 
(number four, five and six) were merged into 
one, as it became apparent that they each 
were a bit too weak to stand on their own. 
 
9.3.1.	 Weighted Objectives Method
In order to evaluate the concept in a strucôÙ
tured and easy to follow manner, it was deôÙ
cided to use the Weighted Objectives MethôÙ
od (WOM) (Cross, 2008). This method allows 
the concepts to be evaluated on a long list 
of objectives, and also for the objectives to 
be weighted depending on how important 
they are seen to be in the total evaluation 
of the concept. Please note that it is not the 
method that is deciding on the final concept 
for us, it is only functioning as a way of ilôÙ
lustrating which traits are most important, 
and as a guidance tool for the reader to folôÙ
low our line of thought. The specification of 
demands described in the previous chapter

ãÂ	 The generic concept is called the hygiene data reward system

data related to hygiene in a camp

three systemsã interplay

ãÂ	 when the system has been set up, the ngo doesnãt have to 
support the refugeesã interaction with it
ãÂ	 donors are brought closer to the refugees as they are 
able to track the funds
ãÂ	 the blockchain facilitator is an important actor in the 
ngosã uac, acting as the backbone of the whole system
ãÂ	 other ngos can become part of the system as 
collaboration is made easier
ãÂ	 points of variation are introduced to show where there is

blockchain technology. The refugee can use 
their reward in a shop which is also part of 
the system. The motivational system is deôÙ
fined for and used by the population of one 
specific refugee camp at a time. 
 
The data collection and analysis system conôÙ
sists of the data generated when a refugee is 
registered using a latrine. The registrations 
are coupled with information collected when 
the refugees sign up for the system, such as 
gender, age, country of origin and so on. FiôÙ
nally the data on how the refugees spend the 
reward in the shops is also collected. This 
system is used by the camp management 
and WASH management in one specific refôÙ
ugee camp at a time.
 
The collaboration system consists of severôÙ
al NGOs working together on improvements 
in the administration of refugee camps. This 
system is thus used by different organisaôÙ
tions and by many different stakeholders. 
This chapter will present the final concept 
which is based on the concept chosen in the 
previous chapter. Here it has been specified 
using user activity cycles from respectively 
the NGOãs and refugeeãs perspective. 
The final concept was renamed to ãHygiene 
Data Reward Systemã, as it gives rewards to 
refugees for doing activities that generate 
data related to hygiene in a camp. In this 
way, the concept has a short-term payoff by 
making refugees use the latrines, and a long-
term payoff of generating data for analysis 
and interventions. The concept is described 
here as a generic concept, since many essenôÙ
tial details in the concept are not possible to 
specify, without having a concrete context 
to use it on. The generic concept and the 
descriptions of it can therefore be seen as 
a template which can be filled out with the 
correct details. 
The unspecified parts of the concept are furôÙ
ther elaborated in ã10.4 Points of Variationã. 
The generic concept is described using three 
different methods;  concept sketch, storyôÙ
board and UACs. 
 
10.1.	 Concept Sketch: Thorough 
description of three systems
A sketch of the Hygiene Data Reward SysôÙ
tem can be seen in Figure 43 and in AppenôÙ
dix page 83. The sketch is divided into the 
three systems which formed the base of the 
idea generation, with the motivation system 
at the bottom, the data collection and analyôÙ
sis system in the middle, and the collaboraôÙ
tion system on the top. 
 
The motivation system consists of the refôÙ
ugees receiving some kind of reward when 
they use the latrines in the camp. Their visits 
at the latrine are registered through the refôÙ
ugees carrying a small device, which is regôÙ
istered by a receiver installed at the latrine. 
The reward is automatically transferred to 
the refugeeãs online wallet, which is linked to 
an online ID. Both the wallet and ID relies on

10.3.	 User Activity Cycles
A more systematic way of describing the geôÙ
neric concept is through UACs. We decided 
to create two UACs which are seen from reôÙ
spectively a refugeeãs and the NGOãs point 
of view. The UACs give a thorough overview 
of how the concept works, and especially 
which actors are involved. They describe the 
full cycle a user goes through when interôÙ
acting with the system. The relevant actors 
for each of step in the cycle are illustrated in 
the UACs with circles. They have varying size 
and closeness to the central actor, dependôÙ
ing on how large a role they play, and who 
they interact with. The UACs give a good 
foundation for analysis and discussion of the 
concept. The UACs can be seen in full size in 
Appendix page 84 and page 85. 
10.3.1.	Refugee Point of View
The cycle, visible in Figure 45 on the next 
page, describes the same story as the storyôÙ
board, with the addition that refugees expeôÙ
rience the improvements resulting from the 
data analysis, and subsequent intervention. 
It is also added that the refugees eventually 
leave the camp.
 
What is worth noting in this cycle are the 
connected actors involved in each step. As 
mentioned, the coloured circles represent 
an interaction between the refugee and the 
actor assigned to the respective colour of the 
circle. If two circles are touching each othôÙ
er, it represents an interaction between the 
external actors. For example: In the very first 
step the refugee is interacting with the camp 
management when arriving to the camp and 
while being registered. At the same time, 
the camp management is interacting with 
the blockchain facilitator, by generating 
data which is stored in the blockchain. And 
the blockchain facilitator is interacting with 
various sub-suppliers who are taking care of 
maintenance, serverspace etc. 
 
When looking at the interactions during the 
rest of the cycle, it becomes apparent that 
the camp management does not have a very 
10.2.	 Storyboard: Description of 
Activities
The three systems above all take place simulôÙ
taneously, since they are all dependent on 
each othersã existence. However, in order to 
ease the explanation of what happens during 
the operation of the systems, the storyboard 
to the right (Figure 44) shows the different 
actions taking place in an approximately 
chronological order. 
  
1) The refugee registers for being part of the 
Hygiene Data Reward System programme, 
and has an online ID and wallet set up. This 
might very well happen when the refugee arôÙ
rives to the camp in the first place, and has to 
go through a registration process anyway. 2) 
The refugee then receives the device which 
registers the latrine visits, which 3) can have 
various forms and be carried in different 
ways. 4) The refugee then goes to the latrine 
during his/her stay in the camp, and 5) reôÙ
ceives a reward related to the registrations 
in his/hers online wallet. 6) The refugee can 
check his/hers balance via access to the onôÙ
line wallet. 7) The refugee can then goes to 
a shop which is also part of the programme, 
and 8) spend the reward. 9) (figure on next 
page) The camp and WASH management reôÙ
ceives the data generated from latrine use 
registrations and consumption in shops, 
and 10) performs analysis based on this data. 
They review the results, and 11) make interôÙ
ventions in the camp based on these. 12) The 
data is furthermore sent to a shared platform 
between several NGOs, who perform a meta 
analysis, with data from several different refôÙ
ugee camps. 13) The NGOs share their analôÙ
ysis and knowledge, enhancing collaboration 
between them.

chain facilitator to meet, and decide how the 
governance of the system will be, with input 
from the donors. This would be e.g. which 
kinds of certificates and access should exist 
in the blockchain, how the consensus mechôÙ
anism functions, who is in charge of mainôÙ
taining which parts of the blockchain and so 
on. After this process of agreement is done, 
the blockchain facilitator will create the code 
responding to the demands, which is then 
released and given to the NGOs. Afterwards 
it is the code that runs itself, with the NGOs 
as the users and the blockchain facilitator 
participating only for maintenance and supôÙ
port. This structure means that a separate 
blockchain will exist for each camp using the 
concept, or at least a separate sub-blockôÙ
chain connected to a main blockchain, if 
there are multiple camps in the same area, or 
if the same NGO has several similar projects. 
Once the blockchain is operational for the 
particular camp, the during-phase starts and 
the story described above with registrations 
of latrine use and distribution of rewards 
runs its course. One added activity here is 
that the camp management facilitates IT 
support for the refugees, in case they have 
any questions related to their own interacôÙ
tion with the blockchain, or with the regisôÙ
tration devices. It can be seen that the blockôÙ
chain facilitator is present in almost all steps 
of the cycle, acting as the skeleton on which 
the whole concept functions. 
It is also worth noting that other NGOs are 
active actors in the later steps of the cycle, 
in terms of collaboration and knowledge 
sharing. This incorporation of collaborators 
is not necessarily a matter of course as pracôÙ
tices are today, but has become possible due 
to blockchain technology.
  
In the post-phase of the cycle, the concept is 
phased out, and the project is evaluated for 
further knowledge sharing and improvement 
of the concept. The donors are involved 
here, as they get insights into how well the 
activities have functioned, how the money 
has been spent, and how many people have 
received aid. 
active role in the during-phase, since the 
concept is pretty much self-running once it 
is set up. This means the concept is does not 
demand much manpower and attention to 
function, and thus addresses the NGOs need 
for getting the most out of the resources 
available. The camp management of course 
still plays an active role in the beginning and 
ending of a refugeeãs stay in the camp. This 
corresponds well to how the practice is toôÙ
day. NGOs do not want to minimise this conôÙ
tact, since communication with refugees is 
essential and has high priority.
Instead of interacting directly with the camp 
management in the during-phase, the refuôÙ
gees are in fact interacting with an interface 
set up by the camp management, which is 
linked directly to the blockchain. This both 
decreases the workload for NGOs as menôÙ
tioned above, but also increases the indeôÙ
pendence of the refugees, since they beôÙ
come more in control of their own situation 
and are not as dependent on assistance from 
the camp management. 
It is also worth noting that there is a rather 
small distance between the refugee and the 
donors in the ãreceive rewardã and ãspend 
rewardã steps. This does not mean that the 
refugee and donor are in direct contact, but 
with the audit trails enabled by the blockôÙ
chain technology, donors can get insight 
into what the money they have donated is 
spent on.

The UAC from the NGO point of view is illusôÙ

it describes additional steps about the conôÙ

about the demounting and outphasing of 
the concept. The activities described above 
are in this figure only present in the during-
phase. 
The pre-phase describes the work that has 
to be done before the actual implementation 
of the concept can take place. The main step 
here is for the camp management and blockôÙ

The list can be found in Appendix page 86. 
It is easy to spot that the NGO point of view 
has by far the most points of variation. This 
is only to be expected, considering the NGO 
is the actor who has to take many decisions 
regarding the operation of the system, while 
the refugees are less influential users. And 
even though the points of variation in the 
refugee point of view, are questions that will 
concern the refugees, most of them will also 
concern the NGO. 
 
The purpose of these points of variation is to 
provide better grounds for further developôÙ
ment. It is thought that a team with insight 
in a specific context could answer the quesôÙ
tions which would then determine the exact 
configuration of the concept.
10.4.	 Points of Variation 
The concept, as it is now, is at the level of 
detail we deem suitable, without specifyôÙ
ing the specific context it will be applied in. 
The concept can function in many settings 
as it leaves some questions unanswered, and 
doesnãt restrict itself to a specific type of laôÙ
trine or a specific type of reward. It contains 
just the essential value which we gain from 
the technology and from the unique combiôÙ
nation of the system levels. 
In order to be able to move on from this point, 
we have identified and listed the unspecified 
details. From now on we call these the points 
of variation, as they define the parts of the 
concept that vary depending on the context.
The points of variation were identified using 
the UAC, and inspired by discussions with 
NGOs. In Figure 45 and Figure 46 it can be 
noted that some of the activities are bold and 
black. These activities represent the points 
of variation, while the greyed out activities 
are fixed. 
 
The points of variation have been expanded 
to include a list of questions which should be 
answered in order to design the activity.

11.1.	 Three Storylines
The idea behind the generic concept is to 
facilitate creation of a core concept which 
can be applied and detailed according to the 
context. To try this out three storylines are 
presented. The storylines are written in the 
style of case studies, in the past tense. The 
storylines have been chosen from the far exôÙ
tremes of the archetype to show the breadth 
of the concept and test its limits. All camp 
information is fictional but based on statisôÙ
tics from similar camps.
For each story, there are supporting sketchôÙ
es, which create a walk-through of the conôÙ
cept. Sketching provides a way to commuôÙ
nicate mental pictures, and is an important 
tool for all designers (Roberts et al., 2017). 
Creating and visualising these storylines 
gives a good foundation for discussing the 
concepts. Moreover, simple sketches allow 
for change to occur, which is important at 
this stage of the design. 
The generic concept developed in the preôÙ
vious chapter is as far as we can go withôÙ
out having context specific information. We 
do however want to explore the conceptãs 
strengths and weaknesses further. 
One of the conclusions of the context study 
was that each camp, and each NGO has their 
own way of working and that the context inôÙ
cludes important details which have to be 
taken into account when planning intervenôÙ
tions. In the generic concept, the points of 
variation express the holes which need to be 
filled with details based on context specific 
information. To explore the points of variaôÙ
tion further, and to visualise what is possible 
with the generic concept, this chapter goes 
into further detail with example use casôÙ
es. First we present three storylines which 
showcase the breadth of the concept. The 
storyline cases are based on the archetype 
camp,   but goes to the extremities of it with 
regards to e.g. the number of refugees in the 
camp. One case is then chosen for a detailed 
description using a combination of UAC 
and TTS. This case is also used for a Failure 
mode, Effect and Criticality Analysis (FMEôÙ
CA), which will explore what can go wrong in 
the system.
Chapter 11	 I Concept Detailing
Picture 29.	Sketching is an important tool for communication
==PAGE:148==
concept detailing I chapter 11 I section 3
134
own. All refugees were given the option to 
join a hygiene programme, which gave benôÙ
efits to the people who used the facilities. 
Participants would receive a bracelet, which 
registered every time the owner went inside 
the latrine building. If the bracelet was regôÙ
istered every day for a week, new options 
opened up at the weekly distribution of hyôÙ
giene products. Instead of e.g. receiving the 
standard white soap it became possible to 
get one with various fragrances. 
 
The system didnãt eliminate all the homeôÙ
made latrines, but it made it possible to track 
where the latrines were used the least, and 
investigate the cause. Demographic inforôÙ
mation was collected and variances in use 
could be analysed. The bracelet also became 
a symbol of status and cleanness, something 
which was reinforced by the access to speôÙ
cial hygiene products. The biggest advantage 
however came when an outbreak of cholera 
was prevented from spreading to the entire 
camp. An unusually high frequency of chilôÙ
drenãs visits to the latrines was detected, and 
a quick vaccine programme saved both medôÙ
icine costs and lives. 
11.1.1.	Dedeeb, Kenya
The camp of Dedeeb in rural Kenya houses 
50.000 people, primarily internally displaced 
refugees. The camp has existed for more 
than ten years and is very well-settled. It 
seems almost like a town. The refugees living 
there are focused on normalising life, as they 
are bound to stay for a long time. 
The sanitation in Dedeeb used to be insufôÙ
ficient and faced by great challenges. The 
WASH management struggled with abanôÙ
doned and vandalised facilities. Many houseôÙ
holds had tried setting up their own latrines 
and showers by connecting hoses to the 
common facilities, and letting the sewage 
flow out below their shelter. This behaviour, 
however natural, resulted in malfunctioning 
of the common facilities as well as water and 
feces flowing out onto the streets. Outbreaks 
of disease were common, but the WASH 
management had poor chances of getting an 
overview of where the problems were worst, 
or to be proactive. To face these challenges a 
new system was introduced which provided 
a better overview and supported the ongoôÙ
ing activities to motivate people to use the 
common facilities, rather than building their 
People are encouraged to enter 
the benefit programme
The word is spread through 
e.g. the WASH committees
Participators receive a fancy 
bracelet with RFID chip
The bracelet becomes a status 
symbol, a proof of having good 
personal hygiene

11.1.2.	Shatali, Lebanon
The Shatali camp in Lebanon has existed for 
three years and houses 20.000 people. The 
refugees come from the neighbouring counôÙ
tries, and donãt know when they will move on. 
Most refugees receive some form of cash-
aid, but registration used to be difficult and 
the constant need for verification of the refôÙ
ugeesã presence in the camp was tiring and 
expensive for both staff and refugees. The 
many organisations which paid out cash aid 
had different methods for verification and 
conditions for receiving the benefits. The 
coordination in the camp was generally a 
challenge, as all organisations had their own 
methods for collecting information, which 
moreover rarely was consistent. Shatali also 
struggled with sanitation challenges as open 
defecation was common, especially in areas 
where the distance to the facilities was large. 
Children also often defecated in the open, in 
areas where they also played and therefore 
became very vulnerable to diseases.

The second summer an acute intervention 
was made to stop the open defecation. All 
refugees were offered additional aid, if they 
participated in the sanitation programme. 
The additional aid was a small amount, but 
the aid was free to be used for all purposes, 
which was seen by the refugees as a great 
improvement in life quality. The programme

11.2.	 Using UAC and TTS for 
Concept Detailing
The Shatila case is the one which fits the 
archetype best and is therefore chosen for 
further elaboration. In Chapter 3 it was exôÙ
plained how UACs can be combined with the 
Theory of Technical Systems. Based on this 
approach we use the method to detail the 
Shatila case. The strength of the UAC lies in 
getting an overview of all the activities a user 
goes through. TTS allows for more detail, as 
the designer is forced to consider which paôÙ
rameters participate in which part of this 
complex cycle and how. The method allows 
for asking questions on e.g. which informaôÙ
tion is necessary to perform a given activity. 
 
Each step in the generic UAC has been listed 
as a transformation, and the parameters parôÙ
ticipating in the activity have been mapped 
out. An example can be seen below in Figure 
47. Here only an extract has been brought, as 
the whole chain is very long. 
An important aspect for our analysis, is which 
actors are part of the system not only as the 
human system but also who the information 
belongs to or who sets the goals. To be able 
to see and understand who the participatôÙ
ing actors are, the parameters (represented 
as bubbles) have been coloured. When there 
are more actors, the colours are ranged toôÙ
wards the middle, according to which actor 
plays the central role. The computer is e.g. 
the NGOãs, but the sticker becomes the refôÙ
ugeeãs property. 
The TTS chain and the UAC take the perôÙ
spective of subsequently the refugee and the 
NGO. This primarily affects the blockchain 
facilitator, who is not a part of the visible 
system to the refugees, and therefore selôÙ
dom participates in their TTS chain.
Making this TTS chain forced us to go into 
detail and answer some important questions. 
Especially what information is necessary to 
fulfill the transformation was interesting to 
ponder upon. It showed us that it was imôÙ
portant to know some more about what inôÙ
formation the NGOs have and want. It also 
supported the questions asked in the points 
of variation, and helped updating those.

11.3.	 Failure Mode, Effect and 
Criticality Analysis
In order to get a deeper understanding of 
what failures and flaws the system concept 
has, we use the FMECA. FMECA is commonly 
used for hazard identification, in many enôÙ
gineering domains, for a wide range of uses, 
both in complex technological systems as 
well as for simple consumer products. It is 
an inductive, ãbottom-upã method, as it foôÙ
cuses on components, identifying how they 
can fail, and then analyses what these failôÙ
ures would mean for the whole system (DuiôÙ
jim, 2011).
 
Hazard identification is the act of finding 
all hazards; defined as situations or states 
that could lead to injury, death, damage or 
loss (Duijim, 2011). The definition implies 
threats toward human health is a primary 
focus. In this analysis however, the focus is 
not as much on identifying hazards towards 
humans, as it is on identifying hazards toôÙ
wards the system. In other words, we seek 
to find out everything that could go wrong 
with the system. FMECA is typically reported 
in tabular form. Each element is listed, and 
Code based on rules

concept detailing I chapter 11 I section 3
142
can most likely be detected by using data 
analysis. It would as an example be obvious 
to check for cheating if the same five IDs alôÙ
ways use the latrines together.
 
Connectivity
The system is rather dependent on some deôÙ
gree of access to the GSM network and the 
internet, as well as electricity. Connectivity 
is becoming a gradually smaller problem, but 
the FMECA shows us where the issue is highôÙ
est, and therefore where to prioritise efforts 
for countermeasures. The receiver, mounted 
at the latrines, has the most critical failure 
modes (D2 and D6). It is crucial, as it is the 
sole point for collecting data and a failure in 
this might result in beneficiaries not receivôÙ
ing their money. The receiver is placed in 
an exposed position at the latrines, and has 
the smallest memory capacity and battery 
back-up. It is therefore important to monitor 
these closely, have a back-up receiver, and 
an alarm or notification in case one receivôÙ
er hasnãt sent data for a specific amount of 
time. Less critical, but still important is the 
NGOs computer. It is easier to ensure conôÙ
nectivity for this machine, but crucial in orôÙ
der to be able to operate the system. At the 
refugee view, the failure mode B4 shows us 
that connectivity is very important as it gives 
access to the system and viewing personal 
information. This access might become very 
important for adaptation, as not having inôÙ
sight in the system and oneãs own data might 
make the user give up on the system, as the 
person assumes it doesnãt work.
 
Data security
When collecting large amounts of personal 
information, one must be aware of the huôÙ
man consequences if it fell into the wrong 
hands. These failure modes are present as 
C10 and C11. Refugees are often threatened 
by governments, religious or cultural groups 
and their personal information must thereôÙ
fore be treated with great care (DRC, 2017). 
Hacking is a probable hazard, which using 
blockchain technology makes much less 
probable, but still important to bear in mind. 
The greatest hazard is however the human 
system is chosen as the starting point for the 
FMECA analysis, as it is most tangible and 
also is where the system is in contact with 
humans, which is expected to be the most 
critical factor for the system.
 
The criticality analysis consists of ranking 
the severity of the failure modes in order to 
prioritise the countermeasures. The metric 
criticality, is a combination of the severity 
measure and frequency of occurrence. Each 
failure mode therefore is divided into a probôÙ
ability class between 0 and 0.2 and a severity 
class (I-IV) (Duijim, 2011; Standard IEC60812). 
The failure modes are colour coded, so the 
most important can be easily distinguished. 
 
The FMECA done for the Shatila case can 
be found in Appendix page 90. The most 
critical failure modes derived from the FMEôÙ
CA are described below.
Cheating
When there is a direct economic benefit 
for people, cheating will always be a factor 
to consider. When we are dealing with peoôÙ
ple in deep need, it might be even more so. 
The failure mode A10 highlights this issue. If 
the sticker is carried by someone else than 
the owner, the cash aid might be wrongfully 
distributed to this person. Several counterôÙ
measures can be taken against this failure 
mode to either lower the severity or the 
probability of it happening. The trade-off 
will be between easy usability for the refugee 
and security. One can implement additionôÙ
al check-points, such as refreshing the RFID 
registration once a week using an app. Or 
introduce a more secure registration, which 
could be making the rfid chip non-removable 
by e.g. building it into a bracelet, or installôÙ
ing iris scanners by the latrines. Instead of 
making additional check-point, the severity 
of the failure can be lowered by using closed 
loop money. If the money can only be used in 
the camp, there is a lower chance someone 
who has travelled on to some other place is 
benefiting. Moreover security measures at 
the point of use e.g. in the shop, can prevent 
cheating. And lastly, some of the cheating

Chapter 12 I Roskilde Festival Test
Key Messages
ãÂ	 It is very easy to recruit people to an experiment and collect 
their personal information, as long as a motivating reward is 
involved
ãÂ	 A one-time reward proved to be more motivating in this test. this 
is assumed to be due to the group mentality of the participants
ãÂ	 It is most important to make the system as seamless as possible, 
as complexity is a large contributor to people dropping out
Key Questions
ãÂ	 How difficult is it to recruit people to an experiment that involves 
giving up personal information?
ãÂ	 Is a continuous reward system or a one-time reward 
more motivating?
ãÂ	 Which factor is most important to optimise a system for, to ensure 
lasting participation?
==PAGE:160==
==PAGE:161==
roskilde festival test I chapter 12 I section 3
147
permarkets and food stalls. Similarly to a refôÙ
ugee camp, the Roskilde Festival is in some 
regards a quite chaotic situation, with thouôÙ
sands of people suddenly packed together in 
very little space. And the festival also expeôÙ
riences some of the same sanitation issues, 
that is present in refugee camps.
There are 2490 toilets at the festival, meanôÙ
ing the festival has over 52 persons per toilet 
[53]. This number is interesting compared to 
UNHCRãs standards, stating that in a severe 
crisis situation 50 persons per toilet is the 
highest acceptable number (UNHCR, 2017b). 
This chapter describes the test of the motiôÙ
vational system in combination with the data 
collection and analysis system (Figure 51). 
The test aimed to function as a demonstraôÙ
tion and evaluation of the system prototype. 
The idea is to set up an experiment which 
can simulate one of the many possible use 
cases to gain insight into the strengths and 
weaknesses of the generic concept. The folôÙ
lowing hypotheses are the focal point of the 
experiment.
 
ãÂ	
H1: People will give up a bit of personal inôÙ
formation and join a project, in order to 
gain a reward.
ãÂ	
H2: Data can be collected, even in a chaotôÙ
ic situation, and used to improve hygiene 
facilities.
ãÂ	
H3: Rewards can motivate people to use 
the hygiene facilities. 
ãÂ	
H4: Continuous, constant rewards give a 
higher incentive to use the hygiene faciliôÙ
ties, than one-time rewards.
 
12.1.	 Experiment Site
Roskilde Festival was chosen as the site for 
the experiment, as it is accessible and has 
characteristics which can emulate a refuôÙ
gee camp. Roskilde Festival is a 8-day music 
festival with 130.000 participants, of whom 
most camp at the site in tents for the whole 
period [53] . The campsite itself is just a bare 
field, but for the week it lasts, it becomes a 
city with its own infrastructure of shops, suôÙ
Chapter 12	 I Roskilde Festival Test

easier to explain and less transgressive for 
the participants. Participants were asked 
to take a picture of their hands making a 
gesture in front of the sinks and send it to 
a specified Facebook profile each time they 
washed their hands. Three test groups were 
subjected to the test. One test group was 
asked to participate actively in the system 
and got continuous reward for participaôÙ
tion. One test group only got a reward at the 
end of the testing period. The third group 
was a reference group, which merely filled 
out a questionnaire. The test groups were 
told that exploring hand washing behaviour 
was the sole purpose of the experiment, and 
were not told that other groups existed. We 
strived to ensure that all participants lived 
equally close to the handwashing facilities 
(Figure 52) and that each test group had the 
same demographic composition. Each test 
group consisted of approximately 50 people. 
Further specifications of the test groups and 
experiment design can be seen in Appendix 
page 96.
A simplified FMECA was moreover performed 
to function as a safety net, so a plan of action 
was in place for the most likely failure trigôÙ
gers and their related consequences during 
the experiment (Appendix page 103).
Public urination, leading to urine flooding 
the roads is common and almost completely 
socially accepted. Open defecation is likeôÙ
wise not a rarity. Diseases after the festival 
are common, but serious outbreaks are unôÙ
likely to happen [53].  The mentality of peoôÙ
ple at a huge party and in a refugee camp 
is naturally very different, but in one point 
they are similar. From the challenge analyôÙ
sis we know that many refugees believe they 
will only be at the camp for one week, and 
therefore feel very little responsibility for 
the common good. We argue that the same 
is present at Roskilde Festival, where this 
mentality of the situation being temporary 
leads many to treat the facilities with little 
consideration for the next visitor.
12.2.	 Experiment Design
The experiment was designed to try out difôÙ
ferent motivational systems and test how 
data about peopleãs behaviour can be exôÙ
tracted. The aim was to find a way to turn 
the participants into a data-source, and siôÙ
multaneously test if certain motivational inôÙ
terventions change their behaviour. 
 
The behaviour to affect was chosen to be 
hand washing, as it is close to the original 
concept of registering latrine usage, but

12.3.	 Results
The results are presented, according to the 
hypothesis they cover. 
H1: People will give up a bit of personal inforôÙ
mation and join a project, in order to gain a 
reward. 
When designing the experiment it was exôÙ
pected that the participants would give acôÙ
cess to their name and number of handwashôÙ
es, as well as basic demographics (Figure 
53 and Figure 54). But as participants were 
asked to befriend a specific Facebook profile 
to participate, an unforeseen side effect was 
access to much more personal information, 
such as friend lists, likes and photos. None of 
the participants questioned the anonymity 
or data protection of their information. The 
two groups however had different participaôÙ
tion rates.
In group one, seven camps were asked to 
participate adding up to approximately 50 
people in total. Of these, 37 people immediôÙ
ately accepted joining the experiment and 
befriended the Facebook-profile. However 
only 31 people filled in an initial questionôÙ
naire. 
In group two, six camps were asked to parôÙ
ticipate, of which one camp rejected. In the 
remaining five camps a total of 48 persons 
accepted joining the experiment, who all 
also befriended the Facebook-profile, and 
filled out an initial questionnaire. A few perôÙ
sons were not present in the camps when we 
visited them, and two requests for joining 
came later in the day. 
Of all these participants, only one person 
had unfriended the Facebook profile one 
week after the festival ended. 
To conclude, it was far easier than anticipatôÙ
ed to get participants for the experiment, 
especially for group two. The difference 
between group one and two may be due to 
group one being asked later in the day, when

H2: Data can be collected, even in a chaotic

The first part of this hypothesis was self-fulôÙ

to send data. In group one half of the parôÙ

periment, due to forgetfulness or because it 
was difficult for them to send the pictures. In 
group two, more than 80% reported forgetôÙ
ting to send pictures, and a few participants 
also reported a lack of interest in the reward. 
Their ability to send pictures was however 
also sometimes hindered by lack of power on 
their phones. From this we can conclude that 
it is important that the registration method 
is as seamless as possible, as people easily 
forget to register themselves.
Nevertheless, we were able to collect some 
data. The data is not big data, and as the 
group cannot be said to be statistically repôÙ
resentative, this experiment only shows the 
potential for making interventions.

The data could also be used to plan mainôÙ
tenance by analysing at what time the sinks 
are used the most. Figure 55 as an example 
shows that in the middle of the day and late 
in the evening the activity around the sinks is 
highest. This is a bit surprising as one might 
have expected hand washing to be most 
concentrated when people are expected to 
eat breakfast and dinner. Here we can see a 
good potential for adding more data sources, 
as this information e.g. could be linked with 
food stalls sales to investigate whether the 
cause is shifted eating times at the festival or 
is people simply donãt wash their hands beôÙ
fore eating. The figure also tells us that very 
many guests use the sinks at night, which 
could spark ideas for trying ways of commuôÙ
nicating with the guests at this time when it 
is dark and e.g. light signals or fluorescent 
paint could work. 
 
H3: Rewards can motivate people to use the 
hygiene facilities. 
 
A central idea in the test was that people 
would be more motivated to use the hygiene 
facilities if they received a reward for it. This 
could not be entirely proven in the experiôÙ
ment. The number of registered handwashes 
was simply too low (Figure 59), and in both 
groups more than half of the participants 
reported forgetting to register their handôÙ
washes. The expected number of handwashôÙ
es at Roskilde Festival was remarkably equal 
for both group one and two. Group two howôÙ
ever showed improvement both on expected 
number of handwashing at home and at the 
festival (Figure 57 and Figure 58). Especially 
the figure for handwashing at home is interôÙ
esting, as group two reports better handôÙ
washing habits at home than the reference 
group. 
 
H4: Continuous, constant rewards give a 
higher incentive to use the hygiene facilities, 
than one-time rewards.
 
This hypothesis was completely rejected, 
as group two accounted for 77% of all regôÙ
istrations, and kept a high registration rate

all throughout the experiment. This is obviôÙ
ous from Figure 60 and Figure 61. Group one 
starts out well, but the registrations drop 
dramatically.  Both groups received messagôÙ
es every day as reminders to keep registerôÙ
ing, but group two responded far better to 
the experiment. One reason for this could 
be that we underrated how big the social 
effect would be. Group two had a promise 
to receive the reward, as long as everyone 
in the group participated. This way there 
was a feeling of responsibility for the group. 
From this we can conclude that the chance 
of winning something is not as good as beôÙ
ing certain to get a reward. It is also interôÙ
esting to reflect on the group mentality. It 
is the common responsibility that drives the 
participants to a certain behaviour. However 
the common responsibility only extends to 
smaller groups, as we know from other beôÙ
haviour at festivals, refugee camps and elseôÙ
where, that humans donãt always do what is 
best for the group, even though it is best for 
themselves in the end. 
 
12.4.	 Sources of Error
There are several sources of error, which 
could affect the result of the experiment. 
The most obvious source of error is that the 
participants were under the influence of 
alcohol. To minimise the effect, the particôÙ
ipants were recruited as early in the mornôÙ
ing as possible. It did however seem to affect 
the participants recruited latest in the day, 
which were mainly participants for group 
one. There is also always the error source 
that the observer can affect the result, simôÙ
ply by observing; also called the Hawthorne 
effect [55]. All groups were asked to fill out 
the questionnaire while we as the observôÙ
ers were close to them, which might have 
affected their answers. The groups one and 
two report the largest difference between 
habits at home and habits at the festival, 
which might strengthen the hypothesis that 
the participants become more aware of their 
lack of handwashing by being in the experôÙ
iment. Lastly, all handwash registration atôÙ
tempts were accepted, even though the 
rules had been set so that special handgesôÙ
Figure 60.	Group 1 handwash registrations by 
name. 
Figure 61.	Group 2 handwash registrations by 
name. 
tures should be made each day. Too few of 
the participants had understood these rules. 
This strengthen the assumption that the 
registration method must be extremely easy 
or even completely seamless for the user. 
12.5.	 Conclusion
During the experiment, we have gained exôÙ
perience with set-up of a pilot project, and 
found useful results which can be used to 
strengthen the concept. The most importôÙ
ant conclusion is that the more seamless a 
data collection method is, the better, since 
it seems to be a large problem that people 
forget to participate if it is too advanced. 
Furthermore, it is important not to underôÙ
rate the power of group mentality, and that 
people are more likely to actively participate 
in something if their involvement somehow 
affects not only themselves, but also their 
peers.

In Chapter 12, the motivational system in

In this chapter, the other half of the concept 
will be tried out; the collaboration system, 
in combination with the data collection and 
analysis system (Figure 62). This was done 
through three workshops with the Danish 
Refugee Council (DRC), Danish Red Cross 
and Danish Church Aid. The main purpose

what use they could find for it, specifically 
with regards to improving conditions in a 
camp, and collaborating with other NGOs or 
external partners.

The three workshops had a similar approach 
and structure. They were held within the 
same month, but had slightly differing modôÙ

lowing we specify with methods were used, 
and what specific focus each organisation 
had. The workshops were all facilitated in a 
semi-structured manner, keeping the parôÙ
ticipants on topic but also allowing for them 
to speak their mind freely. The rest of the 
chapter includes quotes from the workôÙ
shops. These are included to illustrate the 
discussion, but are not all necessarily direct 
quotes.

wrong with the type, location or condition of

Camp layout and type of latrine
When having an overview of all the latrines 
in the camp and data on how often they are 
used, it can be detected if some latrines are 
visited more than others. If some latrines 
have a very large use rate, it can be investiôÙ
gated why these are better than the others, 
and the attractive advantages can perhaps 
be transferred to other latrines. If some laôÙ
trines have a low use rate, it can be investiôÙ
gated why they are not attractive to use, e.g. 
through discussion with the refugees living 
near them. The refugees can then have a say 
on how they think the latrines can be imôÙ
proved. 
Optimisation of daily operation
If the use rate of the latrines is known, it can 
be optimised when the latrines are emptied, 
so that none almost empty latrines are takôÙ
en care of while others are almost full and 
not getting emptied. Since it most often is an 
external service provider who empties the 
latrines, it will be ordering this service that 
is optimised. 
Evaluate interventions
Interventions can be evaluated using before 
and after data. It can e.g. be if an intervention 
was made to minimise a safety threat relatôÙ
ed with going to the latrine, such as womôÙ
en getting assaulted on their way. Then the 
use rate before and after, for the latrines in 
question, can be used to evaluate the interôÙ
vention. It can also be a hygiene promotion 
intervention focusing on eliminating open 
defecation, where the impact likewise can be 
evaluated through looking at the discrepanôÙ
cy in use rate before and after. 
13.2.	 Desired Data
When asked about what data could be interôÙ
esting to collect for the residents of a refuôÙ
gee camp, the participants mentioned sevôÙ
eral demographic factors; age, gender, area 
of origin, if they have kids, are pregnant, are 
single mothers, and where they live in the 
camp. The interest in pregnancy and single 
mothers shows that there is a special interôÙ
est in the more vulnerable selection of the 
population. The NGOs mentioned that a wide 
range of schemes are used to determine an 
individuals needs and whether they are eligiôÙ
ble for aid. Some schemes are standardised 
while some varied depending on the situaôÙ
tion. The administrative work in filling them 
out and interpreting them was mentioned as 
a burden, but it is important to have this type 
of information. The factors mentioned above 
would be part of an online ID, and in the HyôÙ
giene Data Reward System linked to the inôÙ
dividual personãs registrations of latrine use. 
The registration would include the time and 
location of the visit. 
13.3.	 Interventions Based on Data
The workshops consisted of a professional 
discussion on what the data could be used 
for. Imagining that the above mentioned 
data was at hand, the participants came up 
with multiple application ideas. Analysis of 
the data could be used to improve the conôÙ
ditions in a camp, and making specific interôÙ
ventions. Other types of advantages are deôÙ
scribed later on.  
13.3.1.	Sanitation Improvements
With regards to sanitation and hygiene, the 
data could be used for the following purposôÙ
es.

Use linked to demographics
When knowing how many women, men and 
children are present in the camp, it can be 
analysed who is using the latrines and how 
often they do it. If some groups are not usôÙ
ing the latrines, an investigation can be made 
into why this is. There can e.g. be something 
ã We will normally get [insight into disôÙ
crepancies] through consultation, 
but now we can also get quantitative 
data to support it.

13.3.2.	General Camp Improvements
Other than the already mentioned improveôÙ
ments, the participants mentioned the folôÙ
lowing additional improvements:
Replace existing registration
The registrations of latrine use might make 
some of the questions which are currently 
filled out on the day of arrival unnecessary. 
As it is now, each individual or family goes 
through a registration process, noting down 
names and all information relevant for their 
stay in the camp. The latrine registration 
might be able to replace noting down the 
day of arrival or the sector in which they live, 
since this can be derived from the timestamp 
and location of the latrine registration. 
We would not have to bother 
them with all these questions
Identify sources of error
Just as it is visible who is using the system 
and generating data, it will also be possible 
to see who is not using the system. An analôÙ
ysis can then be made into why this group 
or individual is not using the Hygiene Data 
Reward System. The reason for them not usôÙ
ing this system might also be the reason why 
some other activities or interventions are 
not successful for this individual or group. 
Identify unaccompanied children
If a young child has been registered and is 
using the system, and that child is always 
going to the latrine alone (meaning there is 
no correlation between the childãs registraôÙ
tions and an adultãs registrations), it might 
indicate that the child has no parents to take 
them. This child could then be sought out 
and given the necessary attention. 
13.4.	 Positive Remarks
The participants had the following positive 
remarks about the collaboration system in 
combination with the data collection and 
analysis system.
Supplement to consultation
A widely used approach in refugee camps 
today is to have conversations with the refuôÙ
gees, both in terms of registering them upon 
arrival and continuously throughout their 
stay in the camp. This involvement of refôÙ
ugees is both rewarding in terms of ensurôÙ
ing participation from refugees, and making 
them feel more in control and less like benôÙ
eficiaries. It is however also time consuming, 
and the results that come from it might be 
inaccurate due to confusion or mistrust beôÙ
tween the refugees and NGO employees. If 
the consultation could be supported by acôÙ
curate data, it would give another dimension 
to the work they do. It would also mean that 
the NGOs have to bother the refugees less 
with a long range of questions and surveys, 
if they can get the same results through data 
collection.
You are sure that the data is accurate. 
Perhaps sometimes people lie, sometimes 
they just think they are doing things difôÙ
ferently than they are.
Equal representation in data
In relation to the consultation approach 
above, it is an issue that certain demoôÙ
graphic groups are not represented among 
the refugees that communicate with NGOs. 
This is for example women, children, elderly 
and disabled. If a data collection system is in 
place where the majority of the camp popuôÙ
lation is registered, then the data will also be 
representing all the demographic groups in 
the camp according to their respective size. 
It will not only be the loudest or most promôÙ
inent persons that are heard. 
Everyone is equally represented. It is e.g. 
not only the men talking
ã
ã
ã

ngo perspectives I chapter 13 I section 3
159
Cheaper and optimised operation
If the NGO has its own system for distribôÙ
uting cash aid programmes, it will be much 
cheaper than todayãs methods. Many large 
banking and financial services have too 
heavy fees for it to be feasible to run a large 
scale cash-based aid programme. FurtherôÙ
more, it will be possible for service providers 
to access information which can give them 
insight into what needs a specific refugee 
camp has, and thus target their service to 
meet these needs. It will both be cheaper for 
the service provider and better for the refuôÙ
gee camp. 
Tracing funds
The possibility for having accurate audit 
trails was stressed as a much needed new 
feature. It would make it possible both for 
NGOs and donors to have better control over 
the money. This would help answer some of 
the questions the NGOs have, regarding how 
many people they are helping and what kind 
of impact it has had. 
We think we know where the money 
goes, but we actually have very little idea 
about where the money goes. I think the 
donors will be very interested in seeing 
where the money goes, since you can acôÙ
tually track every dollar... right?
Refugeesã rights are improved
The online ID and wallet, which is also accesôÙ
sible to the refugees, can shift what informaôÙ
tion the refugees have to give up in order to 
receive aid, and how much they are still in 
control of this information. The procedure 
today is, that the refugees give up their inôÙ
formation to the NGO employees, who then 
type it in the NGOãs data sheet or program. 
The refugee has from that point on no conôÙ
trol of their data. If their data is stored in an 
online ID, they will be able to access it themôÙ
selves, and due to the structure of the blockôÙ
chain code they will also in some way still 
own their own data, as it is linked to their ID. 
They are being registered and logged as 
it is now, without any insight into 
what is going on. With blockchain they 
will at least get that.
Another positive effect of the refugees havôÙ
ing more insight into their data and how it 
is used is that it might become easier to get 
consent from them to actually collect and 
use their data in the first place. This effect 
will be enhanced if the refugees also have 
some additional benefits from being part of 
the system. It could e.g. be if they can use 
their online ID to book appointments for a 
health clinic faster than if they have to do it 
through an NGO employee. 
It will be easier to get the
 consent from the refugees, if they 
also benefit from it.
Adjustability to context
The NGOs all work in a wide range of difôÙ
ferent countries with varying context speôÙ
cific needs. As a result, they put great emôÙ
phasis on the need of flexibility, and were 
enthusiastic about the possibility of having 
some standard code modules which could be 
tweaked according to the situation.

Right now it looks like blockchain makes 
the NGOs stop talking to the refugees. 
Participation is important!
Benefits are mainly for NGOs
The main goal for NGOs, is to make life betôÙ
ter for people. It therefore seems contraôÙ
dicting that the largest benefits for having a 
data collection system based on blockchain 
technology is for the NGOs. Since many of 
the larger benefits of this system are quite 
abstract and figure on a high level in the 
organisation, it is a bit difficult to pinpoint 
exactly which problems it solves for the refôÙ
ugees, which can not be solved with any exôÙ
isting methods today. 
What is it that blockchain can do for the 
refugees, which is not being done now? 
Or is it what [it] can do for NGOs? 
We have to put the refugees first.
Setup and operation of system
The practicalities regarding setting up the 
system and deciding how it should function, 
what data to collect, and filling out the points 
of variation, is also a source of concern. It is 
difficult to determine who has the necesôÙ
sary knowledge and skills to make these deôÙ
cisions, as you risk putting too much power 
into the hands of a limited group of people. 
Who will sit down and 
make that decision?
Another inevitable concern is the fear for a 
breakdown of the whole system. This risk is 
present in all IT systems around the world, 
so it is not something new for this type of 
system. It will however make the work of 
NGOs extra vulnerable to such breakdowns, 
if all data storage, value and means of transôÙ
actions is linked to the same system. 
What if it all just breaks down? Like 
Skynet in Terminator 3!
13.5.	 Limitations and Concerns
Along with all the possibilities and positive 
remarks, the participants also mentioned a 
range of limitations and concerns connected 
with having such a system:
Data collection and security
When discussing the affordance of blockôÙ
chain, Big Data and analytics, it often applies 
that the more data you have to work with, 
the better. This is conflicting with a principle 
that both DRC and DRK mentioned, which is 
that data should only be collected if it has a 
specific purpose. Sometimes it is hard for the 
NGOs to get as much as the name and age 
out of people, and this will only get harder if 
more information is to be collected without 
having a good explanation for why it should 
be done
We have a principle: We do not collect 
data without having a specific use of the 
data in mind
Furthermore, it is an inevitable concern that 
the personal information falls into the wrong 
hands. Especially if a considerable amount 
of personal data is collected and linked to an 
online ID. The blockchain system might be 
very hard to hack, but that does not elimiôÙ
nate the risk of an NGO employee extractôÙ
ing the personal data, e.g. due to threats or 
bribe, and sending it to someone who should 
not have access to it. 
Weaker communication with refugees
As described in supplement to consultation, 
it is possible to minimise some of the face to 
face conversations with refugees by replacôÙ
ing the desired information with a data colôÙ
lection initiative. This is however not entirely 
a good trait, as it might give a sense of the 
NGOs not wanting to talk with the refugees 
anymore, and thus not prioritising their parôÙ
ticipation in the refugee camp. It is importôÙ
ant to remember that the consultation part 
can not and should not be eliminated comôÙ
pletely.
ã
ã
ã
ã
ã
==PAGE:175==
ngo perspectives I chapter 13 I section 3
161
We have built so many systems which only 
work in the camp, and they donãt help the 
refugees in the long run.
Hold governments accountable
Since most refugees are located in develôÙ
oping countries, it is often an issue that the 
local governments are not doing their part 
in providing aid for the many refugees. The 
reason for this can of course be that they 
do not have the resources, but it is often 
suspected that it is due to the government 
turning a blind eye to the crisis.  If the NGOs 
can present a set of bulletproof data analysis 
results and statistics of the crisis, it can be 
used as proof to convince the governments 
to step up and do what they can to help. 
If you have the right type of data you can 
put it right in the face of the people who 
actually have to do this, instead of the 
NGOs. These are the needs. 
Start working!
13.6.	 Long Term Advantages
In addition to the positive remarks about 
the short term impacts, the participants also 
mentioned a range of long term advantages 
with using such a system. These advantages 
are more abstract, but are nevertheless inôÙ
teresting and important to consider.
Integration with existing systems
Humanitarian work today is often characôÙ
terised by functioning really well when the 
organisations are present in the vulnerable 
area, and then leaving behind a community 
which have a very hard time coping on their 
own once the aid is no longer present. This is 
especially true for refugee camps. This issue 
can be addressed with initiatives to prepare 
the refugees for ãreal lifeã after the refugee 
camp, and also by cooperating with existing 
governmental or financial institutions in the 
respective country. By establishing an online 
ID and wallet for the refugees, and trying to 
incorporate this with e.g. the social security 
system or national bank, the refugees have 
a better chance to become included in the 
society later in their life. 
Picture 33.	Group discussion at DRC Workshop
ã
ã
==PAGE:176==
ngo perspectives I chapter 13 I section 3
162
Small players in a large game
Even though NGOs are non-governmental 
and strive to be neutral with regards to polôÙ
itics and conflicts, they can not completely 
avoid all involvement in any political conôÙ
flicts. Especially not if they are helping those 
who are fleeing from war, or directly workôÙ
ing in war zones, which is very often the case 
with refugee camps. This makes them a very 
small player in a game dominated by powerôÙ
ful and aggressive players. Since they do not 
have neither the resources nor a focus on 
creating their systems and data storage to 
be resistant towards these powerful players, 
they do not have much of a chance to proôÙ
tect themselves from potential attacks. With 
blockchain technology, they might be able to 
take a step towards something that is just a 
little more resistant than the methods they 
are using now. And if more NGOs collaborate 
better, the joining of forces might also enable 
them to stand stronger in the large game. 
We are a very small player in a very 
large game. Right now, if the US 
government wants to hack our data, 
there is nothing we can do. With 
blockchain we would have a better 
chance withstanding this.
The network effect of blockchain
The use of blockchain in humanitarian work 
is a direct argument for NGOs to start colôÙ
laborating more. This is due to the network 
effects of blockchain and the data analysis, 
where more participants and data equals 
more positive outcomes. If more NGOs have 
a project with similar needs, they will be able 
to co-develop the necessary code and setôÙ
up of a blockchain, and share the risks and 
costs. This is also strengthened by the fact 
that the cost of setting up the system is large 
compared to the cost of running the system 
afterwards. 
Blockchain has network effects. 
The more participants, the more value.
13.7.	 Final Remarks
These final remarks mentioned by the parôÙ
ticipants are more related to the work of a 
humanitarian organisation in general, and 
what issues they are facing in this ever 
changing world. They are not supposed to be 
perceived as problems which can be solved 
with the system we propose, but are mereôÙ
ly included as they give a good insight into 
what potential ripple effect an implementaôÙ
tion of a relatively small project, along with a 
change in IT system, can have. 
Already existing problems
The whole aspect of collecting data and the 
related problems with security and handling 
personal information is something which is 
present in humanitarian work today. It is not 
a new problem which arises with the proôÙ
posed system. On the contrary, the new sysôÙ
tem will most likely be more safe than the 
current methods used today, and will at the 
same time offer a large range of new opporôÙ
tunities. 
We think it is safe because it is an 
Excel sheet. Thatãs not true.

section 4

Introduction to Section 4
Research Question
Section 3 consisted of the development of the concept, presentation of the Hygiene 
Data Reward System and various tests of it. Section 4 will lay the foundation for, and 
touch upon a second descriptive study by giving recommendations and reflecting. 
The following research question will be answered:
RQ4: How can we inspire humanitarian organisations 
to use new technology? 
Outline
First, we will make a presentation of the final concept. This includes a four-pager 
which is targeted to NGOs, and gives a short description of the key outcomes of this 
thesis. This works as an inspiration for NGOs and our final product. Second, we will 
conclude on the whole thesis and give our recommendation for further work. Lastly, 
we will reflect on both the concept, the methods used and our process.
Outcome
When having finished reading this section, you will have a thorough understanding 
of the final concept, and why it is relevant for NGOs to look into the concept and the 
underlying technology. You will have recapitulated the work done in this thesis, and 
an idea of the future possibilities within this field of work. 
==PAGE:180==
==PAGE:181==
concept presentation I chapter 14 I section 4
167
enable all the benefits that the NGOs have 
shown interest in. Since the workshop parôÙ
ticipants did not have a thorough insight 
into the technicalities of blockchain, further 
details have not been specified. 
In the future, we recommend looking more 
into giving refugees more control and 
strengthening their rights. It has been found 
that these benefits are just as significant 
as receiving a more tangible reward, such 
as cash. This opens up some opportunities 
in the future when mobile phones are even 
more common property than they are now. 
If the refugees can be part of the system and 
access data through their mobile phones, 
instead having to carry a chip, the system 
could become even more seamless, and free 
from additional technical artefacts. The chip 
is thus not something that necessarily should 
be carefully considered and developed in poôÙ
tential future projects; it is merely the best 
available technology for the concept now. 
14.1.	 Four-pager
The concept has been updated with the 
mentioned specifications and changes, and 
has now reached its final form in this thesis. 
It will be presented in the next four pages, 
which are  designed to be used separately as 
a source of inspiration, or to facilitate disôÙ
cussions in humanitarian NGOs. They presôÙ
ent the specific advantages NGOs can have 
by using blockchain technology, and give 
ideas for application possibilities. They furôÙ
thermore present the concept as an inspiraôÙ
tion and kick-off for further development. 
The concept was tested both at Roskilde 
Festival and through NGO workshops. This 
resulted in the following specifications and 
changes:
The test at Roskilde Festival showed that it 
was very important that the solution is as 
seamless as possible. The concept is thereôÙ
fore adjusted to always register the refugeesã 
visits to the latrines automatically, by regisôÙ
tering a chip they carry. Previously a check-
in registration had also been considered, but 
the Roskilde Festival showed that the most 
seamless possibility was preferable. It is furôÙ
thermore recommended that NGOs take 
group mentality into consideration. This 
can be done by signing up whole families or 
groups, instead of targeting the individual 
refugees. The Roskilde Festival showed that 
a feeling of responsibility towards the group 
was a strong incentive to stay and participate 
in the programme. Even though the concept 
is not as dependent on a whole group particôÙ
ipating in order to gain something, as it was 
with the Roskilde Festival experiment, impleôÙ
menting a community aspect could possibly 
strengthen the motivation for becoming and 
staying in the system. 
The professional discussions with NGOs gave 
us the idea to replace ãrewardã with ãbeneôÙ
fitã. It turned out that ãhanding out rewardsã 
had too large associations with handing out 
cold cash. The concept is flexible enough to 
allow for many other types of rewards, othôÙ
er than just cash, and the word ãbenefitã is 
hence found more suitable. 
The NGO discussions have also contributôÙ
ed to specifying which type of blockchain is 
most suitable for the concept. A consortium 
blockchain with known users, in a permisôÙ
sioned network, is estimated to be most suitôÙ
able. This type allows for multiple different 
users to participate, which is important as 
NGOs are large organisations. It also has the 
necessary level of security, since it functions 
in a supervised environment. A permissioned 
consortium blockchain is versatile enough to

The value of blockchain* 
for NGOs
Whatãs blockchain?
Ease collaboration
Increase independence
Ensure authenticity
*and Big Data
Blockchain can easiest be comôÙ
pared to a kind of Google Drive, 
except there is no ãGoogleã who 
has control over the data, and the 
network also works for transactions 
of value, not just sharing informaôÙ
Blockchain can ensure there is only one version of 
a file or information. When the rules are set, they 
cannot be broken, which enables collaboration 
even when there is no trust. All organisations can 
use the blockchain in their own way, but still be part 
of the common system. 
Once it has been set up, nobody really owns a 
blockchain system or can alter it to benefit from it 
themself. It can therefore free NGOs from depenôÙ
dence on governments or corporations. The high 
security of blockchain can ensure data doesnãt fall 
into the wrong hands.
Empower people
Very secure IDs can be created, which are not in 
danger of being lost or altered. Coupled with a digôÙ
ital wallet, all transactions can be minutely tracked, 
so e.g. donors can follow their funds all the way to 
the beneficiaries. 
tion. Blockchain is a decentralised, 
transparent and autonomous digital 
system for exchanging value and 
information. It uses a unique code, 
which secures the system through 
cryptography. Over time the transôÙ
action history in the system gets 
locked in blocks of data that are 
cryptographically linked together. 
This creates an immutable record of 
all the transactions across the netôÙ
work. 
The transparency in the system can give people 
better control over their own data, and how it is 
used. Moreover, data represents people equally, 
and doesnãt favor the strongest or loudest in the 
community. 
This technology enables 
a greater accountability 
for us and our donors. 
We could track funds 
in real time and tell you 
that the 100DKK you 
donated yesterday were 
spent on rice and tuition 
fees today 
- Kaspar Bro Larsen,
Danish Red Cross

Speed up response time
Blockchain can provide data, which can be used in 
valuable Big Data analysis that can support deciôÙ
sion making. The scare resources can be spent betôÙ
ter, and by pooling data with other organisations, 
they can learn not only from own, but also othersã 
successes and mistakes.
Target funds
As all kinds of value can be transferred on the 
blockchain, it can become the perfect middleway 
between vouchers and cash. Some funds can be 
earmarked, while other can be restriction free, but 
all used from the same wallet.

==PAGE:186==
Hygiene Data Benefit System
- a case example
A digital ID is created for each individual. 
The refugees can access the ID by using 
simple text messaging or possibly by a 
more advanced app. Important documents 
are securely stored, and can not be stolen 
or altered. The app gives control over oneãs 
own data, and who it is shared with.
A low-tech RFID device incorporated into a 
sticker or bracelet registers the owner each 
time they use the latrines. A receiving deôÙ
vice with internet connection and appropriôÙ
ate reading distance sends the data in real 
time, writing it into the blockchain.
The benefit is dispersed. It could be a cash 
aid program where the eligibility is obtained 
by using the system. This money is spent in 
a store, using e.g. mobile money or plastic 
cards. Some aid could be earmarked, only 
to be used in specific stores or for specific 
purposes.
The 
advantages 
of 
blockchain 
technology are here concretised 
in a concept which addresses the 
challenge of inadequate access to 
latrines in a refugee camp. This conôÙ
cept is one of many possible appliôÙ
cations, and is meant as a specific 
example for inspiration. 
The core of the concept is to motiôÙ
vate refugees to use the latrines by 
offering them a benefit, and at the 
same time registering when the refôÙ
ugees visit the latrines, and what 
they do with the benefit. The data 
generated from this unlocks a large 
range of possible data analyses and 
consequent interventions. 
The refugee point of view of the 
concept is described with the drawôÙ
ings below. The whole concept, 
including the data collection and 
analysis and collaboration aspects 
are elaborated to the right. 
==PAGE:187==
Motivate
Collect data and analyse
Collaborate
Benefit
A digital benefit is disôÙ
bursed to the refugees 
when they use the 
latrines. This is done by 
registering their 
presence at the latrines
Give control
The digital identity 
enables the refugees 
to have access to their 
own data, and insight 
into how it is used
Feedback
When the refugees are 
registered when using 
the latrines, they indiôÙ
rectly give feedback on 
the state and quality of 
the latrines
Seamless
Making the registration of 
latrine visits as automatic 
as possible, a larger and 
more accurate amount of 
data is secured
Sanitation statistics
The latrine use registraôÙ
tions give statistics on 
which latrines are being 
used the most, at what 
time, and by whom
Digital identity
The reward is linked 
to their digital identity. 
This account can 
enable financial incluôÙ
sion for refugees in the 
long run
Camp population
Having the refugees 
register themselves at 
least once a day gives 
an overview of the size 
and demographics of 
the camp
Refugee needs
Analysing how the 
refugees spend the 
reward gives an 
overview of what they 
need the most
Knowledge sharing
By using a shared platform, 
it becomes easier to share 
knowledge and aggregate 
data related to latrine use 
and camp demographics
Long term decisions
By collecting large datasets 
including several different 
refugee camps, more long 
term decisions can be guidôÙ
ed by what the data says
Independency
The disbursement of rewards 
is not dependent on an exterôÙ
nal money transfer company. 
Instead more organisations 
can use the same system
