What now?
Blockchain technology is still at its early stages, even though 
many companies, particularly in the financial sector, have reôÙ
cently come up with real world use cases. However, the time 
for NGOs to start looking seriously into implementation is maôÙ
ture. According to the Gartner Hype Cycle 2016, blockchain 
technology will be mainstream in 5-10 years. NGOs should 
start influencing now how the technology will develop, in orôÙ
der to ensure that it will cater to their needs in the future. 
But how to get started? NGOs have a big burden to lift in orôÙ
der to keep up with the digital development. We believe the 
best way to do this is to form partnerships and collaborate, 
both with private companies and especially other NGOs. OthôÙ
er NGOs are facing the exact same challenges. By pooling 
resources, the risk and cost of implementing new technology 
can be shared. Private companies, on the other hand, have to 
adapt their methods and understand that an NGO is not like 
their typical customer, in order to cater to their needs. 
Want to know more? This leaflef is an extract of a thesis, 
which among other things explores how blockchain can be 
used in refugee camps. You are welcome to contact us with 
any questions, or request the whole report. 
==PAGE:189==
conclusion and further work I chapter 15 I section 4
175
operations is not easy. We recommend huôÙ
manitarian organisations to take advantage 
of the new technological possibilities, and 
ease this process by creating partnerships. 
These partnerships could be with private 
companies, which are experts in the techôÙ
nology and can provide long term services. 
But if the wish for independence weights 
higher, partnerships should be created with 
other NGOs. Collaboration is central for getôÙ
ting an outcome of high quality in data projôÙ
ects, due to the network effects of Big Data. 
At the same time, the costs and risks of takôÙ
ing a chance on a new project can be can be 
spread on all the collaborating partners.
To conclude on the core objective, we believe 
that high-tech solutions can indeed contribôÙ
ute to relieving the sanitation challenges in 
a refugee camp. In addition, we believe that 
high-tech solutions have many other suitôÙ
able applications in refugee camps. Coupling 
of blockchain with cash-based aid was found 
to be a straightforward match, and the idea 
of having a digital ID for refugees has reôÙ
ceived wide interest from several NGOs.
Beside the recommendations addressed to 
NGOs, we would also like to conclude and 
give our academic recommendations for poôÙ
tential future project teams:
Expand the system boundaries
In the introduction of this project we argue 
why we set the boundaries of the project 
to only include the activities inside a refuôÙ
gee camp. This was the correct choice for a 
project of this scope, but for further develôÙ
opment it will be necessary and beneficial 
to explore beyond these boundaries. 75% of 
refugees donãt live in refugee camps, and orôÙ
ganisations like the UN work intensively to 
ensure that refugee camps will only be the 
last resort if no other accommodation is 
possible. There is also a strong political focus 
on including refugees in the host communiôÙ
ties. Moreover, one must remember that the 
refugee camp is hopefully a temporary situôÙ
ation for the individual and that the person 
This thesis took its starting point in the sanôÙ
itation challenges in a refugee camp context, 
and sought to find out whether high-tech 
solutions can contribute to ensuring adeôÙ
quate sanitation in planned refugee camps. 
We found that humanitarian organisations 
struggle in ensuring capability to handle 
crises, such as disease outbreaks or huge 
numbers of people arriving at the same time. 
Moreover, refugees are not motivated to use 
the facilities and therefore often defecate in 
the open. The problems were complex and 
interconnected, but were mapped using a 
combination of causal loop diagrams and 
UACs to get an overview of and deep insight 
into the challenges. 
The result was a suggestion for a three-fold 
intervention, with systems that address difôÙ
ferent challenges for the refugees, the WASH 
management in a camp, and the NGOs in 
general. A broad research into the solution 
space, using case studies as well as deep 
research into new emerging technologies, 
gave a foundation for creating concepts. 
This resulted in the Hygiene Data Benefit 
System, a system concept whose core lies in 
creating a motivational system for refugees 
which is simultaneously used to collect data 
for sanitation interventions. The concept 
and tools for developing it further have been 
tested and evaluated through an experiment 
at Roskilde Festival, and through workshops 
with the Danish Church Aid, Danish Red 
Cross and the Danish Refugee Council. 
We recommend NGOs to develop a pilot 
project which focuses on only one context, 
one specific refugee camp, or one specific 
problem. The previous four pages work as a 
idea catalouge and starting point for NGOs 
to apply new technical solutions.
We know that for many organisations, usôÙ
ing technology to this degree is not yet a 
natural part of how the organisation works, 
and that the feat of getting digital solutions 
implemented across culture, strategy and

16.1.	 Reflection on the Concept
This reflection relates to learning objective:
8. Create a range of tangible concepts that 
generate value in a humanitarian context 
and relate these solutions to the Sustainable 
Development Goals.
The Hygiene Data Benefit System is an examôÙ
ple of a way that humanitarian organisations 
can implement some of the new technologôÙ
ical advancements which are emerging. The 
concept is unique because it integrates three 
systems with different subgoals, but which 
all still strive to improve sanitation. It also 
reverses the idea of paying for accessing a 
facility and instead gives a benefit. It is a new 
thought in a humanitarian setting, but the 
concept of giving up data to get access to a 
benefit is being increasingly used in internet 
services.
The system is still at an early stage of conôÙ
cept development, and it will take several itôÙ
erations to bring it to the point where it can 
be used on a large scale. However, the way 
the concept has been described using UACs 
and points of variation gives NGOs a clear 
point of departure for future development. 
The strength of the concept lies in its flexiôÙ
bility. Even though it meant that the concept 
cannot be further detailed in this thesis, the 
outline of the concept can readily be used 
for a future project within a specific context. 
It is moreover interesting to see whether the 
concept could be used in a context different 
from a refugee camp. It could for example be 
tried out in a village setting, similar to the 
One Stop Shop case from Uganda. This has 
been discussed with the Danish Red Cross, 
and we believe there is potential for further 
exploration. In such a case, the point of variôÙ

16.2.	 Reflection the Methods
Ideating a system concept
This reflection relates to learning objective: 
1. Form a thorough understanding of relevant 
design theories and methods by seeking out 
and selecting relevant scientific research.
We feel that the work done in the literature 
study and the following usage of the deôÙ

and describe theories, and also use them in 
practice in different ways. The use of methôÙ

During our education in the Design and InôÙ

exclusively been on developing products. 
However, in this thesis the goal was to develôÙ

complex in its nature. The use of Product/
Service-Systems theory was a great conôÙ

concept of PSS had a good fit with the huôÙ
manitarian context. But even though PSS fit 
the project well, we still found it rather diffiôÙ
cult to find applicable methods from our edôÙ

cially idea generation. We felt like we knew 
quite a lot about using systems and finding 
information from systems, but less about 
conceptualising them. The use of System 
Dynamics theory was a great contribution to 
the analytical part of the project. We believe 
it is a very useful tool that could very well 
be added to the standard Design and InnovaôÙ
tion ãtoolboxã. However, as much helpful as 
it was with the analysis, it did not contribute 
much to the conceptualisation. 
We overcame this challenge through many 
considerations on which parts of the known 
methods were suitable for system concepôÙ
tualisation, and which had to be dismissed. 
We e.g. decided to use some categories from 
the PROTEUS Offering cards, which could be 
used for systems, and put other categories

el. Furthermore, illustrations were added to 
each steps to ease the understanding even 
more. Similarly the TTS chain was altered to 
include icons instead of the somewhat crypôÙ
tic abbreviation ãHsã, ãTsã etc. Colours were 
also added to illustrate which actors played a 
role in the respective systems. 
We think that this way of alternating models 
for a desired purpose has worked quite well, 
with regards to having clear communication 
with multidisciplinary stakeholders. HowevôÙ
er, it is important to keep the academic asôÙ
pect in mind in a project like this, and also 
make a ãcorrectã version of the alternated 
models to show that the academic knowlôÙ
edge is still in place. We believe we have kept 
this balance at a suitable level. 
==PAGE:196==
reflection I chapter 16 I section 4
182
We were also extremely fortunate to get 
many stakeholders involved in the project, 
but this also meant that it was necessary to 
be flexible with some of the project deadôÙ
lines. This is always a trade-off when workôÙ
ing with many actors, and we were prepared 
for most of these types of delays. It was imôÙ
portant for us to be flexible in order to enôÙ
sure the collaboration and we therefore alôÙ
tered our plans and interview goals in order 
to make the meetings work.
For all workshops as well as interviews we 
made an interview guide and timeplan. We 
distributed roles and discussed strategies 
for what to do if the conversation went 
astray. After a workshop session, we usualôÙ
ly took half an hour to write down the most 
important points, before engaging in other 
activities. When later reviewing the minutes, 
it was thereby easier to remember what imôÙ
pression we left the meeting with. For each, 
an appendix with the most important points 
was created. This was our systematic way of 
documenting and evaluating the work, which 
hopefully can give readers of this thesis inôÙ
sight into the process.
A new technical field
This reflection relates to learning objectives:
4. Acquire a rudimentary understanding 
of Big Data, blockchain and the Internet of 
Things as well as the technical possibilities 
they provide
The three technological phenomena menôÙ
tioned in the learning objective, were all new 
to the authors of this thesis, and quite out of 
the scope of what type of engineering fields 
our education has focused on. It was thereôÙ
fore both exciting but also demanding to take 
on these digital technologies. We believe 
the engineering education gives us compeôÙ
tences which are broad enough to enable us 
to get a good grip of these technologies. It 
might even have been beneficial for us not 
to be too deep into the technologies, in orôÙ
der to be more creative with the application 
16.3.	 Reflection on the Process
This reflection relates to learning objectives:
10. Independently manage a project involving 
multiple actors and reflect on own learning, 
academic development and specialization
3. Become acquainted with, and proficient in 
information-seeking, regarding best practicôÙ
es within refugee camp management and the 
humanitarian innovation sector.
7. Systematically facilitate, document and 
evaluate field work and workshops in releôÙ
vant contexts, to obtain specialised knowlôÙ
edge
The project was planned with inspiration 
from the DRM process model. This iterative 
process, which is not very rigid in its defiôÙ
nition of the phases, fit very well with our 
project process. Some things could however 
have been done differently. The descriptive 
study phase ended up taking up by far the 
largest part of the project. The amount of 
research needed to get a properly into the 
humanitarian context was surprisingly large. 
There was plenty of material available, opôÙ
posite of what usually is the case, but the 
quantity also made it difficult to set the limôÙ
its for when it was enough. The material we 
acquired about the humanitarian context 
was not academic, and it was a completeôÙ
ly different type of  search strategy which 
was needed. Weãve watched Youtube vidôÙ
eos and read blogs, newspapers, weãve used 
Facebookãs live-video feature to ask quesôÙ
tions to UNHCR and weãve read through an 
extreme amount of NGO reports. Not all of 
this knowledge has been directly useful for 
the project, and a lot of it is not even cited. 
It has been a challenge to incorporate these 
unconventional sources into an academic 
report. But we believe weãve succeeded in 
supporting our arguments for various design 
choices, and we recommend others to take 
advantage of the plethora of material that is 
available online nowadays. 
==PAGE:197==
reflection I chapter 16 I section 4
183
We had many Skype interviews, which 
worked surprisingly well. The advantage was 
that no travel time was required, and it was 
therefore much easier to schedule a quick 
interview. We highly recommend having 
many short interviews in the beginning of 
a project, with many different stakeholders. 
This way you donãt depend on one person 
or organisation who might become busy or 
feel overwhelmed by questions, and you get 
insights from many angles simultaneously, 
which means your impression becomes less 
fixed on few stakeholders needs. 
Skype interviews, where the interviewee and 
interviewer cannot necessarily see each othôÙ
er can however also be tricky, because they 
eliminate the possibility to read the body 
language. It is therefore important to be 
aware that this type of interview is different, 
and that depending on the purpose, it might 
affect the results. 
Lastly, weãve been balancing with whom our 
project was directed to. Trying to speak to 
both IT specialists, who have a very technical 
language, and humanitarian work specialists, 
who e.g. use lots of abbreviations, has been a 
challenge. When writing the report, most of 
the time we have tried to ensure that both 
groups can follow our thoughts. In order to 
keep some of the descriptions short, and in 
a professional language, some sections are 
more directed towards one group rather 
than the other. Finally however, our conôÙ
cept and models had the effect of impressôÙ
ing both groups at workshops, which was 
the time and place where we could be most 
influential.
possibilities. If the project were to carry on it 
would however be necessary to team up with 
someone who has better coding capabilities. 
Choosing these technologies from the beginôÙ
ning of the process also gave us another inôÙ
teresting dilemma. A common mantra in DeôÙ
sign and Innovation is to define the problem 
before finding the solution to it, a bottom up 
approach. By choosing the technologies first, 
we took a more top-down like approach. This 
gave us some important advantages when it 
came to engaging the stakeholders, who got 
interested due to the technology we were 
working with. It did however also set some 
boundaries on the solution space, which 
might also have been some of the reason for 
why the ideation phase was unusually chalôÙ
lenging to us. 
Communication with stakeholders
This reflection relates to learning objectives:
5. Effectively communicate and mediate beôÙ
tween different types of multidisciplinary
stakeholders.
6. Present technical and scientific knowledge 
in a suitable manner according to the recipiôÙ
ent and context, using visual, oral and writôÙ
ten language
9. Understand and enhance the contact 
between efficiency-oriented humanitarian 
organisations and innovation and solution 
oriented business and entrepreneurial comôÙ
munities
As previously mentioned, we managed to get 
a wide selection of stakeholders involved in 
the project. In the beginning of the project, 
we were worried about not having one dedôÙ
icated partner organisation. Moreover, we 
were concerned about not being able to go 
to a refugee camp in person. These doubts, 
however valid, were put to rest when we disôÙ
covered the value of having multiple partôÙ
ners, and that distances are much smaller 
if you remember the technical possibilities 
available.
